كتاب الطهارة
قيدنا أصل المسألة بالجنب لأن الطاهر إذا انغمس لطلب الدلو ولم يكن على أعضائه نجاسة لا يصير الماء مستعملاً اتفاقاً لعدم إزالة الحدث وإقامة القربة، وإن انغمس للاغتسال صار مستعملاً اتفاقاً لوجود إقامة القربة وحكم الحدث حكم الجنابة ذكره في البدائع، وكذا حكم الحائض والنفساء إذا نزلا بعد الانقطاع، أما قبل الانقطاع وليس على أعضائهما نجاسة فإنهما كالطاهر إذا انغمس للتبرد لأنها لا تخرج من الحيض بهذا الوقوع فلا مستعملاً. كذا في فتاوى قاضيخان والخلاصة. وقيدنا بكونه انغمس لطلب الدلو أو للتبرد لأنه لو انغمس بقصد الاغتسال للصلاة قالوا صار الماء مستعملاً اتفاقاً لوجود إزالة الحدث ونية القربة لكن ينبغي أن لا يزول حدثه عند أبي يوسف لما نقلوه عنه أن الصب شرط عنده في غير الماء الجاري وما هو في حكمه لإسقاط الفرض ولم أر من صرح بهذا، وقد علمت فيما قدمناه في الكلام على ماء الفساقي أن قولهم بأن ماء البئر يصير مستعملاً عند الكل مبني على قول ضعيف عن محمد، والصحيح من مذهب محمد أن ماء البئر لا يصير مستعملاً مطلقاً لأن المستعمل هو ما تساقط عن الأعضاء وهو مغلوب بالنسبة إلى الماء الذي لم يستعمله فاحفظ هذا وكن على ذكر منه ينفعك إن شاء الله تعالى ثم رأيت بعد هذا العلامة ابن أمير حاج في شرح منية المصلي صرح بما ذكرته وقال : الماء المستعمل هو الماء الذي لاقى الرجل الذي زال حدثه فيجب نزح جميع الماء على رواية نجاسة الماء المستعمل ولا يجب نزح شيء منها على رواية طهارته بل هو باق على طهوريته وقد عرفت أن رواية الطهارة هي المختارة. فعلى هذا قولهم صار الماء مستعملاً معناه صار الماء الملاقي للبدن مستعملاً لا أن ماء البئر صار مستعملاً وقيدنا بقولنا ليس على أعضائه نجاسة حقيقية لأنه لو كان كذلك اهـ.
غير طهور) قال الرملي أقول : سيأتي قريباً أنه طاهر طهور على الصحيح. قوله : (لكن ينبغي الخ) أقول : فيه نظر لأنه مخالف لإطلاقهم الاتفاق. وعبر في السراج بقوله «بلا خلاف» وكذا بقوله بالاتفاق إلا في قول زفر والذي يظهر لي أن أبا يوسف إنما يشترط الصب فيما إذا لم ينو الاغتسال ليجعل الصب قائماً مقام النية، ويدل عليه ما سيأتي من أنه لو تدلك صار مستعملاً بالاتفاق لقيامه مقام نية الاغتسال قوله : (وقد علمت فيما قدمناه في الكلام على ماء الفساقي الخ) أقول قد قدمنا الكلام على ذلك فلا حاجة إلى الإعادة بعد إحاطتك بما هنالك، وما نقله عن ابن أمير حاج لا يقوى على معارضته كلام الدبوسي المتقدم وعلى إطلاق عباراتهم باستعمال الماء اتفاقاً، وعلى هذا فلا حاجة إلى البناء على ما ذكر ولا إلى تأويل الكلام بخلاف المتبادر منه إلى الإفهام . ثم رأيت في شرح نظم الكنز للعلامة المقدسي قال ما نصه : وأما تأويل الكلام بأن المراد بصيرورته مستعملاً صيرورة ما لاقي أعضاءه منه مستعملاً فهذا بعيد جداً إذ لا يحتاج إلى التنصيص على ذلك أصلاً اهـ. قوله: (والظاهر منه إذا نزل للدلو وتدلك في الماء صار الماء مستعملاً) أي إذا لم يكن تدلكه لإزالة الوسخ كما في البحر الرائق ج 12 /

كتاب الطهارة
أبي لتنجس الماء اتفاقاً، وقيد المسألة في المحيط بقوله لم يتدلك فيه ولم يبين مفهومه، وكذا في الخلاصة. والظاهر منه أنه إذا أنزل للدلو وتدلك في الماء صار الماء مستعملاً اتفاقاً لأن الدلك فعل منه قائم مقام نية الاغتسال فصار كما لو نزل للاغتسال. وقيد المسألة بعضهم بأن لا يكون استنجى بالأحجار، فمفهومه أنه لو كان مستنجياً بالأحجار تنجس الماء اتفاقاً لكن هذا يبتنى على أن الحجر في الاستنجاء مخفف لا مطهر وفيه خلاف ذكره في التنجيس وذكر أن المختار أنه مخفف لا مطهر وسنذكره إن شاء الله تعالى في موضعه فإن قلت: لم قال أبو يوسف بأن الصب شرط في العضو لا في الثوب؟ وما الفرق بينهما؟ قلت : روي عن أبي يوسف روايتان : في رواية أن الصب شرط فيهما ووجهه أن القياس يأبي التطهير بالغسل لأن الماء يتنجس بأول الملاقاة، وإنما حكمنا بالطهارة ضرورة أن الشرع كلفنا بالتطهير والتكليف يعتمد القدرة وسمي الماء طهوراً وذلك يقتضي حصول الطهارة به والضرورة تندفع بطريق الصب فلا ضرورة إلى طريق آخر مع أن الماء حال الصب بمنزلة ماء جار، وفي غير حالة الصب راكد والراكد أضعف من الجاري. وفي رواية أن الصب شرط في العضو لا في الثوب وهو المشهور عنه، ووجهه أن غسل الثياب بطريق الصب لا يتحقق إلا بكلفة ومشقة لأنها تغسلها النساء عادة وكل امرأة لا تجد خادماً يصب الماء عليها ولا ماء جارياً، وأما غسل البدن يتحقق بطريق الصب من غير كلفة . كذا في النهاية .
الماء مستعملاً عنده .
وقال القاضي الاسبيجابي في شرح مختصر الطحاوي : جنب اغتسل في بئر ثم إلى العشرة قال أبو يوسف : تنجس الآبار كلها. وقال محمد : يخرج من الثالثة طاهراً ثم ينظر إن كان على بدنه عين نجاسة تنجست المياه كلها وإن لم يكن عين نجاسة صارت المياه كلها مستعملة، ثم بعد الثالثة أن وجدت منه النية يصير مستعملاً، وإن لم توجد منه النية لا يصير ولو أنه غسل الثوب النجس في إجانة وعصره ثم في إجانة إلى العشرة فإن الثوب يخرج من الثالثة طاهراً والمياه الثلاثة نجسة في قولهم جميعاً، وأبو يوسف فرق بين الثوب والبدن فقال : لأن في الثوب ضرورة ولا ضرورة في البدن ا هـ . ولا يخفى أن مقتضى مذهب أبي يوسف من اشتراط الصب أن لا تتنجس المياه كلها عنده لما أن الحدث لم يزل ونية الاغتسال وإن وجدت لكن لا اعتبار بها إذا لم يصح الغسل عنده ، وقد علمت فيما قدمناه عند الكلام على ماء الفساقي أن ما ذكره الاسبيجابي وغيره من كون ماء الآبار يصير مستعملاً عند محمد مبني على القول الضعيف لا على الصحيح فارجع إليه تجد لك فرجاً كبيراً إن شاء الله تعالى. وقد ظهر لي أن قولهم بنجاسة ماء الآبار عند أبي يوسف وقولهم بنجاسة ماء البئر إذا نزل للاغتسال عنده مفرع على رواية عن أبي يوسف أن من نزل في البئر وهو جنب كان الماء نجساً والرجل نجس، وقد ذكر هذه الرواية الاسبيجابي وذكر هذه الفروع بعدها فالظاهر أنها مفرعة عليها لا على القول المشهور عنه أن الرجل بحاله والماء بحاله والله الهادي للصواب.
Book of Purification: We restricted the original issue to the junub because if a pure person immerses to fetch a bucket and has no impurity on his limbs, the water does not become musta'mal by consensus due to lack of removing hadath and performing devotion. If he immerses for ghusl, it becomes musta'mal by consensus due to performing devotion, and the ruling of hadath is like that of janabah, as mentioned in al-Bada'i. Similarly, the ruling for a menstruating woman and a woman in post-childbirth bleeding when they descend after cessation. Before cessation, if there is no impurity on their limbs, they are like a pure person immersing for cooling, because they do not exit menstruation with this immersion, so it does not become musta'mal. Thus in Fatawa Qadikhan and al-Khulasa. We restricted it to his immersing for fetching a bucket or cooling, because if he immerses with the intention of ghusl for prayer, they said the water becomes musta'mal by consensus due to removing hadath and intention of devotion. But it should be that his hadath is not removed according to Abu Yusuf, as they reported from him that pouring is a condition for him in other than flowing water and what is in its ruling for dropping the obligation, and I have not seen anyone state this explicitly. And you have known from our earlier discussion on the water of fasaqi that their statement that well water becomes musta'mal by all is based on a weak narration from Muhammad. The correct from Muhammad's madhhab is that well water never becomes musta'mal, because musta'mal is what falls from the limbs, and it is overcome relative to the water that has not been used. So preserve this and keep it in mind; it will benefit you, Allah willing. Then after this I saw the scholar Ibn Amir Hajj in Sharh Munyat al-Musalli stating what I mentioned and saying: Used water is water that contacted a person whose hadath has been removed. So it is obligatory to remove all water according to the narration of impurity of used water, and no removal is required according to the narration of its purity; rather it remains on its purifying property. And you have recognized that the narration of purity is the chosen one. Accordingly, their statement 'the water became musta'mal' means the water contacting the body became musta'mal, not that the well water became musta'mal. And we restricted it by our saying 'there is no actual impurity on his limbs' because if it were otherwise (i.e., there was impurity), then [the water becomes impure by consensus]. End quote. (Not pure) Al-Ramli said: I say: It will soon come that it is pure and purifying according to the correct view. His statement: (But it should be...) I say: In this there is consideration because it contradicts their absolute statement of consensus. And he expressed in al-Siraj with 'without dispute' and likewise with 'by consensus' except for the view of Zufar. And what appears to me is that Abu Yusuf only requires pouring when he did not intend ghusl, to make pouring stand in place of intention. And it is indicated by what will come: that if he rubbed, the water becomes musta'mal by consensus because rubbing stands in place of the intention of ghusl. His statement: (And you have known from our earlier discussion on the water of fasaqi...) I say: We have already discussed that, so there is no need for repetition after your mastery of what is there. And what he narrated from Ibn Amir Hajj does not have strength to oppose the statement of al-Dabusi mentioned earlier and the absolute expressions of their using water by consensus. And accordingly, there is no need to build upon what was mentioned nor to interpret the speech contrary to what is apparent to understanding. Then I saw in Sharh Nazm al-Kanz by al-Maqdisi who said verbatim: As for interpreting the speech that by 'its becoming musta'mal' is meant the becoming musta'mal of what contacted his limbs from it, this is very far-fetched, as there is no need to specify that at all. End quote. His statement: (And the apparent is that if he descends for a bucket and rubs in the water, the water becomes musta'mal) i.e., if his rubbing is not for removing dirt, as in al-Bahr al-Raiq, vol. 12. (Page 178) Book of Purification: ...for the water to become impure by consensus. And the issue was restricted in al-Muhit by his statement 'he did not rub in it' and he did not explain its implication, and similarly in al-Khulasa. And the apparent from it is that if he descends for a bucket and rubs in the water, the water becomes musta'mal by consensus because rubbing is an action of his that stands in place of the intention of ghusl, so it becomes as if he descended for ghusl. And some restricted the issue by his not having performed istinja' with stones, so its implication is that if he had performed istinja' with stones, the water becomes impure by consensus. But this is based on the view that the stone in istinja' is lightening (mukhaffif) not purifying (mutahhir), and there is dispute about this mentioned in al-Tanjis, and it was mentioned that the chosen view is that it is lightening not purifying, and we will mention it insha'Allah in its place. If you ask: Why did Abu Yusuf say that pouring is a condition for the limb but not for the garment? And what is the difference between them? I say: Two narrations are related from Abu Yusuf: In one narration, pouring is a condition in both, and its justification is that analogy (qiyas) disallows purification by washing because the water becomes impure at the first contact. Rather, we ruled for purity due to necessity because the Law obligated us to purify, and obligation depends on ability, and water is called tahur, which implies attaining purity through it, and necessity is satisfied by the method of pouring, so there is no need for another method, especially since water during pouring is like flowing water, while without pouring it is stagnant, and stagnant is weaker than flowing. In another narration, pouring is a condition for the limb but not for the garment, and this is the well-known from him. Its justification is that washing clothes by pouring is only accomplished with hardship and difficulty because women usually wash them, and every woman does not find a servant to pour water on her nor flowing water. As for washing the body, it is accomplished by pouring without hardship. Thus in al-Nihaya. Water becomes musta'mal according to him. And al-Qadi al-Asbijabi said in Sharh Mukhtasar al-Tahawi: A junub performed ghusl in a well, then up to ten [wells]. Abu Yusuf said: All the wells become impure. Muhammad said: He emerges pure from the third, then if there is actual impurity on his body, all the waters become impure; if there is no actual impurity, all the waters become musta'mal. Then after the third, if intention is found from him, it becomes musta'mal; if intention is not found, it does not become musta'mal. And if he washed a najis garment in a basin and wrung it, then in a basin up to ten, the garment emerges pure from the third, and the three waters are impure according to all of them. And Abu Yusuf differentiated between the garment and the body, saying: Because in the garment there is necessity, and there is no necessity in the body. End quote. And it is not hidden that the implication of Abu Yusuf's madhhab of requiring pouring is that all the waters do not become impure according to him, because hadath has not been removed and even if intention of ghusl existed, it is not considered if ghusl is not valid according to him. And you have known from our earlier discussion on the water of fasaqi that what al-Asbijabi and others mentioned that well water becomes musta'mal according to Muhammad is based on the weak narration, not the correct one. So refer to it, you will find a great relief for you, insha'Allah. And it has appeared to me that their statement of impurity of well water according to Abu Yusuf and their statement of impurity of well water if he descended for ghusl according to him are branches of a narration from Abu Yusuf that whoever descends into a well while junub, the water is impure and the man is impure. Al-Asbijabi mentioned this narration and mentioned these branches after it, so the apparent is that they are branches of it, not of his well-known statement that the man is in his state and the water is in its state. And Allah is the Guide to the correct.
Source pages p. 177

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq is an expansion probe on the ruling of water becoming musta'mal (used) when a person enters a well or body of water. The author, Ibn Nujaym, is commenting on the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq. He begins by restricting the discussion to the case of a junub (one in major ritual impurity) because for a pure person (tahir) who immerses to fetch a bucket without any impurity on his limbs, the water does not become musta'mal by consensus (ittifaqan) since there is no removal of hadath or performance of devotion (qurbah). However, if the pure person immerses with the intention of ghusl (full ablution), the water becomes musta'mal by consensus because the intention and act of devotion are present. The same ruling applies to a menstruating woman or a woman in postpartum bleeding if they descend after the bleeding has stopped; before cessation, they are like a pure person immersing for cooling, as they do not exit their state of ritual impurity through mere immersion. This is cited from Fatawa Qadikhan and al-Khulasa.

The author then addresses a nuance: if a person immerses with the intention of ghusl for prayer, the water becomes musta'mal by consensus due to the removal of hadath and intention of devotion. However, he notes that according to Abu Yusuf, the hadath should not be removed because Abu Yusuf stipulates that pouring (sabb) is a condition for removing hadath in still water (non-flowing). The author observes that he has not seen anyone explicitly state this implication. He then refers to an earlier discussion on the water of fasaqi (public water dispensaries) to clarify that the statement that well water becomes musta'mal by everyone is based on a weak narration from Muhammad (al-Shaybani). The sound (sahih) position of Muhammad is that well water never becomes musta'mal because musta'mal is defined as water that has fallen from the limbs after washing, and the water in the well is in such a large quantity that the small amount that contacts the limbs is overcome (maghlub) by the rest. Thus, the well water remains tahur (purifying). The author urges the reader to remember this.

He then quotes Ibn Amir Hajj from his commentary on Munyat al-Musalli, who explicitly states that used water is water that has contacted a person whose hadath has been removed. According to the narration that used water is impure (najis), all the water must be removed; according to the narration that it is pure (tahir), no removal is required and it remains purifying. The chosen (mukhtar) view is the narration of purity. Therefore, when scholars say 'the water became musta'mal,' they mean only the water that directly contacted the body, not the entire well water. The condition that there be no actual impurity (najasah haqiqiyyah) on the limbs is added because if there is, the water becomes impure by consensus.

Al-Ramli objects to Ibn Nujaym's earlier comment that Abu Yusuf's condition of pouring might prevent the removal of hadath, arguing that it contradicts the explicit consensus statements. He notes that in al-Siraj and other works, the ruling is stated as 'without dispute' except for Zufar. Al-Ramli explains that Abu Yusuf only requires pouring when the person did not intend ghusl, making the pouring stand in place of intention. This is supported by the fact that if a person rubs (tadallaka) his body while in the water, the water becomes musta'mal by consensus because rubbing acts like the intention of ghusl. Thus, the earlier concern about Abu Yusuf's condition is unnecessary.

The author then cites al-Maqdisi's commentary on Nazm al-Kanz, who strongly rejects the interpretation that 'becoming musta'mal' refers only to the water in contact with the limbs, saying such an interpretation is far-fetched and unnecessary.

Further, the author discusses the scenario of a person descending for a bucket and rubbing (tadalluka) in the water. According to al-Muhit and al-Khulasa, the ruling is not explicit about rubbing, but the apparent view is that it makes the water musta'mal by consensus because rubbing is equivalent to the intention of ghusl. Some scholars added a condition: that the person must not have performed istinja' (cleaning after relieving oneself) using stones, because if he did, the water would become impure by consensus. This is based on the view that stones are only lightening (mukhaffif) not purifying (mutahhir). The author notes that this will be discussed in its proper place.

A question arises: why did Abu Yusuf say pouring is a condition for the limb but not for the garment? The author explains that there are two narrations from Abu Yusuf: one that pouring is a condition for both, and the other (and more famous) that it is a condition only for the limb. The first narration is based on qiyas (analogy): water becomes impure upon first contact, but purification is a legal obligation that must be possible, so pouring overcomes the weakness of stagnant water. The second narration is based on practical difference: washing garments by pouring is burdensome for women, who typically do the laundry without assistance, while washing the body by pouring is not burdensome.

Finally, the author presents a scenario from al-Asbijabi's commentary: If a junub performs ghusl in a well and then continues to ten wells, Abu Yusuf says all wells become impure. Muhammad says the person emerges pure from the third well; if there is actual impurity on his body, all waters become impure; if no actual impurity, all waters become musta'mal. After the third well, if intention is present, the water becomes musta'mal; otherwise not. In contrast, when washing a najis garment in basins, all agree the garment becomes pure after three washings, but the three waters are impure. Abu Yusuf distinguishes between garment (necessity) and body (no necessity). The author concludes that this ruling of impurity at Abu Yusuf is based on a weak narration and not his well-known position. The correct position is that the man and the water each remain in their original state (i.e., the water remains pure). The guidance is from Allah.

Key Terms
صب (sabb): Pouring water over the limbs during washing. Abu Yusuf stipulates this as a condition for the removal of hadath in still water.
تدليك (tadlik): Rubbing the body while in water. This is considered a substitute for the intention of ghusl, causing the water to become musta'mal by consensus.
مخفف (mukhaffif): Lightening; the view that istinja' with stones only lightens the impurity but does not purify completely.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a person descends into a well to fetch a bucket (no intention of ghusl) but rubs his body, what is the ruling on the well water according to the text?
A) It does not become musta'mal.
B) It becomes musta'mal by consensus.
C) It becomes impure.
D) It remains as it was.

Key Takeaway
Rubbing substitutes for intention, and in the absence of pouring, Abu Yusuf's view differs; but the sound position is that well water remains tahur, and these disputes are based on weak narrations.

Water in a Well After Ghusl: Pouring (Sabb) as a Condition for Purification
Intra-Hanafi dispute on water used for ghusl in a well

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani regarding the status of water used for ghusl in a well, and identify the mufta bihi position according to the author of al-Bahr al-Raiq.

Core Idea
Pouring (sabb) conditions purification; its absence determines whether water becomes impure or merely used.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq examines a classic Hanafi dispute: what happens to water in a well when a person in janabah bathes in it? The central issue is whether pouring (sabb) is a necessary condition for valid purification. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad offer differing views, and the author weighs their proofs to arrive at the relied-upon position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal scenario and contested issue
Understand the case: a person in major impurity (janabah) descends into a well. At stake is the status of the water—does it become impure (najis), used (musta'mal), or remain pure?
2
Examine Abu Yusuf's two narrations on pouring (sabb)
Abu Yusuf has two transmitted opinions: one requiring pouring for both body and garment, the other (well-known) only for the body. Grasp the reasoning behind each: qiyas and necessity.
The well-known narration exempts garments due to hardship.
3
Examine Muhammad al-Shaybani's view
Muhammad holds that the person emerges pure on the third dip. Water becomes used (musta'mal), not najis, unless there is visible impurity. Intention also affects the used-water status.
Muhammad's view does not require pouring for the water to remain merely used.
4
Analyze the author's tarjih
The author declares Muhammad's view weak and clarifies that the view attributing impurity to Abu Yusuf is based on a weak narration. The correct position is that water remains pure and purifying.
The author sides with Abu Yusuf's well-known narration that the man and water remain as they were.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical consequence: according to the mufta bihi, a person can use well water after bathing without it being impure or used, provided no visible impurity and no rubbing (dalk).

كتاب الطهارة لتنجس الماء اتفاقاً، وقيد المسألة في المحيط بقوله لم يتدلك فيه ولم يبين مفهومه، وكذا في الخلاصة. والظاهر منه أنه إذا أنزل للدلو وتدلك في الماء صار الماء مستعملاً اتفاقاً لأن الدلك فعل منه المشهور عنه، ووجهه أن غسل الثياب بطريق الصب لا يتحقق إلا بكلفة ومشقة لأنها تغسلها النساء عادة وكل امرأة لا تجد خادماً يصب الماء عليها ولا ماء جارياً، وأما غسل البدن يتحقق بطريق الصب من غير كلفة . كذا The book of purification. The water becomes impure by agreement. And the issue in al-Muhit is restricted by his statement: 'he did not rub himself in it' and its implication is not explained. Similarly in al-Khulasa. The apparent meaning is that if he descended for the bucket and rubbed himself in the water, the water becomes used by agreement, because rubbing is an act from him that takes the place of the intention to perform ghusl, so it is as if he descended for ghusl. Some have restricted the issue by his not having performed istinja with stones, so its implication is that if he had performed istinja with stones, the water becomes impure by agreement. But this is based on the fact that the stone in istinja is a lightener (mukhaffif) not a purifier (mutahhir), and there is a difference of opinion mentioned in al-Tanjis, and it is mentioned that the chosen view is that it is a lightener not a purifier, and we will mention it, God willing, in its place. If you say: Why did Abu Yusuf say that pouring is a condition for the limb but not for the garment? And what is the difference between them? I say: Two narrations are related from Yusuf: In one narration, pouring is a condition in both, and its reason is that analogy (qiyas) refuses purification by washing because it becomes impure by the first contact, but we have ruled for purification by necessity because the law obligated us to purify, and obligation depends on ability, and water is called tahur, which necessitates that purification is achieved with it, and the necessity is removed by the way of pouring, so there is no need for another way, especially since water in the state of pouring is like running water, and in the non-pouring state it is stagnant, and stagnant is weaker than running. In another narration, pouring is a condition for the limb but not for the garment, and this is the well-known narration from him. Its reason is that washing garments by way of pouring is only achieved with effort and hardship because women usually wash them, and every woman does not have a servant to pour water on her nor running water. As for washing the body, it is achieved by pouring without effort. Thus in al-Nihaya. Water is used according to him. And al-Qadi al-Isbijabi said in his commentary on Mukhtasar al-Tahawi: A janab person bathed in a well, then up to ten (vessels?). Abu Yusuf said: All the wells become impure. And Muhammad said: He comes out on the third one pure. Then he looks: if there is a visible impurity on his body, all the waters become impure; if there is no visible impurity, all the waters become used. Then after the third, if intention is found from him, it becomes used; if intention is not found, it does not become used. And if he washes the najis garment in a basin and wrings it, then in another basin up to ten, then the garment comes out on the third one pure, and the three waters are najis in all their opinions. And Abu Yusuf distinguished between the garment and the body, saying: because in the garment there is necessity, and there is no necessity in the body. End. And it is not hidden that the requirement of the madhhab of Abu Yusuf of stipulating pouring is that all the waters do not become impure according to him, because the hadath has not been removed, and intention of ghusl, even if found, is not considered if the ghusl is not valid according to him. And you have known from what we presented earlier in the discussion on the water of the fasaqi that what al-Isbijabi and others mentioned about the waters of wells becoming used according to Muhammad is based on the weak opinion, not the correct one. So refer to it, you will find great relief, God willing. And it became clear to me that their statement about the impurity of well water according to Abu Yusuf and their statement about the impurity of well water when he descends for ghusl according to him is based on a narration from Abu Yusuf that whoever descends into a well while in janabah, the water is impure and the man is impure. Al-Isbijabi mentioned this narration and mentioned these branches after it. So it appears that they are based on it, not on the well-known statement from him that the man remains as he was and the water remains as it was. And God is the guide to correctness.
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani regarding the status of water in a well when a person in major impurity (janabah) bathes in it. The author begins by noting that water becomes impure by consensus if the person rubs himself (dalk) and intends ghusl. However, the focus shifts to the condition of pouring (sabb). Abu Yusuf has two narrations: one requires pouring for both body and garment purification, the other only for the body (the well-known view). The reasoning for the first is based on analogy (qiyas) that water initially becomes impure upon contact, but purification is allowed by necessity because the Law obliges purification and ability is required; pouring makes water like running water. The second narration exempts garments due to hardship. The author then cites al-Isbijabi presenting a practical scenario: a janab person bathes in a well with multiple vessels. Abu Yusuf declares all waters impure; Muhammad says the person emerges pure on the third dip, and subsequent water becomes used only if intention is present. The author notes that Muhammad's view is based on a weak opinion, and the correct position is that water remains pure (not impure or used) unless there is visible impurity. Finally, the author clarifies that Abu Yusuf's ruling of impurity in this context is based on a weak narration, not his well-known stance that the man and water remain unchanged. The operative ruling (mufta bihi) leans toward Abu Yusuf's view that pouring is required for body purification, and water does not become impure solely by contact without pouring.

Key Terms
الصب (al-sabb): The act of pouring water over the body or garment during purification; a condition for valid washing according to Abu Yusuf.
الماء المستعمل (al-ma' al-musta'mal): Water that has been used for a valid act of worship (e.g., ghusl) and is no longer tahur (purifying) for that act; it is considered pure but not purifying.
الرواية (al-riwayah): A transmitted version of an Imam's opinion; here, two different narrations from Abu Yusuf regarding the condition of pouring.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the well-known narration of Abu Yusuf mentioned in the passage, what is required for the purification of a limb but not for a garment?
A) Pouring (صب) of water
B) Rubbing (دلك) of the limb
C) Intention (نية) for worship
D) Using three separate washings

Key Takeaway
The relied-upon Hanafi position is that water in a well does not become impure or used solely by immersion in janabah; pouring (sabb) is a condition for purification, and without it the water retains its purifying quality.

Taharah — Purification
intermediate
10 min
The Ruling on Tanned Hides: Purification and the Exception for Human Skin
Ruling and intra-Hanafi dispute on purification of hides by tanning

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on purification of hides through tanning, identify the exceptions, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding human skin.

Core Idea
A tanned hide becomes pure and permissible to use, except pig skin (remains impure) and human skin (pure but unusable due to human honor).

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the general Hanafi rule that tanning purifies animal hides, making them legally permissible for use. However, two exceptions are highlighted: pig skin, which remains impure due to its inherent impurity, and human skin, which becomes pure but cannot be used out of respect. The commentary delves into an intra-Hanafi debate on whether human skin is truly purified or not, and the correct interpretation of the exception.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general ruling and its exceptions
The text states: 'Every hide that is tanned is purified, except the skin of the pig, the human, and the hair of a human, and the carcass and their bones.' Understand that this rule applies only to hides capable of tanning.
2
Distinguish between real and legal tanning
Real tanning uses astringent substances; legal tanning uses sun and air. Both types are effective except that after legal tanning, water may cause re-impurity according to some narrations.
3
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute on human skin
Some scholars argue that the human skin exception means it does not become pure. Others, including the preferred view, hold that it becomes pure but is impermissible to use due to karamah (human honor).
4
Understand the linguistic argument
The author Ibn Nujaym rejects interpreting 'tahara' as 'legal permissibility' because purity does not automatically imply permissibility, as seen with human hair and bones which are pure but not usable.
5
Apply the ruling to practical scenarios
For example, using tanned human skin for a prayer mat would be sinful even if pure; using pig skin invalidates prayer due to impurity. The practical consequence of the dispute: is the prayer valid if one carries a tanned human skin? According to the mufta bihi view, the prayer is valid but sinful.

قوله : (وكل إهاب دبغ فقد طهر لما كان يتعلق بدباغ الإهاب ثلاث مسائل طهارته وهي تتعلق بكتاب الصيد والصلاة فيه وهي تتعلق بكتاب الصلاة، والوضوء منه بأن يجعل قربة وهي تتعلق بالمياه ذكر في بحث المياه لإفادة جواز الوضوء منه بطريق الاستطراد فاندفع بهذا ما قيل إن هذا الموضع ليس لبيان هذه المسألة والإهاب الجلد غير المدبوغ والجمع أُهب بضمتين وبفتحتين اسم له، وأما الأديم فهو الجلد المدبوغ وجمعه أدم بفتحتين. كذا في المغرب. وكذا . يسمى صرماً وجراباً كذا في النهاية. وقوله كل إهاب» يتناول كل جلد يحتمل الدباغة لا ما لا يحتمله فلا حاجة إلى استثنائه وبه يندفع ما ذكره الهندي أنه كان ينبغي جلد الحية فلا يطهر جلد الحية والفأرة به كاللحم، وكذا لا يطهر بالذكاة لأن الذكاة إنما تقام مقام الدباغ فيما يحتمله كذا في التنجيس. وفيه : إذا أصلح أمعاء شاة ميتة فصلى وهي معه جازت صلاته لأنه يتخذ منها الأوتار وهو كالدباغ، وكذلك العقب والعصب كذا لو دبغ المثانة فجعل فيها لبن جاز ولا يفسد اللبن، وكذلك الكرش إن كان يقدر على اصلاحه. وقال أبو يوسف في الأملاء: إن الكرش لا يطهر لأنه كاللحم ا.هـ. وأما قميص الحية فهو طاهر كذا في السراج الوهاج . ثم الدباغ هو ما يمنع عود الفساد إلى الجلد عند حصول الماء فيه. والدباغ على ضربين حقيقي وحكمي؛ فالحقيقي أن يدبغ بشيء له قيمة كالشب والقرظ والعفص وقشور الرمان ولحي الشجر والملح وما أشبه ذلك، وضبط بعضهم الشب بالباء الموحدة. وذكر الأزهري أن غيره تصحيف وضبطه بعضهم بالثاء المثلثة وهو نبت طيب الرائحة مرّ الطعم يدبغ به ، ذكره الأزهري في الصحاح وبأيهما كان فالدباغ به جائز . وأما القرظ فهو بالظاء لا بالضاد ورق شجر السلم بفتح السين واللام ومنه أديم مقروظ أي مدبوغ بالقرظ . قالوا: والقرظ نبت بنواحي تهامة كذا ذكره النووي في شرح المهذب. وإنما نبهنا عليه لأنه يوجد مصحفاً في كثير من كتب الفقه يقرأ بالضاد. والحكمي أن يدبغ بالتشميس والترتيب والإلقاء في الريح لا بمجرد التجفيف والنوعان مستويان في سائر الأحكام إلا في حكم واحد وهو أنه لو أصابه الماء بعد الدباغ الحقيقي لا يعود نجساً باتفاق

وقيل جلد الخنزير والآدمي لا يقبلان الدباغ لأن لهما جلوداً مترادفة بعضها فوق بعض، وعلى هذا يكون الاستثناء منقطعاً كما لا يخفى وإنما استثنى الجلد ولم يستثن الإهاب مع كونه مناسباً للمستثنى منه وهو قوله (كل) إهاب دبغ لما أن الإهاب هو الجلد قبل أن يدبغ فكان مهيأ للدباغ. يقال تأهب لكذا إذا تهيأ له واستعد وجلد الخنزير والآدمي لا يتهيان للدبغ فلذا استثني بلفظ الجلد دون الإهاب وإنما قدم الخنزير على الآدمي في الذكر لأن الموضع موضع إهانة لكونه في بيان النجاسة وتأخير الآدمي في ذلك أكمل. فحاصله أن في المشايخ من قال إنما لا يطهر جلد الخنزير بالدباغ لأنه لا يندبغ لأن شعره ينبت من لحمه ولو تصوّر دبغه لطهر، وقال بعضهم : لا يطهر وإن اندبغ لأنه محرم العين كذا في معراج الدراية. وفي
صدر الشريعة. قال الفاضل قاضي زاده ثم إنه قال الزيلعي في شرح الكنز: واستثناء الآدمي مع الخنزير يدل على أنه لا يطهر وليس كذلك، بل إذا دبغ طهر ولكن لا يجوز به الانتفاع لسائر أجزائه . وقال بعض شراح الوقاية : الاستثناء من الطهارة نجاسة وهذا في جلد الخنزير مسلم فإنه لا يطهر بالدباغ، وأما جلد الآدمي ففي غاية السروجي ذكر أنه إذا دبغ طهر ولكن لا يجوز الانتفاع به كسائر أجزائه فكيف يصح . هذا الاستثناء؟ فإنه لا يطهر بالدباغ، وأما جلد الآدمي فقد ذكر أنه إذا دبغ طهر ولكن لا يجوز الانتفاع به كسائر أجزائه فكيف يصح . هذا الاستثناء؟ قلت : فيه خلل لأنه إذا أراد معنى قول المصنف هو معنى جاز استعماله شرعاً فليس كذلك، وإن أراد أن معنى قوله طهر يستلزم معنى جاز استعماله شرعاً فيتعلق الاستثناء بذلك المعنى المنفهم من الكلام المذكور التزاماً لا بصريح معنى الكلام المذكور فيصح الاستثناء بالنظر إلى الآدمي أيضاً لعدم جواز استعماله شرعاً كعدم جواز استعمال جلد الخنزير، وإن كانت علة عدم جواز الاستعمال مختلفة فيهما قلنا : يلزم حينئذ أن يبقى صريح معنى الكلام المذكور على كليته بلا استثناء شيء منه وليس . يطهر جلد الخنزير بالدباغ فلا صحة للكلية المذكورة. لا يقال يجوز أن يكون مراد صدر الشريعة بقوله «فقد طهر» معنى فقد جاز استعماله شرعاً مجازاً بطريق ذكر الملزوم وإرادة اللازم ويجعل استثناء الآدمي قرينة عليه فلا يراد حقيقة قوله فقط طهر» لامتناع الجمع بين الحقيقة والمجاز فلا تكون الكلية إلا في جواز الاستعمال. وقد استثنى منه جلد الخنزير والآدمي فلا يلزم المحذور لأنا نقول: طهارة الشيء حقيقة لا تستلزم جواز استعماله شرعاً، ألا يرى أن جلد الآدمي إذا دبغ طهر ولكن لا يجوز الانتفاع به شرعاً احتراماً له نص عليه في المحيط والبدائع وغيرهما. وكذا شعر الإنسان وعظمه طاهران عندنا ولكن لا يجوز الانتفاع بشيء منهما لكرامة الإنسان على ما صرحوا به قاطبة، فلم تتحقق علاقة اللزوم بين طهر وبين جاز استعماله شرعاً حتى يصح حمل قوله «فقد طهر» على معنى فقد جاز استعماله رعاً مجازاً بعلاقة اللزوم. وأيضاً قوله وكل إهاب دبغ فقد طهر» ليس عبارته فقط بل هو كلام عامة الفقهاء، ولا شك أن مرادهم به ليس مجرد جواز استعماله شرعاً بل
شر
بصحيح
إذ لا
Every hide that is tanned is purified, except the skin of the pig, the human, and the hair of a human, and the carcass and their bones. Exception. His statement: 'And every hide that is tanned is purified' – because three issues relate to tanning of a hide: its purification, which relates to the book of hunting and prayer in it; and it relates to the book of prayer; and performing wudu from it by making it a waterskin, which relates to the book of waters – mentioned in the discussion of waters to indicate the permissibility of performing wudu from it by way of digression. Thus what was said that this place is not for explaining this issue is refuted. Al-ihab is the untanned skin; the plural is 'uhub' with two dammas or two fat'as. As for al-adim, it is the tanned skin; its plural is 'adim' with two fat'as. Thus in al-Mughrib. Similarly, it is called 'sarman' and 'jirab' – thus in al-Nihayah. His statement 'every hide' includes every skin that can accept tanning, not what cannot accept it, so there is no need to exclude it. And by this is refuted what al-Hindi mentioned: that the skin of a snake should have been mentioned, but the skin of a snake and a mouse are not purified by tanning just like meat; similarly, they are not purified by slaughter because slaughter takes the place of tanning in what can accept it – thus in al-Tanjis. And in it: If he fixed the intestines of a dead sheep and prayed while it was with him, his prayer is valid because bowstrings are made from it, and it is like tanning; similarly, the hamstring and sinew; likewise, if he tanned a bladder and put milk in it, it is permissible and the milk does not become spoiled; similarly, the stomach if he is able to fix it. Abu Yusuf said in al-Imla': that the stomach is not purified because it is like meat. End. As for the snake's shirt, it is pure – thus in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj. Then tanning is what prevents the return of corruption to the skin when water reaches it. Tanning is of two types: real and legal. The real is that one tans with something of value, like alum, qaraz, gallnut, pomegranate peel, tree bark, salt, and the like. Some scholars specify alum with a single baa. Al-Azhari mentioned that other than it is a mispronunciation, and some specify it with a triple thaa, which is a pleasant-smelling plant, bitter taste, used for tanning – al-Azhari mentioned it in al-Sihah. Whichever it is, tanning with it is permissible. As for qaraz, it is with 'zaa' not 'daad', and it is the leaves of the 'salam' tree (with fatha on seen and lam). From it is 'adim maqruuz' meaning tanned with qaraz. They said: qaraz is a plant in the Tihama region – thus mentioned by al-Nawawi in Sharh al-Muhadhdhab. We only alert to this because it is found misspelled in many books of fiqh, read with 'daad'. And the legal type is that one tans by sun-drying, arranging, and exposing to the wind, not by mere drying. The two types are equal in all rulings except in one ruling: that if water touches it after real tanning, it does not return to impurity by agreement of the narrations; after legal tanning, there are two narrations, and we will speak on the chosen one with its counterparts, God willing. His statement: 'Except the skin of the pig and the human' means every tanned hide is permissible to use legally except the skin of the pig due to the impurity of its essence, and the skin of the human due to his honor. With this formulation, what was said is refuted: that the exception is from purification, and this is accepted for the pig skin, for it is not purified by tanning; as for human skin, it has been mentioned in al-Ghayah that if tanned, it becomes pure but it is not permissible to benefit from it like its other parts – so how can this exception be valid? (Sharh al-Munyat al-Halabi). His statement: 'and we will speak on the chosen one with its counterparts' – al-Ramli said: what is to come is preponderance of non-return. His statement: 'With this formulation is refuted what was said' – i.e., what some commentators of al-Wiqayah said, and this formulation is by some marginal annotators. And it is said: The skin of the pig and the human do not accept tanning because they have layered skins one above another. On this basis, the exception is disconnected (munqati'), as is not hidden. He only excluded the skin and not the 'ihab' despite its suitability to the excepted-from, because 'ihab' is the skin before tanning, so it is ready for tanning. One says 'ta'ahhaba li kadha' if he is prepared for it. The skin of pig and human are not prepared for tanning, so he excluded using the word 'skin' instead of 'ihab'. He only mentioned pig before human because the context is one of disrespect due to discussing impurity, and delaying the human is more perfect. The summary is that among the mashayikh are those who say: The pig skin is not purified by tanning because it does not become tanned, as its hair grows from its flesh; if its tanning were conceivable, it would become pure. Some said: It does not become pure even if it becomes tanned because it is prohibited in essence – thus in Mi'raj al-Dirayah. And in Sadr al-Shari'ah: al-Fadil Qadi Zada said, then al-Zayla'i said in Sharh al-Kanz: The exception of the human with the pig indicates that it is not purified, but it is not so; rather, if tanned it becomes pure, but it is not permissible to benefit from it due to its other parts. Some commentators of al-Wiqayah said: The exception is from purification of impurity, and this is accepted for the pig skin, but human skin – in Ghayat al-Saruji it is mentioned that if tanned it becomes pure, but it is not permissible to benefit from it like its other parts. So how can this exception be valid? The marginal annotator Ya'qub Pasha intended to correct this exception and said: He meant legally permissible to use except pig skin due to its impurity and human skin due to its honor. Then he said: So it is not objected that the exception is from purification of impurity – this is accepted for pig skin, but human skin, as mentioned, if tanned becomes pure but cannot be used, so how can the exception be valid? I say: There is a flaw, because if he intended the meaning of the author's statement as meaning legally permissible to use, it is not so. If he intended that his statement 'has been purified' necessitates the meaning of legally permissible to use, then the exception attaches to that understood meaning necessarily, not to the explicit meaning of the mentioned speech, so the exception is valid with respect to the human as well due to the impermissibility of legally using it, like the impermissibility of using pig skin, even if the cause of impermissibility differs. We say: In that case, the explicit meaning of the mentioned speech would remain universal with no exception from it, and pig skin is not purified by tanning, so the claimed universality is not true. It cannot be said that it is possible that Sadr al-Shari'ah intended by his statement 'has been purified' the meaning of 'has become legally permissible to use' metaphorically by way of mentioning the concomitant and intending the necessary, and making the exception of the human a context for it, so the literal meaning of 'has been purified' is not intended due to the impossibility of combining literal and metaphorical, so the universality is only in permissibility of use. And he excluded from it pig and human skin, so the difficulty does not arise. Because we say: The purification of something does not rationally necessitate the permissibility of legally using it. Do you not see that human skin, if tanned, becomes pure, but it is not permissible to benefit from it legally out of respect? – as stated in al-Muhit, al-Bada'i', and others. Similarly, human hair and bones are pure according to us, but it is not permissible to benefit from any of them due to the honor of man, as they have all stated. So the relation of necessity between 'purified' and 'legally permissible to use' is not established such that his statement 'has been purified' can be metaphorically interpreted as 'has become legally permissible to use' via the relation of necessity. Also, his statement 'every hide that is tanned has been purified' is not just his wording, but rather it is the speech of all jurists, and there is no doubt that what they intend by it is not merely legal permissibility of use, but rather... (incomplete)
Source pages p. 179

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary (sharh) on the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq regarding the purification of animal hides through tanning. The author, Zayn al-Din ibn Nujaym, begins by stating the general rule: every hide that is tanned becomes purified (tahir), with explicit exceptions: pig skin, human skin, human hair, and the flesh and bones of a carcass. The commentary then explains that the term 'ihab' refers to untanned skin, while 'adim' is tanned skin. The rule applies only to hides that can be tanned; hence, the skins of snakes and mice are not included because they cannot be effectively tanned—they remain impure like meat. The passage then discusses the ruling on other animal parts: if one repairs the intestines of a dead sheep and prays with them, the prayer is valid because the intestines are used as bowstrings and this treatment is analogous to tanning. Similarly, the hamstring, sinew, tanned bladder, and even the stomach (if properly treated) are considered purified. However, Abu Yusuf in al-Imla' held that the stomach is like meat and does not become pure. The author then defines tanning (dibagh) as a process that prevents the hide from corrupting when water reaches it. Tanning is divided into two types: real (haqiqi) using astringent substances like alum, qaraz, gallnut, etc., and legal (hukmi) achieved by sun-drying, arranging, and exposure to wind (not mere drying). Both types are equivalent in all rulings except one: after real tanning, if water touches the hide, it does not revert to impurity by consensus; after legal tanning, there are two narrations (one that it reverts, one that it does not). The author promises to discuss the preferred view later. The most significant portion of the passage is the intra-Hanafi dispute concerning the exception of human skin. The matn explicitly excludes pig and human skin from the general purification. Some scholars argued that this exception implies human skin does not become pure through tanning. However, others countered that human skin does become pure when tanned but cannot be used due to the inherent honor (karamah) of humans—just as human hair and bones are pure but not permissible to use. The commentator then engages in a rigorous analysis of whether the word 'tahara' (purification) in the matn should be interpreted literally (ritual purity) or metaphorically (legal permissibility of use). He rejects the metaphorical interpretation because ritual purity does not automatically entail permissibility of use, as seen with human body parts. He concludes that the exception is valid in both senses: for pig skin, it never becomes pure; for human skin, it becomes pure but remains impermissible to use, so the exception from the general ruling of 'purification' is justified because the general ruling implies both purity and permissibility. However, the text ends abruptly mid-argument, leaving the final resolution incomplete.

Key Terms
إهاب (ihāb): Untanned animal skin or hide.
دباغ (dibāgh): The process of tanning hides to prevent corruption and make them pure for use.
أدم (adīm): Tanned leather.
طهر (ṭahara): To become ritually pure (tahir). Here it also implies the permissibility of use in some interpretations.
كرامة (karāmah): Honor and dignity of humans, which prohibits using human body parts even if they are pure.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi madhhab, what is the ruling on tanned human skin?
A) It becomes pure and permissible to use.
B) It becomes pure but remains impermissible to use.
C) It does not become pure at all.
D) It becomes pure only if tanned with specific substances.

Key Takeaway
Tanning purifies hides except pig (impure) and human (pure but impermissible); the Hanafi school holds that purity does not automatically grant permissibility of use.

Taharah — Purification
intermediate
10 min
Exceptions in Tanning: Pig and Human Skin (Intra-Hanafi Dispute)
Intra-Hanafi dispute on exceptions to purification by tanning

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on the purification of pig and human skins through tanning, distinguish the intra-Hanafi disagreements, and apply the principle of respect for humans to legal rulings.

Core Idea
Pig skin is never purified by tanning; human skin is not purified to uphold human dignity.

The hadith 'Any hide that is tanned becomes pure' establishes tanning as a means of purification. However, the Hanafi school excepts pig and human skin. The exact reasoning and ruling for human skin are debated among Hanafi scholars. This lesson unpacks the arguments and the relied-upon position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general principle
Start with the prophetic hadith: 'Ayyuma ihabin dubigha qad tahura' — any hide tanned becomes pure. This is the primary dalil.
The hadith is from Muslim and Abu Dawud. It is ahad but accepted.
2
Recognize the exception formula
The passage states that pig and human skin are excepted from the general rule. The exception is either disjoined (munqati') or based on linguistic nuance (mushakala).
The term 'jild' is used instead of 'ihab' because pig and human skin are not prepared for tanning.
3
Analyze the dispute over pig skin
Some say pig skin cannot be tanned due to its structure; others say even if tanned it remains impure because it is najis al-ayn. The relied-upon view is that it does not become pure.
Abu Yusuf reported a minority view that it is purified, but zahir al-riwayah rejects it.
4
Analyze the dispute over human skin
Two positions: (1) it becomes pure but cannot be used; (2) it does not become pure at all to prevent disrespect. The second is stronger due to the principle of human dignity.
The debate involves interpreting the exception: if the hadith means literal purification, then the exception is disjoined.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The view of non-purification for human skin is preferred by later Hanafi masters (e.g., al-Hidayah) because purification does not entail permissibility of use, as seen with human hair and bone.
This view also deters people from skinning and tanning human remains.
6
Extend to elephant and dog skin
Elephant skin is purified by tanning according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, contradicting Muhammad. Dog skin is debated: some say it is purified, but the school leans towards impurity.
The hadith on ivory supports the purity of elephant bone, and analogously its skin.

قال العلامة في فتح القدير : هذا الحديث يبطل قول محمد بنجاسة عين الفيل وسيأتي تمامه في عظم الميتات إن شاء الله تعالى. ويدخل أيضاً في عموم قوله كل إهاب جلد الكلب فيطهر بالدباغ بناء على أنه ليس بنجس العين. وقد اختلفت روايات المبسوط فيه فذكر في بيان سؤره أن الصحيح من المذهب عندنا عين الكلب نجس إليه يشير محمد في الكتاب بقوله : وليس الميت بأنجس من الكلب والخنزير. ثم قال : وبعض مشايخنا يقولون عينة ليس بنجس ويستدلون عليه بطهارة جلده أن
And it was said: The skin of the pig and the human do not accept tanning because they have layers of skin on top of each other. According to this, the exception is broken (munqati'), as is not hidden. And he only excepted the skin (jild) and did not except the hide (ihab), even though it is appropriate for the excepted-from, which is his saying: 'Every hide that is tanned', because the ihab is the skin before it is tanned, so it is prepared for tanning. It is said: 'tahaba' for something when you are prepared and ready for it. And the skin of the pig and the human are not prepared for tanning, so he excepted using the word 'jild' instead of 'ihab'. And he only mentioned the pig before the human in remembrance because the place is a place of dishonor due to mentioning impurity, and delaying the human in that is more perfect. So the summary is that among the shuyukh, some said: The skin of the pig is only not purified by tanning because it does not get tanned, because its hair grows from its flesh; and if its tanning were conceivable, it would become pure. And some said: It does not become pure even if it is tanned because it is an inherently impure (najis al-ayn) [thing], as in M'arij al-Dirayah. And in Sadr al-Shari'ah, al-Fadil Qadizada said, then al-Zayla'i said in Sharh al-Kanz: The exception of the human with the pig indicates that it does not become pure, but it is not like that; rather, if it is tanned, it becomes pure, but it is not permissible to benefit from it like the rest of its parts. And some commentators of al-Wiqayah said: The exception from purification is impurity, and this is accepted regarding the pig skin, for it is not purified by tanning. As for human skin, in Ghayat al-Saruji it is mentioned that if it is tanned, it becomes pure, but it is not permissible to benefit from it like the rest of its parts. So how is this exception valid? The commentator Ya'qub Pasha intended to correct this exception and said: He meant: it is permissible to use it legally except the skin of the pig due to its impurity, and the skin of the human due to his honor. Then he said: So what was said is not objected, that an exception from purification is impurity, and this is accepted regarding pig skin, for it is not purified by tanning. As for human skin, it has been mentioned that if it is tanned, it becomes pure, but it is not permissible to benefit from it like the rest of its parts. So how is this exception valid? I say: There is a flaw in this because if he meant the meaning of the author's statement 'means it is permissible to use it legally', it is not so. And if he meant that the meaning of his statement 'it has become pure' necessarily implies the meaning 'it is permissible to use it legally', then the exception attaches to that meaning understood from the mentioned speech implicitly, not to the explicit meaning of the mentioned speech. So the exception is valid with regard to the human as well, due to the lack of permissibility of using it legally, just like the lack of permissibility of using pig skin, even if the reason for the lack of permissibility of use differs between them. We say: It would then follow that the explicit meaning of the mentioned speech remains in its entirety without any exception from it, and that is not so. The skin of the pig is not purified by tanning, so the mentioned generality is not valid. It cannot be said that it is possible that Sadr al-Shari'ah intended by his statement 'fa-qad tahura' the meaning 'fa-qad jaza isti'maluhu shar'an' metaphorically by way of mentioning the implied and intending the necessary, and making the exception of the human a قرينة (indication) for it, so that the literal meaning of 'fa-qad tahura' is not intended, because combining between literal and metaphorical [meanings] is impossible. So the generality is only in the permissibility of use. And he excepted from it the skin of the pig and the human, so the objection does not arise, for we say: The literal purification of a thing does not necessitate the permissibility of its use legally. Do you not see that the skin of the human, if tanned, becomes pure, but it is not permissible to benefit from it legally out of respect for him, as stated in al-Muhit, al-Bada'i' and others. Likewise, human hair and bone are pure according to us, but it is not permissible to benefit from anything of them due to the honor of the human, as they have explicitly stated unanimously. So the relationship of necessity between 'tahura' and 'jaza isti'maluhu shar'an' is not established so as to justify carrying his statement 'fa-qad tahura' in the sense of 'fa-qad jaza isti'maluhu shar'an' metaphorically by a relation of necessity. Also, his statement 'wa kull ihabin dubigha fa-qad tahura' is not only his wording but it is the statement of the general body of jurists, and there is no doubt that their intention by it is not merely the permissibility of using it legally, but rather a statement of its literal purification. Otherwise, it would entail that the statement of its literal purification is completely disregarded, despite being an important matter upon which many issues depend, including: if something of it falls into a small amount of still water, it does not make it impure; and if something of it falls on the body of a person praying or on his garment, prayer is permissible with it, etc. Also, they have adduced as proof for it the hadith of the Prophet (peace be upon him) 'Ayyuma ihabin dubigha qad tahura', and no one has disputed that the intended meaning of purification in it is literal purification. End of what al-Fadil Qadizada mentioned. And some answered the first objection by saying that the relation is not limited to necessity, so let 'tahura' be metaphorical for 'jaza isti'maluhu shar'an' by another relation because between them a relation of causality and effectuation exists that is undeniable in general, even if there is no relation of necessity. I say: He means that causality is established in general, even if not consistent, because the purification of human skin, bone, and hair is not a cause for the permissibility of using it legally, nor is it an implication. The better view is what the author mentioned, that the exception is separated (munqati'), or it is said: He expressed the lack of permissibility of benefitting from human skin legally by its lack of purification, as a form of congruity (mushakala) because of mentioning it together with the pig, whose skin is not purified by tanning literally. So reflect. His statement: 'And he only mentioned the pig before...' i.e. in this place as in most books, because the place is a place of dishonor, or because it contains an indication. Al-Mabsut: It is narrated from Abu Yusuf that it is purified by tanning, but in the zahir al-riwayah, it is not purified, either because it cannot be tanned or because its very substance is impure. As for the human, some said: His skin cannot be tanned; even if it were to accept tanning, it would become pure because it is not inherently impure, but it is not permissible to benefit from it, and it is not permissible to tan it out of respect for him, and upon this is the consensus of the Muslims as quoted by Ibn Hazm. And some said: His skin is not purified by tanning at all out of respect for him. So the statement of lack of purification of his skin is a magnification of his honor, so that no one dares to skin him and tan him and use him. And included in the generality of his statement 'kull ihab' is the skin of the elephant, so it is purified by tanning, contrary to Muhammad who said that the elephant is inherently impure, while according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) it is like other predators. He said in al-Mabsut: From the chapter of hadath, and it is the most correct, for it has come in the hadith of Thawban that the Prophet (peace be upon him) bought for Fatima two bracelets of ivory, so the usage of ivory by the people appeared without objection, indicating its purity. And al-Bayhaqi narrated that he used to comb with an ivory comb. Al-Jawhari said: Ivory is the bone of the elephant. The commentator said in Fath al-Qadir: This hadith invalidates Muhammad's statement of the impurity of the elephant's substance, and its completion will come in the chapter on bones of dead animals, God willing. And also included in the generality of his statement 'kull ihab' is the skin of the dog, so it is purified by tanning based on the view that it is not inherently impure. And the narrations in al-Mabsut differ on it: He mentioned in the discussion of its leftover water that the correct position of the school according to us is that the dog's substance is impure, and Muhammad alludes to this in the book with his statement: 'The dead animal is not more impure than the dog and the pig.' Then he said: Some of our shuyukh say that its substance is not impure, and they use as evidence the purity of its skin that
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute on the purification of skins through tanning, focusing on the pig and human as exceptions. The author begins by citing a view that pig and human skin cannot be tanned because they have multiple layers, making the exception from the hadith 'Every hide that is tanned becomes pure' a disjoined exception (istithna' munqati'). The discussion then delves into the reason for using the word 'jild' instead of 'ihab' in the hadith: 'ihab' refers to skin prepared for tanning, while pig and human skin are not so prepared. There is disagreement among the shuyukh: some say pig skin is not tanned because its hair grows from the flesh, but if it could be tanned it would become pure; others say it is inherently impure (najis al-'ayn) and never becomes pure. For human skin, the debate is whether it becomes pure but cannot be used (out of respect) or it does not become pure at all. The author discusses the attempt by Ya'qub Pasha to reinterpret the hadith to mean 'permissible to use' rather than 'pure' to make the exception of human skin consistent, but this is refuted because literal purification does not entail permissibility of use, as exemplified by human hair and bone being pure but not usable. The better view is that the exception is either disjoined or stylistic congruity (mushakala). The passage then mentions the ruling on elephant and dog skins: elephant skin is purified by tanning per Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, contrary to Muhammad who deems the elephant inherently impure; the hadith about ivory supports the majority. For dog skin, the school is split: the zahir al-riwayah holds the dog impure, but some shuyukh say it is not inherently impure and thus its skin can be purified by tanning.

Key Terms
إهاب (ihab): The skin of an animal before it is tanned, prepared for tanning.
دبغ (dibagh): The process of tanning, which purifies certain skins except those that are inherently impure or exceptional.
نجس العين (najis al-ayn): A substance considered impure in its very essence, such that tanning does not purify it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position in this passage, what is the ruling on human skin after tanning?
A) It becomes pure and permissible to use for all purposes.
B) It becomes pure but cannot be used due to the honor of humans.
C) It does not become pure at all out of respect for humans.
D) It remains impure because human skin cannot be tanned.

Key Takeaway
Pig and human skins are exceptions to the general rule of purification by tanning; pig due to impurity, human due to respect, and the Hanafi mufta bihi is that they remain impure.

Tanning Hides: General Rule and Disputes on Human, Elephant, and Dog
Expansion of the ruling on tanning with internal Hanafi disputes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on tanning hides of humans, elephants, and dogs, and identify the intra-madhhab dispute regarding the dog and elephant.

Core Idea
Tanning purifies most hides, but human skin remains impure due to respect, and pig hide due to inherent impurity.

The general rule from the hadith 'Any hide that is tanned becomes pure' applies broadly, but exceptions exist. Human skin is not purified to prevent disrespect, and the pig is considered inherently impure. Internal Hanafi disputes arise over the dog and elephant: are they inherently impure or not? This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq unpacks these issues, citing proofs and later preferences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the General Rule
Locate the hadith 'أيما إهاب دبغ طهر' and note that it is the primary dalil for purification by tanning. Understand that the word 'طهر' is taken literally by all schools.
This hadith is narrated by Muslim and others, and its generality covers all hides unless there is evidence to exclude.
2
Analyze the Human Exception
Two views: (1) human skin can become pure if tanned but using it is forbidden due to respect; (2) it does not become pure at all to prevent abuse. The latter is relied upon.
The consensus (ijma) mentioned by Ibn Hazm prohibits tanning human skin out of honor.
3
Examine the Elephant Dispute
Imam Muhammad considered the elephant's essence impure, but Abu Yusuf and later scholars (including the author) treat it like other predators. Evidence from the Prophet's use of ivory supports the latter.
The author quotes Fath al-Qadir and the hadith of Thawban to show elephant hide becomes pure.
4
Evaluate the Dog Dispute
Two positions within the Hanafi school: some (including Muhammad in one narration) consider the dog's essence impure, so hide not purified; others (relied upon) consider it pure, citing the hadith and practical consequences like water and prayer.
The author leans toward purity, noting that the hadith is clear and that abandoning it would lead to many inconvenient rulings.
5
Determine the Mufta Bihi
The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that dog hide becomes pure by tanning, as it is not najis al-ayn. For human, it does not become pure. For elephant, it becomes pure. Pig hide remains impure even if tanned.
These positions are based on the hadith, consensus, and pragmatic reasoning.

The book of purification. Al-Mabsut: It is narrated from Abu Yusuf that it (the hide of an impure animal) becomes pure by tanning, but in the apparent narration it does not become pure, either because it cannot be tanned or because its essence is impure. As for the human, some said: his skin cannot be tanned, such that if it were tanned it would become pure because it is not inherently impure, but it is not permissible to benefit from it, nor is it permissible to tan it out of respect, and there is consensus of the Muslims on this as Ibn Hazm reported. And some said: his skin does not become pure by tanning at all out of respect. So the view that his skin is not pure is to honor him, so that no one dares to skin it, tan it, and use it. Included in the generality of his statement 'every hide' is the elephant's hide; it becomes pure by tanning, contrary to Muhammad in his statement that the elephant is inherently impure, but according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) it is like other predators. He said in al-Mabsut from the chapter of hadath: and it is the most correct, for there came in the hadith of Thawban that the Prophet ﷺ bought for Fatima two bracelets of ivory, so the people's use of ivory became apparent without objection, indicating its purity. And al-Bayhaqi narrated that he used to comb with an ivory comb. Al-Jawhari said: ivory is the bone of the elephant. The scholar in Fath al-Qadir said: this hadith invalidates Muhammad's statement of the inherent impurity of the elephant, and its completion will come in the chapter on bones of dead animals, God willing. Also included in the generality of his statement 'every hide' is the dog's hide; it becomes pure by tanning, based on it not being inherently impure. And the narrations of al-Mabsut have differed on it: in Bayan al-Su'ru, it is mentioned that the correct view in our madhhab is that the dog's essence is impure, to which Muhammad points in the book with his statement: 'A dead animal is not more impure than a dog or a pig.' Then he said: 'And some of our scholars say that its essence is not impure, and they derive evidence from the purity of its hide that the explanation of its purity is literal, otherwise it would necessarily mean that the explanation of its purity is completely abandoned despite being an important matter from which many issues derive, including: if something from it falls into still water, it does not make it impure; and if something from it falls on the body of a praying person or his garment, prayer with it is permissible, etc. Also, they derived evidence from his statement ﷺ: 'Any hide that is tanned becomes pure' and no one disputed that by 'purity' here is meant literal purity, as mentioned by al-Fadil Qadi Zadah. And some responded to the first (objection) by saying that the relationship is not limited to necessity; so let 'becomes pure' be metaphorical for 'its use becomes permissible legally' due to another relationship, because between them there is a cause-and-effect relationship that is not entirely denied, even if there is no relationship of necessity. I say: meaning that causation exists in general even if not consistent, because the purity of human skin, bone, and hair is not a cause for the permissibility of its use legally, nor is it necessary for it. And better is what the author mentioned: that the exception is discontinuous, or it is said: he expressed the impermissibility of benefiting legally from human skin by its lack of purity, by way of similarity (mushakalah) because it was mentioned alongside the pig, whose skin does not become pure by tanning literally, so consider. His statement: 'He only mentioned the pig first' i.e., in this place as in most books, because the context is one of disrespect, or because it contains an indication of the complete lack of capacity for purity in the pig, and delay in such places indicates honor, as in His statement: 'And had it not been for God repelling the people, some of them by means of others, there would have been demolished monasteries, churches, synagogues, and mosques' [al-Hajj 40]. And the author mentioned the human first due to his honor. And it was mentioned from Abu Yusuf that if a pig is slaughtered, its hide becomes pure by tanning. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 181

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq is an expansion (sharh) on the ruling of tanning hides. The author first discusses the ruling of a hide that is inherently impure (najis al-ayn): there is a narration from Abu Yusuf that tanning purifies it, but in the apparent narration (zahir al-riwayah) it does not, either because it cannot be tanned or because its essence is impure. For human skin, the majority hold that it cannot be tanned, and even if tanned it becomes pure (since it is not inherently impure), but using it is prohibited out of respect, and there is consensus on not tanning it. Some scholars say it does not become pure at all to prevent abuse. Regarding the elephant, Imam Muhammad considers it inherently impure, but the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) consider it like other predators, and the relied-upon view is that its hide becomes pure by tanning. The hadith about the Prophet buying ivory bracelets for Fatima, and the widespread use of ivory without disapproval, supports this. The dog's hide is also included in the general rule of purification by tanning, but there is a strong dispute within the Hanafi school: some narrations hold the dog's essence is impure, others not. The author leans toward the view that it is not inherently impure, citing the hadith 'Any hide that is tanned becomes pure' and deriving practical consequences (e.g., falling water, prayer garments). The passage concludes with a discussion of why the pig is mentioned before the human in some texts: to show the pig's complete inability to be purified, while the human is given precedence due to his honor. A minority report from Abu Yusuf says the pig's hide can be purified by tanning if it is slaughtered.

Key Terms
إهاب (ihāb): The skin or hide of an animal, whether tanned or not.
دباغ (dibāgh): The process of tanning, which chemically alters the hide to make it pure and usable.
نجس العين (najis al-'ayn): Inherent impurity that cannot be removed by any process, including tanning.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Human skin is inherently impure.
C) Respect for the human being (hurma).
D) It would be harmful.

Key Takeaway
Tanning purifies most hides, but human skin and pig hide are exceptions; the dog hide is purified according to the relied-upon view.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute on the Dog's Essential Impurity and Tanning
Analyzing the Hanafi internal dispute on dog's inherent impurity and tanning

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the two Hanafi positions on the dog's essential impurity (najis al-'ayn) and state the practical consequences for tanning, water purity, and prayer validity.

Core Idea
The dog's essence (bone, hair) is pure according to Abu Hanifa, but its flesh and saliva are impure; tanning purifies the hide only if the essence is pure.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq delves into a classic internal disagreement within the Hanafi school: is the dog impure in its very essence (najis al-'ayn) or not? The answer determines rulings on tanning its hide, water contamination, and prayer while carrying a dog. Understanding this dispute reveals the nuanced methodology Hanafi scholars use to weigh transmitted texts (nusus) and practical benefits (istihsan).

Guided Path
1
State the core issue
Identify the exact point of disagreement: whether the dog's essential nature (bone, hair, sinew) is inherently impure (najis) or pure (tahir).
2
Identify the two positions
Contrast Abu Hanifa's view (essence pure) with the view of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani in one transmission (essence impure). Note also the external opposition from al-Shafi'i.
3
Examine the evidence for each view
Abu Hanifa argues from permissible benefit (guard, hunt) and the general import of matns. The Sahibayn rely on the impurity of leftover water (su'r) and analogy with the pig.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response to objections
How do proponents of purity respond to the impurity view's evidence? They distinguish between essential purity (bone/hair) and impurity of flesh/saliva, and reject the analogy with the pig.
5
List the practical differences (thamarah al-khilaf)
Concrete scenarios: tanning the hide, purification of well water after a dog falls in, validity of prayer while carrying a puppy.
