Selling Tanned Hides and the Effect of Tadhkiyah on Non-Edible Animals
Stating Hanafi rulings on sale of tanned/untanned hides and intra-Hanafi dispute on tadhkiyah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling tanned and untanned hides, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on whether tadhkiyah purifies the meat of non-edible animals.

Core Idea
Selling tanned hide is permissible; selling untanned hide is not. Slaughter purifies the hide but not the meat of non-edible animals (preferred view).

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses two interrelated rulings: the sale of animal hides before and after tanning, and the purifying effect of Islamic slaughter (tadhkiyah) on the hide and meat of non-edible animals. While there is consensus that tanned hide may be sold and used, the sale of untanned dead animal hide is prohibited. More nuanced is the intra-Hanafi dispute on whether slaughter purifies the meat of animals that are not eaten—a dispute rooted in different analogies to tanning and the nature of impurity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling on selling hides
First, extract the Hanafi position on selling tanned vs. untanned hides. The passage clearly states that selling tanned hide is permissible, while selling untanned hide of a dead animal is not.
The author corrects a mistaken attribution to Abu Hanifa by al-Nawawi.
2
Understand the general rule of tadhkiyah
The passage explains that if an item is purified by tanning, it is also purified by tadhkiyah—both hide and meat are purified, whether the animal is edible or not. This is the starting point.
This is qualified later for non-edible animals.
3
Pinpoint the intra-Hanafi dispute
The core dispute is whether tadhkiyah purifies the meat of non-edible animals. Identify the two camps: those who hold it purifies (e.g., al-Bada'i', al-Hidayah) and those who hold it does not (e.g., al-Asrar, al-Kifayah, al-Tabyin).
The author's own works differ on this point.
4
Identify the relied-upon view
Determine which view is mufta bihi. The passage indicates that the majority of later mashayikh (including al-Ramli) prefer the view that slaughter purifies the hide but not the meat, akin to tanning.
Al-Ramli's marginal note confirms this choice.
5
Note the conditions for tadhkiyah to purify
The passage mentions that tadhkiyah only purifies if performed by a Muslim or Ahl al-Kitab in the proper location (between the chest and jaws). Majusi slaughter does not purify.
This is relevant for the overall ruling on hides and meat.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم عندنا يجوز بيع الجلد المدبوغ وينتفع به وهو قول الشافعي في الجديد وجمهور العلماء، وأما بيعه قبل الدباغ فقد نقل النووي في شرح المهذب أن أبا حنيفة يقول بجواز بيعه ورهنه كالثوب النجس وهو سهو منه فإن مذهب أبي حنيفة عدم جواز بيع جلود الميتة قبل الدباغ ذكره في المحيط وشرح الطحاوي وكثير من الكتب وفي بعض الكتب ذكر خلافاً. قال بعضهم إنه ملحق بالميتة وبعضهم ألحقه بالخمر، فالظاهر منه الاتفاق على عدم الجواز. واعلم أن ما طهر جلده بالدباغ طهر بالذكاة لحمه وجلده، سواء كان مأكولاً أو لا. أما طهارة جلده فهو ظاهر المذهب كما في البدائع وفي النهاية أنه اختيار بعض المشايخ . وعند بعضهم إنما يطهر جلده بالذكاة إذا لم يكن سؤره نجساً ا.هـ. وأما طهارة لحمه إذا كان غير مأكول فقد اختلف فيه فصحح في البدائع والهداية والتنجيس طهارته، وصحح في الأسرار والكفاية والتبيين نجاسته وفي المعراج أنه قول المحققين من أصحابنا، وفي الخلاصة هو المختار واختاره قاضيخان وفي التبيين أنه قول أكثر المشايخ. وأما المصنف فقد اختلف كلامه فصحح في الكافي نجاسته واختار في الكنز في الدبائح طهارته وسنتكلم عليها بدلائلها وبيان ما هو الحق ثمة إن شاء الله تعالى. لكن في كثير من الكتب أن الذكاة إنما توجب الطهارة في الجلد واللحم إذا كانت من الأهل في المحل وهو ما بين اللبة واللحيين سمي بحيث لو كان مأكولاً يحل أكله بتلك الذكاة فذبيحة المجوسي لا توجب الطهارة لأنها إماتة وقد قدمنا عن معراج الدراية معزياً إلى المجتبى أن ذبيحة المجوسي وتارك التسمية. وقد قوله: (واختاره قاضيخان قال الرملي أقول : الذي اختاره قاضيخان في فتاواه من باب البيع الفاسد طهارته فراجعه تجد ما في نقله عنه، اللهم إلا أن يكون قد اختاره في كتاب آخر من كتبه فيكون كلامه قد اختلف كما وقع للمصنف في الكنز وفي الكافي تبيين قوله : (وفي التبيين إنه قول أكثر المشايخ قال الرملي :أقول : عبارة التبيين على ما في النسخ التي أطلعنا عليها. وقال كثير من المشايخ : يطهر جلده بها ولا يطهر لحمه كما لا يطهر بالدباغ وهو الصحيح، وأنت تعلم ما بينهما
Then, according to us, it is permissible to sell tanned hide and benefit from it, and this is the opinion of al-Shafi'i in the new view and the majority of scholars. As for selling it before tanning, al-Nawawi transmitted in Sharh al-Muhadhdhab that Abu Hanifa says it is permissible to sell and pawn it like a najis garment, but this is a mistake on his part, for the madhhab of Abu Hanifa is the impermissibility of selling the hides of dead animals before tanning, as mentioned in al-Muhit, Sharh al-Tahawi, and many books; in some books, there is a difference of opinion. Some said it is attached to the dead animal, and some attached it to wine, so apparently there is agreement on non-permissibility. Know that what is purified by tanning is also purified by slaughter (tadhkiyah) in its meat and hide, whether it is edible or not. As for the purification of its hide, it is the apparent madhhab as in al-Bada'i', and in al-Nihayah it is the choice of some mashayikh. According to some, its hide is purified by slaughter only if its remnant (su'r) is not najis. As for the purification of its meat if it is non-edible, there is disagreement. In al-Bada'i', al-Hidayah, and al-Tanjis, it is declared pure; in al-Asrar, al-Kifayah, and al-Tabyin, it is declared impure; in al-Mi'raj, it is the view of the muhaqqiqun among our companions; in al-Khulasah, it is the chosen view, and Qadi Khan chose it; in al-Tabyin, it is the view of the majority of mashayikh. As for the author (al-Zayla'i), his statements differed: in al-Kafi he declared impurity, and in al-Kanz in the chapter on dhaba'ih he chose purity, and we will discuss its proofs and clarify what is correct there, God willing. However, in many books, tadhkiyah only necessitates purification in the hide and meat if it is from an ahli animal in the correct place (between the chest and the jaws), such that if it were edible, it would be halal by that slaughter. So the slaughter of a Majusi does not necessitate purification because it is merely killing. We have previously mentioned from Mi'raj al-Dirayah, attributed to al-Mujtaba, about the slaughter of a Majusi and one who omits the tasmiyah. And he said: (And Qadi Khan chose it). Al-Ramli said: I say: what Qadi Khan chose in his fatawa from the chapter of fasid sale is its purification; check it and you will find what is in the transmission from him, unless he chose it in another of his books, so his statement may have differed as happened with the author in al-Kanz and al-Kafi. Tabyin: he said: (And in al-Tabyin, it is the view of the majority of mashayikh). Al-Ramli said: I say: the phrase of al-Tabyin according to the copies we have seen: 'And many of the mashayikh said: it purifies its hide by it but does not purify its meat just as it is not purified by tanning, and that is the correct view.' And you know what is between them.
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage addresses two rulings: the sale of tanned and untanned hides, and the effect of Islamic slaughter (tadhkiyah) on the hide and meat of non-edible animals. The Hanafi position is that tanned hide may be sold and used, aligning with al-Shafi'i's new view and the majority. However, selling untanned dead animal hide is not permissible; al-Nawawi's attribution of permissibility to Abu Hanifa is a mistake. The majority of Hanafi books agree on non-permissibility. Regarding tadhkiyah: if the hide is purified by tanning, then slaughter also purifies both hide and meat, whether the animal is edible or not. But there is intra-Hanafi dispute on whether slaughter purifies the meat of non-edible animals. Some hold it purifies (Bada'i', Hidayah), others that it does not (Asrar, Kifayah, Tabyin). The author's own works differ; in Kafi he chose impurity, in Kanz purity. The relied-upon view according to many mashayikh is that slaughter purifies the hide but not the meat, similar to tanning. Additional conditions: slaughter must be by a Muslim or ahl al-kitab in the proper location. Majusi slaughter does not purify.

Key Terms
ذكاة (tadhkiyah): Islamic slaughter that renders the animal's hide and possibly meat pure; includes proper incision and tasmiyah.
سؤر (su'r): Remnant of a liquid after drinking; here it refers to the leftover moisture or filth that may affect purity.
دباغ (dibagh): Tanning; the process of treating animal hide to make it pure and usable.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on selling the hide of a dead animal (maytah) before tanning?
A) Permissible, because it can be sold like any other impure item.
B) Not permissible, because the hide is considered part of the maytah and selling it is not allowed.
C) Permissible only if the buyer will use it for a valid purpose.
D) Not permissible unless the seller is a tanner.

Key Takeaway
The sale of tanned hide is permissible; the sale of untanned dead animal hide is not; and tadhkiyah purifies the hide but not the meat of non-edible animals according to the preferred Hanafi view.

Tanning Hides: Permissible Materials and Sale
Inter-madhhab dispute on tanning agents and ruling on sale of hides

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school permits tanning with any substance that removes putrefaction, and distinguish the Hanafi position from the Shafi'i position on the sale of untanned hides.

Core Idea
A hide is purified by any agent that removes putrefaction; the Shafi'i hadith does not impose exclusivity.

The process of tanning transforms a dead animal's skin from impurity to purity. But what qualifies as a valid tanning agent? The Hanafi school, relying on the linguistic scope of 'dabagh' and the purpose of purification, holds that any substance that removes stench and decay suffices—even sunlight, soil, or salt. This lesson examines the inter-madhhab dispute on this issue and also clarifies the Hanafi ruling on selling hides before and after tanning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed issue
What exactly is being contested? The validity of tanning with sunlight, soil, and salt.
The Shafi'i school restricts tanning to specific agents like alum and qaraz.
2
State the Hanafi position and evidence
Hanafis hold that any substance removing putrefaction is valid, citing the broad meaning of 'dabagh' and authentic hadiths.
The purpose of tanning is purification, and any effective agent achieves it.
3
Present the Shafi'i opposition and evidence
Shafi'is rely on the hadith of Ibn Abbas and Maymunah that mention only water and qaraz.
They interpret this as exclusive, requiring those specific agents.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafis argue that the hadith is illustrative, not restrictive; the term 'dabagh' includes all methods.
The hadith was a response to a specific question and does not negate other valid methods.
5
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
Shafi'is require specific materials; Hanafis permit any effective agent, offering greater flexibility.
Also, selling untanned hides is impermissible according to the relied-upon Hanafi position, contrary to a mistaken report.

Source Text

كتاب الطهارة
۱۸۹
الواهية ا هـ . وإن قلنا إن الكلب كالخنزير فلا يحتاج إلى الجواب وقد قدمنا أن الدباغ جائز بكل ما يمنع النتن والفساد ولو تراباً أو ملحاً. وقال الشافعي لا يجوز بالشمس والتراب والملح لما رواه الدارقطني والبيهقي من حديث ابن عباس في شاة ميمونة قال : إنما حرم أكلها أو ليس في الماء والقرظ ما يطهرها؟ وهو حديث حسن ذكره النووي في شرح المهذب، ورواه أبو داود والنسائي في سننهما بمعناه عن ميمونة قال : يطهرها الماء والقرظ. ولنا ما تقدم من الأحاديث الصحيحة. فإن اسم الدباغ يتناول ما يقع بالتشميس والتتريب فلا يقيد بشيء ولأن المقصود يحصل به فلا معنى لاشتراط غيره وليس الحديث الذي استدل به الشافعي مما يقتضي الاختصاص بل المراد به ما في معناه بالإجماع، ولا يختص بما ذكر في الحديث. ثم عندنا يجوز بيع الجلد المدبوغ وينتفع به وهو قول الشافعي في الجديد وجمهور العلماء، وأما بيعه قبل الدباغ فقد نقل النووي في شرح المهذب أن أبا حنيفة يقول بجواز بيعه ورهنه كالثوب النجس وهو سهو منه فإن مذهب أبي حنيفة عدم جواز بيع جلود الميتة قبل الدباغ ذكره في المحيط وشرح الطحاوي وكثير من الكتب وفي بعض الكتب ذكر خلافاً. قال بعضهم إنه ملحق بالميتة وبعضهم ألحقه بالخمر، فالظاهر منه الاتفاق على عدم الجواز.
Book of Purity. 189. The weak [statement] etc. And if we say that the dog is like the pig, then there is no need for the answer. We have previously mentioned that tanning is permissible with everything that prevents stench and decay, even if it is soil or salt. Al-Shafi'i said: It is not permissible with sunlight, soil, and salt, because of what al-Daraqutni and al-Bayhaqi narrated from the hadith of Ibn Abbas regarding the sheep of Maymunah: He said: 'Did He only forbid its eating? Is there not in water and qaraz (a plant) what purifies it?' It is a good hadith mentioned by al-Nawawi in Sharh al-Muhadhdhab. Abu Dawud and al-Nasa'i narrated it in their Sunan with its meaning from Maymunah: She said: 'Water and qaraz purify it.' And we have the authentic hadiths that preceded. Indeed, the name 'dabagh' (tanning) includes what is done by exposure to sunlight and by covering with soil, so it is not restricted to anything. And because the purpose is achieved by it, so there is no meaning to stipulating something else. And the hadith used by al-Shafi'i as proof does not necessitate exclusivity; rather, what is intended is something similar by consensus, and it is not restricted to what is mentioned in the hadith. Then according to us, it is permissible to sell a tanned hide and benefit from it, and this is the saying of al-Shafi'i in his new opinion and the majority of scholars. As for selling it before tanning, al-Nawawi in Sharh al-Muhadhdhab transmitted that Abu Hanifa said: It is permissible to sell it and pawn it like a najis garment. This is a mistake on his part, for the madhhab of Abu Hanifa is that it is not permissible to sell the hides of dead animals before tanning, as mentioned in al-Muhit, Sharh al-Tahawi, and many books. In some books a difference is mentioned. Some said it is attached to the dead animal, and some attached it to wine. The apparent from this is agreement upon non-permissibility.
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute between the Hanafis and Shafi'is regarding the validity of tanning using sunlight, soil, and salt. The passage begins with a statement that tanning is permissible using anything that prevents putrefaction, even soil or salt. The Shafi'i school objects, citing a hadith that water and qaraz (a plant) are the purifiers. The Hanafi response is that the term 'dabagh' (tanning) linguistically includes these methods, and the hadith does not imply exclusivity. The passage then addresses the sale of hides: tanned hides may be sold by consensus, but untanned hides of dead animals may not be sold according to the Hanafi school, contrary to what was reported by al-Nawawi. The relied-upon position is that selling them is impermissible, as they are treated like the dead animal itself or like wine.

Key Terms
الدباغ (al-dabagh): Tanning; the process of treating animal skin to remove putridity and make it pure.
التشميس (al-tashmis): Tanning by exposure to sunlight.
التتريب (al-tatrib): Tanning by covering with soil.
الجلد المدبوغ (al-jild al-madbug): Tanned hide; skin that has been purified through tanning.
الميتة (al-maytah): Dead animal that has not been slaughtered Islamically.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which of the following is NOT a valid tanning method?
A) Using soil
B) Using salt
C) Using sunlight
D) Using only water without any agent

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits tanning with any substance that removes putrefaction, and selling tanned hides is permissible, but selling untanned dead animal hides is not.

Taharah — Purification

12 min
The Purification of Non-Edible Animal Meat by Dhabh: An Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on meat purity by dhabh

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two conflicting Hanafi positions on whether dhabh purifies the meat of a non-edible animal, explain the evidence for each, and identify the mufta bihi view.

Core Idea
Dhabh purifies the skin of any animal, but whether it purifies the meat of a non-edible animal is disputed among Hanafis.

In this passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, the author presents an intra-madhhab disagreement over a practical question: if a non-edible animal (like a fox) is slaughtered Islamically, does its meat become pure? The skin's purity is settled, but the meat's status is contested. We will walk through the two views, their bases, and the preferred ruling for fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the settled ruling: skin is purified
The author states that the apparent madhhab is that dhabh purifies the skin of any animal, edible or not. Confirm this from the text.
This is from al-Bada'i' and al-Nihayah.
2
Locate the disputed issue: meat of non-edible animals
The text then says there is ikhtilaf regarding the meat. Two camps are named: those who say it becomes pure (al-Bada'i', al-Hidayah, al-Tanjis) and those who say it remains impure (al-Asrar, al-Kifayah, al-Tabyin).
This is an intra-Hanafi dispute, not between madhhabs.
3
Examine the proofs for each view
Although the text does not detail proofs, infer the reasoning: the purity view analogizes dhabh to tanning or to the purification of skin; the impurity view analogizes the meat of a non-edible animal to a dead animal (maytah) which dhabh does not make edible.
The later mention of su'r (leftover water) also relates to purity of the animal itself.
4
Determine the tarjih (preferred view)
The author notes that Qadi Khan and the majority (according to al-Tabyin) chose purity. However, the author of Kanz al-Daqa'iq (al-Nasafi) himself had conflicting statements, but his choice in the Kanz is purity. Therefore, the mufta bihi is that dhabh purifies the meat as well.
This is based on later authority weights (nawadir and fatwa).
5
Consider the additional condition about the slaughterer
The author adds that many books require the slaughterer to be from ahl (People of the Book or similar) and the cut to be in the proper place. The slaughter of a Majusi does not purify by default, but there is a stronger view that it does. This condition interacts with the purity ruling.
This condition is separate from the meat dispute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن ما طهر جلده بالدباغ طهر بالذكاة لحمه وجلده، سواء كان مأكولاً أو لا. أما طهارة جلده فهو ظاهر المذهب كما في البدائع وفي النهاية أنه اختيار بعض المشايخ . وعند بعضهم إنما يطهر جلده بالذكاة إذا لم يكن سؤره نجساً ا.هـ. وأما طهارة لحمه إذا كان غير مأكول فقد اختلف فيه فصحح في البدائع والهداية والتنجيس طهارته، وصحح في الأسرار والكفاية والتبيين نجاسته وفي المعراج أنه قول المحققين من أصحابنا، وفي الخلاصة هو المختار واختاره قاضيخان وفي التبيين أنه قول أكثر المشايخ. وأما المصنف فقد اختلف كلامه فصحح في الكافي نجاسته واختار في الكنز في الدبائح طهارته وسنتكلم عليها بدلائلها وبيان ما هو الحق ثمة إن شاء الله تعالى. لكن في كثير من الكتب أن الذكاة إنما توجب الطهارة في الجلد واللحم إذا كانت من الأهل في المحل وهو ما بين اللبة واللحيين سمي بحيث لو كان مأكولاً يحل أكله بتلك الذكاة فذبيحة المجوسي لا توجب الطهارة لأنها إماتة وقد قدمنا عن معراج الدراية معزياً إلى المجتبى أن ذبيحة المجوسي وتارك التسمية عمداً توجب الطهارة على الأصح وإن لم يكن مأكولاً، وكذا نقل صاحب المعراج في هذه المسألة الطهارة عن القنية أيضاً هنا وصاحب القنية هو صاحب المجتبى وهو الإمام الزاهدي المشهور علمه وفقهه، ويدل على أن هذا هو الأصح أن صاحب النهاية ذكر هذا الشرط الذي قدمناه بصيغة «قيل» معزياً إلى فتاوى قاضيخان وفي منية المصلي: السنجاب إذا أخرج من دار الحرب وعلم أنه مدبوغ بودك الميتة لا تجوز الصلاة عليه ما لم يغسل وإن علم أنه مدبوغ بشيء طاهر جاز وإن لم يغسل، وإن شك فالأفضل أن يغسل ا هـ .
And know that whatever hide is purified by tanning, its meat and hide are purified by dhabh (slaughter), whether the animal is edible or not. As for the purity of its skin, it is the apparent madhhab as in al-Bada'i' and in al-Nihayah it is the choice of some mashayikh. And according to some, its skin is only purified by dhabh if its leftover water (su'r) is not impure, end. As for the purity of its meat if it is not edible, there is a difference of opinion: it is considered pure in al-Bada'i', al-Hidayah, and al-Tanjis; and considered impure in al-Asrar, al-Kifayah, and al-Tabyin. In al-Mi'raj it is the saying of the verifiers among our companions. In al-Khulasah it is the chosen one, and Qadi Khan chose it. In al-Tabyin it is the saying of most mashayikh. As for the author (of Kanz al-Daqa'iq), his words differ: he considered it impure in al-Kafi and chose purity in al-Kanz in the chapter of sacrifices. We will discuss them with their proofs and clarify what is correct there, Allah willing. But in many books, dhabh only necessitates purity in the skin and meat if it is from ahl (people of the book) in the proper place, which is between the lubbah and the jaw bones, such that if it were edible, its meat would be permissible by that dhabh. So the slaughter of a Majusi does not necessitate purity because it is a killing. And we have presented from Mi'raj al-Dirayah, attributing to al-Mujtaba, that the slaughter of a Majusi and one who intentionally omits the basmalah does necessitate purity on the correct view, even if not edible. Similarly, the author of al-Mi'raj transmitted purity in this issue from al-Qunyah as well. And the author of al-Qunyah is the author of al-Mujtaba, Imam al-Zahidi, famous for his knowledge and fiqh. And it indicates that this is the correct view that the author of al-Nihayah mentioned the condition we presented in the form of 'it is said' attributing to the Fatawa of Qadi Khan. And in Munyat al-Musalli: If a squirrel hide is brought from dar al-harb and it is known that it was tanned with fat of a dead animal, it is not permissible to pray on it unless it is washed. If it is known that it was tanned with something pure, it is permissible even if not washed. And if doubted, it is better to wash it, end.
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage discusses the effect of dhabh (Islamic slaughter) on the purity of an animal's skin and meat. The author states that if a hide is purified by tanning, then it is also purified by dhabh, regardless of whether the animal is edible. However, there is disagreement among Hanafi scholars on whether the meat of a non-edible animal becomes pure through dhabh. Some scholars (like those of al-Bada'i' and al-Hidayah) consider it pure, while others (like those of al-Asrar and al-Kifayah) consider it impure. The author then adds a condition: many books require that the slaughterer be from the 'ahl' (People of the Book or similar) and that the cut be in the proper place (between the lubbah and the jaw). The slaughter of a Majusi does not purify. However, according to al-Mujtaba, the slaughter of a Majusi and one who intentionally omits the basmalah does purify on the strongest view. The passage ends with a quote from Munyat al-Musalli about squirrel fur tanned with impure fat.

Key Terms
الذكاة (al-dhabh): Islamic slaughter of an animal by cutting the throat in a specific manner.
السؤر (al-su'r): Remaining water after an animal drinks; used to gauge purity of the animal itself.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi view in the Hanafi school, what is the status of the meat of a non-edible animal after proper Islamic slaughter (dhabh)?
A) It becomes pure as the skin does.
B) It remains impure because the animal is not edible.
C) Its purity depends on the intention of the slaughterer.
D) It is considered pure only if the animal is a permissible type.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, the skin of any animal is purified by dhabh, and the preferred view (mufta bihi) holds that the meat of non-edible animals is also purified, despite internal disagreement.

Ramli's Refinement: Dhabh Purifies Skin, Not Meat
Critical refinement of Qadi Khan's chosen view on dhabh purity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding dhabh's purification of non-edible animals and state the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
Dhabh purifies the skin not the meat – the correct view according to many mashayikh.

In the Hanafi school, a point of internal debate is whether the slaughter (dhabh) of a non-edible animal renders its meat pure, similar to its skin. The commentator al-Ramli intervenes to correct a reported view attributed to Qadi Khan, clarifying that the relied-upon position is that only the skin becomes pure, not the meat, as affirmed in al-Tabyin.

Guided Path
1
Identify the reported view and its context
Examine the author's statement that Qadi Khan 'chose' the view that dhabh purifies. Note that it appears under the chapter of corrupt sale in Qadi Khan's Fatawa, suggesting it may be limited to the hide's saleability.
Ramli hints the view may be context-specific, not general.
2
Locate Ramli's critique of the attribution
Al-Ramli points out that in the Fatawa, the discussion falls under corrupt sale (bay' fasid), which concerns the permissibility of selling the hide, not a blanket ruling of purity for meat.
Ramli advises checking the original text.
3
Compare with al-Tabyin's wording
Al-Ramli quotes from the actual copies of al-Tabyin: 'Many of the mashayikh said: It purifies its skin by it (dhabh) and does not purify its meat.' This clarifies that the position of 'most mashayikh' is the skin-only view.
The author of al-Bahr had cited al-Tabyin in support of a broader purity.
4
Derive the mufta bihi position
The correct and operative view is that dhabh purifies the skin (so it can be used) but does not purify the meat of a non-edible animal, just as tanning purifies only the skin.
This is settled by later authorities like Ramli.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (واختاره قاضيخان قال الرملي أقول : الذي اختاره قاضيخان في فتاواه من باب البيع الفاسد طهارته فراجعه تجد ما في نقله عنه، اللهم إلا أن يكون قد اختاره في كتاب آخر من كتبه فيكون كلامه قد اختلف كما وقع للمصنف في الكنز وفي الكافي تبيين قوله : (وفي التبيين إنه قول أكثر المشايخ قال الرملي :أقول : عبارة التبيين على ما في النسخ التي أطلعنا عليها. وقال كثير من المشايخ : يطهر جلده بها ولا يطهر لحمه كما لا يطهر بالدباغ وهو الصحيح، وأنت تعلم ما بينهما
His statement: 'And Qadi Khan chose it.' Al-Ramli said: I say: That which Qadi Khan chose in his Fatawa from the chapter of corrupt sale is its purity. So refer to it, you will find what is in the transmission from him. Unless he chose it in another book of his, then his words would differ as occurred with the author in al-Kanz and al-Kafi... Clarification of his statement: 'And in al-Tabyin it is the saying of most mashayikh.' Al-Ramli said: I say: The wording of al-Tabyin according to the copies we have seen is: 'And many of the mashayikh said: It purifies its skin by it (dhabh) and does not purify its meat, just as it is not purified by tanning, and that is correct.' And you know what is between them.
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This is a critical note by the commentator al-Ramli regarding the reported position of Qadi Khan. The author of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq had attributed to Qadi Khan the choice that dhabh purifies the meat of non-edible animals. Al-Ramli points out that in Qadi Khan's Fatawa, this is found under the chapter of corrupt sale, implying the hide is pure because it can be sold, but that may not be a general ruling. He also notes that the phrase in al-Tabyin that 'it is the saying of most mashayikh' should be checked against the actual text. Al-Ramli concludes that the correct view is that dhabh purifies the skin but not the meat, as it is with tanning.

Key Terms
البيع الفاسد (al-bay' al-fasid): A defective sale that is not outright invalid but requires certain conditions.
طهارة (taharah): Purity, ritual cleanliness.
دبغ (dibagh): Tanning, a process that purifies the skin of dead animals.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Ramli, what does the phrase 'it is the saying of most mashayikh' in al-Tabyin actually refer to?
A) Dhabh purifies both skin and meat.
B) Dhabh purifies only the skin, not the meat.
C) Dhabh has no effect on non-edible animals.
D) Tanning is more effective than dhabh.

Key Takeaway
For non-edible animals, Hanafi mufta bihi is that dhabh purifies the skin but not the meat, a position verified by later scholars through textual analysis.

Purity of Dead Animal Parts: The Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Rennet and Milk
Ruling and intra-Hanafi dispute on purity of body parts not permeated by life

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi principle regarding the purity of body parts not permeated by life and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on rennet (anfaha) and milk from dead animals, including the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
Solid dead animal parts (hair, bone) are pure; liquid rennet and milk are disputed between Abu Hanifa (pure) and the Sahibayn (impure).

In this lesson, we examine a foundational Hanafi principle: parts of a dead body that do not contain life (such as hair, bones, hooves, and milk) are considered pure after death. However, there is an important internal disagreement regarding rennet (anfaha) and milk from a dead animal. Understanding this dispute reveals how Hanafi scholars apply the broader principle to edge cases involving contact with impure membranes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the General Principle
The passage states: every part of a dead body that is not permeated by life is ruled pure after death. This includes hair, bones, feathers, beaks, hooves, milk, eggs with thin shells, and rennet (anfaha). There is no disagreement on most of these parts.
The principle is based on the idea that impurity resides in the life-flowing blood and fluids, not in dry, solid structures.
2
Recognize the Intention of the Statement
The author mentions these parts in the chapter on water to indicate that if they fall into water, they do not make the water impure. This is a practical application of the purity ruling.
3
Locate the Dispute on Rennet and Milk
The text then notes: 'the difference among them is only regarding rennet and milk: are they impure?' This is the sole point of intra-Hanafi disagreement in this context.
The disagreement is about liquid rennet and milk; solid rennet can be washed if impure.
4
Analyze the Positions
Abu Hanifa: both are pure because life did not permeate them and the impurity of the surrounding tissue does not affect them. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (Sahibayn): they are impure because of direct contact with the impure stomach lining (for rennet) or udder (for milk). However, if solid, rennet can be purified by washing.
This is a classic case of whether a pure substance can become impure by mere contact without life permeation.
5
Consider the Thamarah al-Khilaf (Practical Consequences)
Abu Hanifa's view allows use of milk and rennet from dead animals in food and medicine without purification. The Sahibayn require washing of solid rennet and prohibit liquid rennet and milk unless from a living animal.
Later Hanafi fatwa practice may favor different positions; this lesson focuses on the textual dispute.

قوله: (وشعر الإنسان والميتة وعظمهما طاهران إنما ذكرهما في بحث المياه لإفادة أنه إذا وقع في الماء لا ينجسه لطهارته عندنا، والأصل أن كل ما لا تحله الحياة من أجزاء الهوية محكوم بطهارته بعد موت ما هي جزؤه كالشعر والريش والمنقار والعظم والعصب والحافر والظلف واللبن والبيض الضعيف القشر والأنفحة، لا خلاف بين أصحابنا في ذلك وإنما الخلاف بينهم في الأنفحة واللبن هل هما متنجسان؟ فقالا نعم لمجاورتهما الغشاء النجس؛ فإن كانت الأنفحة جامدة تطهر بالغسل وإلا تعذر طهارتها. وقال أبو حنيفة رحمه الله تعالى : ليسا بمتنجسين وعلى قياسهما قالوا في السخلة إذا سقطت من أمها وهي رطبة فيبست ثم وقعت في الماء لا تنجس لأنها كانت في معدنها كذا في فتح القدير. وفي إدخال العصب في المسائل التي لا خلاف فيها نظر، فقد صرحوا أن في العصب روايتين، وصرح في السراج الوهاج أن الصحيح نجاسته إلا أن صاحب الفتح تبع صاحب البدائع. فالتحرير ما في غاية البيان أن أجزاء الميتة لا تخلوا إما أن يكون فيها دم أو لا، فالأولى كاللحم نجسة، والثانية ففي غير الخنزير والآدمي ليس بنجسة إن كانت صلبة كالشعر والعظم بلا خلاف، وأما الأنفحة المائعة واللبن فكذلك عند أبي حنيفة، وعندهما نجس. وأما الآدمي ففيه روايتان : في رواية نجسة فلا يجوز بيعها ولا الصلاة معها إذا كانت أكثر من قدر الدرهم وزناً أو عرضاً، وفي رواية طاهرة لعدم الدم وعدم جواز البيع للكرامة، وأما العصب ففيه من المخالفة. قوله : (والأنفحة) بكسر الهمزة وفتح الفاء وتخفيف الحاء أو تشديدها، شيء يستخرج من بطن الجدي أصفر يعصر في صوفة مبتلة في اللبن فيغلظ كالجبن ولا تكون إلا لذي كرش، وقيل من نفس الكرش إلا أنه يسمى أنفحة ما دام رضيعاً، وإن رعى العشب كرشاً ويقال لها المنفحة سمي أيضاً. كذا في المغرب من جلبي على الزيلعي . وقال ابن فرشته في شرح مجمع البحرين قوله : (وأنفحة الميتة) مبتدأ وخبره محذوف وهو طاهر بقرينة قوله : ( ولبنها طاهر) أنفحة بكسر الهمزة وفتح الفاء مخففة كرش الجدي أو الجمل الصغير ما لم يأكل يقال لها بالفارسية ينيرمايه، يعني أنفحة الميتة، جامدة كانت أو مائعة طاهرة عند أبي حنيفة، وكذا لبنها . أما الأنفحة الجامدة فلأن الحياة لم تحل فيها، وأما المائعة واللبن فإن نجاسة محلهما لم تكن مؤثرة فيهما قبل الموت ولهذا كان اللبن الخارج
His statement: 'And the hair of a human and a dead animal, and their bones, are pure.' He only mentioned them in the discussion of water to indicate that if they fall into water, they do not make it impure because of their purity according to us. The principle is that every part of a body that is not permeated by life is ruled to be pure after the death of that of which it is a part, such as hair, feathers, beak, bone, nerve, hoof, cloven hoof, milk, eggs with thin shells, and rennet. There is no difference among our companions on that. The difference among them is only regarding rennet and milk: are they impure? They (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: Yes, due to their proximity to the impure membrane. If the rennet is solid, it becomes pure by washing; otherwise its purification is impossible. Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him, said: They are not impure. And by analogy with them, they said regarding a newborn lamb that falls from its mother while wet and then dries, and then falls into water, it does not become impure because it was in its source. As in Fath al-Qadir. And including the nerve among the issues where there is no difference is questionable. For they have explicitly stated that there are two narrations regarding the nerve. And it is stated in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj that the correct view is its impurity, except that the author of al-Fath followed the author of al-Bada'i'. So the correct view is as in Ghayat al-Bayan: The parts of a dead animal are either containing blood or not. The first, like flesh, is impure. The second, for other than pigs and humans, is not impure if they are solid like hair and bone without dispute. As for liquid rennet and milk, it is likewise according to Abu Hanifa, while according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) they are impure. As for a human, there are two narrations: one narration says they are impure, so it is not permissible to sell them nor to pray with them if they are more than the size of a dirham in weight or area. In another narration, they are pure due to the absence of blood and the non-permissibility of sale due to honor. As for the nerve, there is opposition in it. His statement: 'And al-anfaha (rennet)' is said with a kasra on the hamza and a fatha on the fa and a tashdid or takhif on the ha. It is a thing extracted from the stomach of a kid, yellow, pressed in a wet fleece in milk, then it thickens like cheese. It only exists in a ruminant. It is said that it is from the stomach itself, but it is called anfaha as long as it is suckling, and if it grazes grass it is called kirsh. It is also called munfaha. This is from al-Mughrib from Jalabi on al-Zayla'i. And Ibn Firishtha said in Sharh Majma' al-Bahrayn: 'His statement: 'And the anfaha of a dead animal' is a subject and its predicate is omitted, which is 'pure' by the context of his statement 'and its milk is pure'. Anfaha with a kasra on the hamza and a fatha on the fa, lightened, is the stomach of a kid or a small camel that has not eaten. In Persian it is called yenirmaye. Meaning the anfaha of a dead animal, whether solid or liquid, is pure according to Abu Hanifa, and likewise its milk. As for the solid anfaha, because life did not permeate it. As for the liquid and milk, because the impurity of their container did not affect them before death, hence the milk that comes out...
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage discusses the purity of parts of a dead body that do not have life flowing through them. The principle is that anything that does not have life (like hair, bones, horns, hooves, milk, eggs with thin shells, and rennet) is pure after the animal's death. There is no disagreement among the Hanafi school on the majority of these parts. However, there is a dispute regarding rennet (anfaha) and milk: Abu Hanifa considers both pure, while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad consider them impure due to their proximity to the impure membrane. The passage also discusses the issue of nerve (asab), noting two narrations. It then provides a detailed definition of anfaha and its linguistic origin. The overall ruling is that solid parts like hair and bones are pure by consensus, while liquid parts like milk and liquid rennet are subject to the disagreement.

Key Terms
الأنفحة (al-anfaha): Rennet; a substance extracted from the stomach of a young suckling animal used to curdle milk.
العصب (al-'asab): Nerves or sinews of an animal.
اللبن (al-laban): Milk from a dead animal.
الشعر (al-sha'r): Hair, which is a solid part of a dead body not permeated by life.
العظم (al-'azm): Bone, a solid part of a dead body not permeated by life.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school rules that parts of dead animals not containing life are pure, with a specific internal disagreement on rennet and milk arising from different views on contamination by contact.

Taharah — Purification
intermediate
8 min
The Hanafi Principle of Purity for Dead Animal Parts and the Anfahah Dispute
Ruling on purity of dead animal parts with intra-Hanafi dispute on anfahah and milk

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi principle that parts not permeated by life are pure after death, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding rennet (anfahah) and milk from a dead animal, including the evidence of each side and the practical difference.

Core Idea
Solid parts of dead animals without blood are pure; the dispute is whether liquid contents like rennet and milk share that purity.

In this lesson, we examine a key Hanafi principle in taharah: parts of a dead animal that are not penetrated by life—such as hair, bones, hooves—remain pure after death. This principle is foundational for many rulings. However, an important intra-Hanafi disagreement arises over liquid substances extracted from the dead animal's stomach: rennet (anfahah) and milk. Imam Abu Hanifa holds them pure, while his two students consider them impure due to contact with the impure stomach lining. We will unpack the reasoning behind each view and its practical implications.

Guided Path
1
State the foundational principle
Identify the general ruling: hair and bones of dead animals (and humans) are pure. Understand the legal maxim 'ma la tahulluhu al-hayah' (parts not permeated by life remain pure after death).
This principle is agreed upon by all Hanafis.
2
Locate the disagreement
Note that the dispute is only about anfahah (rennet) and milk, not the solid parts. The passage explicitly says: 'لا خلاف بين أصحابنا في ذلك وإنما الخلاف بينهم في الأنفحة واللبن'.
Solid parts like hair, bone, hoof are unanimously pure.
3
Analyze Abu Hanifa's evidence
Abu Hanifa argues that the impurity of the surrounding (stomach membrane) does not affect these substances before death, as evidenced by milk emerging pure from between dung and blood in a living animal. Therefore, death does not change their purity.
The principle of purity is based on the inherent nature of these substances.
4
Analyze the Sahibayn's evidence
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad argue that the impurity of the stomach lining transmits to the contents due to adjacency and contact. For solid anfahah, washing can purify it; for liquid, purification is impossible.
The key difference is whether the impurity of the container affects the contained.
5
Determine the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf)
If anfahah or milk from a dead animal falls into water, according to Abu Hanifa it does not render the water impure; according to the Sahibayn, it does. Solid anfahah can be washed if impure; liquid cannot.
This affects rulings on water purity and food ingredients like cheese.

جزؤه كالشعر والريش والمنقار والعظم والعصب والحافر والظلف واللبن والبيض الضعيف القشر والأنفحة، لا خلاف بين أصحابنا في ذلك وإنما الخلاف بينهم في الأنفحة واللبن هل هما متنجسان؟ فقالا نعم لمجاورتهما الغشاء النجس؛ فإن كانت الأنفحة جامدة تطهر بالغسل وإلا تعذر طهارتها. وقال أبو حنيفة رحمه الله تعالى : ليسا بمتنجسين وعلى قياسهما قالوا في السخلة إذا سقطت من أمها وهي رطبة فيبست ثم وقعت في الماء لا تنجس فالتحرير ما في غاية البيان أن أجزاء الميتة لا تخلوا إما أن يكون فيها دم أو لا، فالأولى كاللحم نجسة، والثانية ففي غير الخنزير والآدمي ليس بنجسة إن كانت صلبة كالشعر والعظم بلا خلاف، وأما الأنفحة المائعة واللبن فكذلك عند أبي حنيفة، وعندهما نجس. وأما الآدمي ففيه روايتان : في رواية نجسة فلا يجوز بيعها ولا الصلاة معها إذا كانت أكثر من قدر الدرهم وزناً أو عرضاً، وفي رواية طاهرة لعدم الدم وعدم جواز البيع من نفس الكرش إلا أنه يسمى أنفحة ما دام رضيعاً، وإن رعى العشب كرشاً ويقال لها المنفحة سمي أيضاً. كذا في المغرب من جلبي على الزيلعي . وقال ابن فرشته في شرح مجمع البحرين قوله : (وأنفحة الميتة) مبتدأ أبي حنيفة، وكذا لبنها . أما الأنفحة الجامدة فلأن الحياة لم تحل فيها، وأما المائعة واللبن فإن نجاسة محلهما لم تكن مؤثرة فيهما قبل الموت ولهذا كان اللبن الخارج من بين فرث ودم طاهراً فلا تكون مؤثرة بعد الموت. قوله: (وقالا) (نجس) يعني قالا أنفحة الميتة مطلقاً نجس ولبنها أيضاً نجس لأن تنجس المحل يوجب تنجس ما فيه . قوله: (وتطهر الجامدة بالغسل) قيد بالجامدة لأن المائعة لا تطهر بالغسل عندهما . كذا في شرح المصنف. (أقول) لا حاجة إلى إرداف قولهما لأنه في طرف النفي من قوله طاهر) ولو قال وقالا تظهر الجامدة بالغسل» لكان كافياً لاح إلى اشتباه آخر وهو أن المائعة إن كانت مما تنعصر كان ينبغي أن تطهر، وإن كانت مما لا تنعصر فكذا عند أبي يوسف لما سبق من أن غير المنعصر عنده يطهر بالغسل والتجفيف ثلاثاً اهـ. قال
His statement: (And the hair of a human and a dead animal, and their bones are pure). He only mentioned them in the discussion of waters to indicate that if they fall into water, they do not make it impure due to their purity according to us. The principle is that every part of an entity that life does not permeate is ruled pure after the death of that of which it is a part, like hair, feathers, beak, bone, nerve, hoof, cloven hoof, milk, thin-shelled egg, and rennet. There is no disagreement among our companions about that; rather, the disagreement among them is about rennet and milk—are they impure? The two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said yes, due to their adjacency to the impure membrane; if the rennet is solid, it becomes pure by washing, otherwise its purification is impossible. Abu Hanifa said: they are not impure. By analogy to their view, they said about a newborn lamb that falls from its mother while wet, then dries, then falls into water, it does not make it impure because it was in its source. This is in Fath al-Qadir. Including nerve among issues where there is no disagreement is questionable, for they explicitly state that there are two transmissions regarding nerve, and it is stated in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj that the correct view is its impurity, except that the author of al-Fath followed the author of al-Bada'i'. The correct analysis is what is in Ghayat al-Bayan: parts of a dead animal either contain blood or not. The first type, like meat, is impure. The second type, other than pig and human, is not impure if it is solid like hair and bone without disagreement. As for liquid rennet and milk, it is likewise according to Abu Hanifa, but according to the two it is impure. As for human, there are two transmissions: in one transmission it is impure, so its sale is not permissible nor prayer with it if it is more than the size of a dirham in weight or area; in another transmission it is pure due to the absence of blood and the impermissibility of sale for respect. As for nerve, there are two transmissions. His statement: (And the anfahah) with kasra on the hamza, fatha on the fa, and tashdid or tashdid on the ha, is something extracted from the belly of a kid, yellow, squeezed into a wet tuft in milk, then it thickens like cheese, and it only exists in ruminants. It is said from the stomach itself, but it is called anfahah as long as it is suckling; if it grazes on grass, it is called karish. It is also called munfahah. So in al-Mughrib from Jalabi on al-Zayla'i. Ibn Farishtah said in Sharh Majma' al-Bahrayn: His statement: (And anfahah of a dead animal) is a subject, its predicate omitted, and it is pure by context of his statement: (and its milk is pure). Anfahah with kasra on hamza and fatha on fa, lightened: the stomach of a kid or young camel that has not eaten; in Persian it is called yenirme. That is, anfahah of a dead animal, solid or liquid, is pure according to Abu Hanifa, and likewise its milk. As for solid anfahah, because life did not permeate it; as for liquid and milk, the impurity of their locus was not effective in them before death, and therefore milk that comes out from between dung and blood is pure, so it is not effective after death. His statement: (And the two said: impure) meaning the two said: anfahah of a dead animal is absolutely impure, and its milk is also impure, because the impurity of the place necessitates the impurity of what is in it. His statement: (And the solid is purified by washing) is restricted to solid because the liquid is not purified by washing according to them. So in Sharh al-Musannif. (I say) There is no need to follow up their statement because it is on the side of negation from his statement 'pure'. If he had said 'the two said the solid is purified by washing', it would have been sufficient, leading to another confusion: that if the liquid is of a type that can be squeezed, it should be purified; and if it is of a type that cannot be squeezed, then according to Abu Yusuf it is similar to what preceded, that non-squeezable according to him is purified by washing and drying three times. End. He said:
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the purity of certain parts of dead animals, specifically rennet (anfahah) and milk. The author begins by stating the general ruling: hair and bones of both humans and dead animals are pure. The reason they are mentioned in the chapter on water is to clarify that if such items fall into water, they do not render it impure because they themselves are pure. The underlying principle is that any part of a living being that is not permeated by life (i.e., does not contain blood or life-sustaining moisture) remains pure after the death of the whole. Examples include hair, feathers, beak, bone, nerve, hoof, cloven hoof, milk, thin-shelled egg, and rennet. There is no disagreement among Hanafi scholars on the purity of solid parts like hair and bone. However, a dispute arises concerning rennet and milk. The two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) consider them impure because they are adjacent to an impure membrane (the stomach lining). If the rennet is solid, it can be purified by washing; if liquid, purification is impossible. Abu Hanifa, on the other hand, holds that they are not impure at all. He argues that the impurity of the surrounding area does not transfer to these substances before death, and the fact that milk emerges pure from between dung and blood shows that the impurity of the environment does not affect them. Therefore, even after death, they remain pure. The passage also mentions a related issue: if a newborn lamb falls from its mother while wet and then dries, and later falls into water, it does not make the water impure because it was in its 'source' (the mother's womb), which is pure by default. The author then notes a point of confusion: some scholars include nerve (asab) in the list of parts without disagreement, but there are actually two transmissions regarding its purity. According to al-Siraj al-Wahhaj, the correct view is that nerve is impure. However, the author of al-Fath followed al-Bada'i' in considering it pure. The author then provides a refined analysis: parts of a dead animal are either those containing blood (like meat) or those without blood. The latter are pure if they are solid, like hair and bone, without disagreement. Liquid rennet and milk follow the same ruling according to Abu Hanifa (pure), but according to the two (impure). Regarding human parts, there are two transmissions: one considers them impure (so sale is impermissible and prayer with them invalid if more than a dirham), and the other considers them pure due to lack of blood, but sale is still prohibited out of respect. The passage also explains the linguistic meaning of anfahah and provides additional commentary on the dispute, concluding with the author's own opinion that solid rennet is purified by washing, but liquid rennet does not become pure by washing according to the two. The author remarks that if the liquid is squeezable, it might be purified by squeezing, but this is not explicitly stated in the text.

Key Terms
الأنفحة (al-anfahah): Rennet: a substance extracted from the stomach of a young ruminant (kid or camel) that has not yet eaten grass, used to coagulate milk into cheese.
ما لا تحله الحياة (ma la tahulluhu al-hayah): Parts of a living being that are not imbued with life, i.e., do not contain blood or life force, such as hair, bones, hooves.
الغشاء النجس (al-ghisha' al-najis): The impure membrane lining the stomach of the dead animal, which is considered najis due to contact with blood and impurities.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, why is milk from a dead animal considered pure?
A) Because milk does not contain blood.
B) Because milk emerges pure from between dung and blood even before death.
C) Because milk is not in contact with the stomach membrane.
D) Because milk is solid.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes between solid non-blood parts (pure) and questionable liquid contents, with a valid intra-Hanafi dispute on rennet and milk.

Taharah — Purification
intermediate
8 min
Purity of Human and Pig Body Parts in Hanafi Fiqh
Ruling on purity of human and pig parts with intra-Hanafi differences

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the purity of human hair, bones, teeth, and pig parts, explain the evidence for human teeth being pure, and differentiate between the views of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad on pig hair.

Core Idea
Human body parts are pure but inviolable; pig parts are impure with a necessity concession for their hair.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq lays out the nuanced Hanafi position on human and pig body parts. While human parts are considered pure due to the absence of blood, their sale and use are prohibited out of respect (karamah). In contrast, pig parts are inherently impure, though a dispensation allows leatherworkers to use pig hair when no substitute exists. The passage also records differences between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad regarding the effect of pig hair on water and prayer.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general ruling for humans vs. pigs
First, note that human parts (hair, nails, bones, teeth) are ruled pure (tahir) on the correct view, while pig parts (hair, bones, all parts) are ruled impure (najis). This is the foundation.
The impurity of pig parts is absolute except for a concession.
2
Distinguish between shaven/cut hair and plucked hair
Hair from a dead animal or human is pure only if shaven or cut (mahluq/majzuz); if plucked (mantuf), it becomes impure due to attached tissue. Apply this distinction to human hair as well.
Plucked hair may carry blood or skin, hence impure.
3
Analyze the ruling on human teeth and bones
Teeth are pure because they contain no blood (the true source of impurity). The passage refutes the weak opinion that human teeth are impure, arguing it is impossible for a dog's tooth to be pure while a human's is impure.
The evidence is talil (rational): the impurifier is blood, absent in teeth.
4
Account for intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on pig hair
Abu Yusuf holds pig hair makes water impure and invalidates prayer if > dirham; Muhammad holds it does not make water impure and prayer is valid. The mufta bihi is Abu Yusuf's view of impurity.
The concession for leatherworkers is a dispensation, not a change in ruling.

روايتان : إحداهما أنه طاهر لأنه عظم، والأخرى أنه نجس لأن فيه حياة والحس يقع به ا هـ . وأما الخنزير فشعره وعظمه وجميع أجزائه نجسة ورخص في شعره للخرازين للضرورة لأن غيره لا يقوم مقامه عندهم. وعن أبي يوسف رحمه الله تعالى أنه كره لهم ذلك أيضاً ولا يجوز بيعه في الروايات كلها وإن وقع شعره في الماء القليل نجسه عند أبي يوسف، وعند محمد لا ينجس. وإن صلى معه جاز عند محمد، وعند أبي يوسف لا يجوز إذا كان أكثر من قدر الدرهم واختلفوا في قدر الدرهم قيل وزناً وقيل بسطاً كذا في السراج الوهاج. وذكر السراج الهندي أن قول أبي يوسف بنجاسته هو ظاهر الرواية وصححه في البدائع ورجحه في الاختيار. وفي التنجيس : لا بأس ببيع عظام الموتى لأنه لا يحل العظام الموت وليس في العظام دم فلا تتنجس فيجوز بيعها إلا بيع عظام الآدمي والخنزير ا هـ. وفي المحيط : إن عظم الميتة إذا كان عليه دسومة ووقع في الماء نجسه وفي السراج الوهاج : شعر الميتة إنما يكون طاهراً إذا كان محلوقاً أو مجزوزاً وإن كان منتوفاً فهو نجس، وكذا شعر الآدمي على هذا التفصيل. وعن محمد في نجاسة شعر الآدمي وظفره وعظمه روايتان الصحيح منهما الطهارة. وفي النهاية واختلف في السن هل هو عظم أو طرف عصب يابس لأن العظم لا يحدث في الإنسان بعد الولادة وقيل هو عظم. وما وقع في الذخيرة وغيرها من أن أسنان الكلب إذا كانت يابسة طاهرة وأسنان الآدمي نجسة بناء على أن الكلب يطهر بالذكاة وما يطهر بها فعظمه طاهر بخلاف الآدمي فضعيف فإن المصرح به في البدائع والكافي وغيرهما بأن . سن الآدمي طاهرة على ظاهر المذهب وهو الصحيح وعلل له البدائع بأن لا دم فيها والمنجس هو الدم ولأنه يستحيل أن تكون طاهرة من الكلب نجسة من الآدمي المكرم إلا إنه لا يجوز بيعها ويحرم الانتفاع بها احتراماً للآدمي كما إذا طحن سن الآدمي الحنطة أو عظمه لا يباح تناول الخبز المتخذ من دقيقهما لا لكونه نجساً بل تعظيماً له كيلا أجزاء الآدمي. كذا هذا وكذا ذكر في المبسوط والنهاية والمعراج. وعلى هذا ما ذكر في التنجيس رجل قطعت أذنه أو قلعت سنه فأعاد أذنه إلى مكانها أو سنه الساقط إلى مكانها فصلي أو صلى وأذنه أو سنه في كمه يجزيه لأن ما ليس بلحم لا يحله الموت فلا يتنجس
Two transmissions: one that it is pure because it is bone, and another that it is impure because there is life in it and sensation occurs through it. End. As for the pig, its hair, bone, and all its parts are impure, and a concession was made for its hair for leatherworkers due to necessity, because nothing else substitutes for it according to them. From Abu Yusuf that he disliked that for them as well, and its sale is not permitted in all transmissions. If its hair falls into a small amount of water, it makes it impure according to Abu Yusuf, whereas according to Muhammad it does not make it impure. If one prays with it, it is valid according to Muhammad, but according to Abu Yusuf it is not valid if it is more than the size of a dirham. They differed about the size of the dirham: it is said weight, and it is said spread. So in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj. Al-Siraj al-Hindi mentioned that the statement of Abu Yusuf about its impurity is the apparent transmission, and he corrected it in al-Bada'i' and preferred it in al-Ikhtiyar. In al-Tanjis: There is no problem with selling bones of the dead because death does not affect bones and there is no blood in them, so they do not become impure; it is permissible to sell them except selling human and pig bones. End. In al-Muhit: If a bone of a dead animal has grease on it and falls into water, it makes it impure. In al-Siraj al-Wahhaj: The hair of a dead animal is only pure if it is shaven or cut; if it is plucked, it is impure. Likewise, human hair follows this detail. From Muhammad regarding the impurity of human hair, nail, and bone, there are two transmissions; the correct one among them is purity. In al-Nihayah: They differed about the tooth: is it bone or a piece of dry nerve? Because bone does not develop in humans after birth, and it is said it is bone. What is mentioned in al-Dhakhirah and others that the teeth of a dog, if dry, are pure, and human teeth are impure, based on the fact that a dog is purified by slaughter and what is purified by it, its bone is pure, unlike humans, is weak. For it is explicitly stated in al-Bada'i', al-Kafi, and others that the human tooth is pure on the apparent madhhab, and this is correct. Al-Bada'i' reasoned it by saying there is no blood in it, and the impurifier is blood, and because it is impossible that what is pure from a dog would be impure from a human who is honored. However, its sale is not permitted, and using it is prohibited out of respect for humans, just as if a human tooth or bone is ground into flour, it is not permissible to eat bread made from that flour, not because it is impure but out of reverence, so that human parts are not consumed. This is how it is mentioned in al-Mabsut, al-Nihayah, and al-Mi'raj. Based on this is what is mentioned in al-Tanjis: A man whose ear was cut off or his tooth extracted, and he reattached his ear to its place or his fallen tooth to its place, then he prayed, or he prayed with his ear or tooth in his sleeve, it is valid, because what is not flesh is not affected by death, so it does not become impure.
Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This passage covers the rulings on human and pig parts. It begins with two transmissions regarding nerve (asab): one considers it pure (as bone), the other impure (due to life and sensation). Then it discusses pig: all parts are impure, but there is a concession for using pig hair for leatherworkers due to necessity, as no substitute exists. Abu Yusuf disapproved of even that concession. Selling pig parts is prohibited in all narrations. Regarding pig hair falling into water: Abu Yusuf says it makes the water impure; Muhammad says it does not. Similarly, praying with pig hair: Muhammad allows it; Abu Yusuf prohibits if more than a dirham's size. The dirham size is disputed: weight or area. Al-Siraj al-Hindi considers Abu Yusuf's view the apparent transmission. Selling bones of dead animals (other than human and pig) is permissible because death does not affect bones and they contain no blood. However, if a bone has grease, it can make water impure. Hair from a dead animal is pure only if shaven or cut; if plucked, it is impure. The same applies to human hair. Regarding human hair, nails, and bones, Imam Muhammad has two transmissions; the correct one is purity. The passage then focuses on teeth: there is disagreement whether a tooth is bone or dried nerve. The correct Hanafi position is that human teeth are pure, as blood is absent, and it is impossible that a dog's tooth is pure while a human's is impure. However, selling human teeth is prohibited out of respect. If human teeth or bones are ground into flour, the resulting bread is not permissible to eat, not because it is impure, but to prevent consumption of human parts. Finally, the passage cites a case: if a man's ear is cut off or his tooth extracted, and he reattaches them and prays, or prays with them in his sleeve, the prayer is valid because these parts are not flesh and are not affected by death, so they remain pure.

Key Terms
الكرامة (al-karamah): Honor and respect due to humans; prohibits selling or using human body parts even if pure.
المنتف (al-mantuf): Plucked (hair); hair that is pulled out is considered impure because it may have blood or tissue attached, unlike shaven or cut hair.
ظاهر الرواية (zahir al-riwayah): The apparent or preferred transmission from the authoritative narrations (the six books of Imam Muhammad).

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Because they are najis.

C) Out of respect for the human being.
D) Because they cannot be purified.

Key Takeaway
Human body parts are pure but inviolable; pig parts are impure with a limited concession for leatherworkers, and intra-Hanafi differences on pig hair are resolved in favour of impurity.

Taharah — Purification

Purity of Bones, Hair, and Teeth: Hanafi Intra-School Differences
Rulings on purity of bones, hair, and teeth with intra-Hanafi disputes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the Hanafi rulings on the purity of bones, hair, and teeth from dead animals and humans, and identify the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) despite intra-Hanafi differences.

Core Idea
Human teeth, hair, and bones are pure (tahir) in the Hanafi school, but prohibited from sale and use out of respect for the human.

In this lesson, we examine a nuanced area of Hanafi fiqh: the purity status of parts from dead animals and humans. While the Hanafi school generally considers dry bones and hair of dead animals pure, there is intra-school disagreement on human parts and pig hair. We will navigate through the various narrations and understand the relied-upon view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two narrations on bone purity
The passage begins by presenting two rivayat on a bone from a dead animal: one says it is pure because it is bone, the other impure because it had life and sensation. This sets the stage for the broader discussion.
This is a common pattern in Hanafi texts: listing rivayat before tarjih.
2
Understand the ruling on pig parts
The pig is a special case: all its parts are intrinsically impure. However, a concession was given for using pig hair in leatherwork due to necessity. Note the disagreement between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad on whether pig hair in water makes it impure.
Abu Yusuf's view (impure) is considered the apparent transmission (zahir al-riwaya) and is relied upon.
3
Trace the dispute on human hair and nails
From Imam Muhammad, there are two narrations on human hair, nails, and bones: one says they are impure, the correct one says they are pure. The same principle applies: no blood, no impurity.
The purity of human hair is conditional on it being shaved or cut, not plucked.
4
Analyze the ruling on human teeth
A weak view claimed dog teeth are pure (due to slaughter purification) but human teeth impure. The correct view, supported by al-Bada'i' and al-Kafi, is that human teeth are pure. The reasoning: no blood, and it is illogical that a dog's tooth be pure and a human's impure.
Despite purity, selling or using human teeth is prohibited out of respect (hurma).
5
Determine the mufta bihi position
The relied-upon position is that all human bones, hair, and teeth are pure (tahir), but they cannot be sold or utilized. This is the 'zahir al-madhhab' and is affirmed in major Hanafi works.
The prohibition is for respect (ta'dhim), not due to impurity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الطهارة
۱۹۱
روايتان : إحداهما أنه طاهر لأنه عظم، والأخرى أنه نجس لأن فيه حياة والحس يقع به ا هـ . وأما الخنزير فشعره وعظمه وجميع أجزائه نجسة ورخص في شعره للخرازين للضرورة لأن غيره لا يقوم مقامه عندهم. وعن أبي يوسف رحمه الله تعالى أنه كره لهم ذلك أيضاً ولا يجوز بيعه في الروايات كلها وإن وقع شعره في الماء القليل نجسه عند أبي يوسف، وعند محمد لا ينجس. وإن صلى معه جاز عند محمد، وعند أبي يوسف لا يجوز إذا كان أكثر من قدر الدرهم واختلفوا في قدر الدرهم قيل وزناً وقيل بسطاً كذا في السراج الوهاج. وذكر السراج الهندي أن قول أبي يوسف بنجاسته هو ظاهر الرواية وصححه في البدائع ورجحه في الاختيار. وفي التنجيس : لا بأس ببيع عظام الموتى لأنه لا يحل العظام الموت وليس في العظام دم فلا تتنجس فيجوز بيعها إلا بيع عظام الآدمي والخنزير ا هـ. وفي المحيط : إن عظم الميتة إذا كان عليه دسومة وقع في الماء نجسه وفي السراج الوهاج : شعر الميتة إنما يكون طاهراً إذا كان محلوقاً أو مجزوزاً وإن كان منتوفاً فهو نجس، وكذا شعر الآدمي على هذا التفصيل. وعن محمد في نجاسة شعر الآدمي وظفره وعظمه روايتان الصحيح منهما الطهارة. وفي النهاية واختلف في السن هل هو عظم أو طرف عصب يابس لأن العظم لا يحدث في الإنسان بعد الولادة وقيل هو عظم. وما وقع في الذخيرة وغيرها من أن أسنان الكلب إذا كانت يابسة طاهرة وأسنان الآدمي نجسة بناء على أن الكلب يطهر بالذكاة وما يطهر بها فعظمه طاهر بخلاف الآدمي فضعيف فإن المصرح به في البدائع والكافي وغيرهما بأن . سن الآدمي طاهرة على ظاهر المذهب وهو الصحيح وعلل له البدائع بأن لا دم فيها والمنجس هو الدم ولأنه يستحيل أن تكون طاهرة من الكلب نجسة من الآدمي المكرم إلا إنه لا يجوز بيعها ويحرم الانتفاع بها احتراماً للآدمي كما إذا طحن سن الآدمي الحنطة أو عظمه لا يباح تناول الخبز المتخذ من دقيقهما لا لكونه نجساً بل تعظيماً له كيلا أجزاء الآدمي. كذا هذا وكذا ذكر في المبسوط والنهاية والمعراج.
Book of Purity. Two narrations: one that it is pure because it is bone, and the other that it is impure because it has life and sensation is perceived by it. As for the pig, its hair, bone, and all its parts are impure, and concession was given in its hair for leather workers due to necessity because nothing else can substitute for it. From Abu Yusuf that he also disliked that for them, and it is not permissible to sell it in all narrations. If its hair falls into a small amount of water, it makes it impure according to Abu Yusuf, while according to Muhammad it does not make it impure. If one prays with it, it is valid according to Muhammad, but not according to Abu Yusuf if it is more than the size of a dirham. They differed on the size of a dirham: some say by weight, some say by area. Mentioned in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj. Al-Siraj al-Hindi mentioned that the statement of Abu Yusuf that it is impure is the apparent transmission, and al-Bada'i' validated it, and al-Ikhtiyar favored it. In al-Tanjis: There is no harm in selling bones of the dead because death does not affect bones, and there is no blood in bones, so they do not become impure; it is permissible to sell them except for selling bones of humans and pigs. In al-Muhit: If a bone of a dead animal has grease and falls into water, it makes it impure. In al-Siraj al-Wahhaj: The hair of a dead animal is only pure if it is shaved or cut; if it is plucked, it is impure; similarly, human hair follows this detail. From Muhammad concerning the impurity of human hair, nail, and bone: two narrations, the correct one being purity. In al-Nihaya: They differed about the tooth: is it bone or the end of a dried sinew? Because bone does not develop in a human after birth; and it is said it is bone. What occurred in al-Dhakhira and others that the teeth of a dog, if dry, are pure, and human teeth are impure, based on the fact that a dog is purified by slaughter and what is purified by it, its bone is pure, unlike the human; this is weak. Indeed, what is explicitly stated in al-Bada'i', al-Kafi, and others is that the human tooth is pure according to the apparent school, and that is correct. Al-Bada'i' explained it by saying there is no blood in it, and the impurity-causing element is blood, and because it is impossible that it be pure from a dog and impure from a human who is honored. However, it is not permissible to sell them, and using them is prohibited out of respect for the human, just as if one grinds a human tooth or bone, it is not permissible to eat the bread made from its flour, not because it is impure, but out of respect, so that human parts are not consumed. This is the case, and it is mentioned in al-Mabsut, al-Nihaya, and al-Mi'raj.
Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This passage is from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqaiq, discussing the purity status of various parts of dead animals (maytah) and humans. It begins by stating two narrations regarding the purity of a specific item (likely a bone or hair): one considers it pure because it is bone, the other impure because it retains life and sensation. The author then focuses on the pig, noting that all its parts are intrinsically impure, though a concession was given for using its hair in leatherwork due to necessity, as no substitute exists. Abu Yusuf disapproved of this concession, and selling pig hair is impermissible in all narrations. A dispute is presented between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad regarding whether pig hair falling into water makes it impure: Abu Yusuf says it does, Muhammad says it does not. Similarly, praying while carrying such hair: Muhammad validates it, Abu Yusuf invalidates if more than a dirham's size. The size of a dirham is debated—whether by weight or area. The author then cites various sources: al-Siraj al-Wahhaj mentions that Abu Yusuf's view is the apparent transmission, while al-Bada'i' and al-Ikhtiyar prefer it. The text then addresses selling dead animal bones: permissible because death does not affect bones and they contain no blood, except for human and pig bones. However, if a bone with grease (dusumah) falls into water, it causes impurity according to al-Muhit. Regarding hair: shaved or cut hair of a dead animal is pure; plucked hair is impure. The same applies to human hair. From Muhammad, there are two narrations on human hair, nails, and bones: the correct one is that they are pure. The passage then delves into the status of human teeth. Some claimed that a dog's teeth are pure (because the dog is purified by slaughter) while human teeth are impure—this is deemed weak. The correct view, supported by al-Bada'i' and al-Kafi, is that human teeth are pure on the apparent school view. The reasoning: there is no blood in teeth; the impurity-causing substance is blood; it is illogical that a dog's tooth be pure and a human's impure since humans are honored. However, selling human teeth is prohibited, and using them is unlawful out of respect, as illustrated by the example of grinding a human tooth or bone into flour—bread from that flour is not eaten, not because it is impure, but to prevent consumption of human parts. This view is affirmed in al-Mabsut, al-Nihaya, and al-Mi'raj.

Key Terms
ميتة (maytah): A dead animal that was not slaughtered Islamically; its meat is impure, but bones and hair may be pure under certain conditions.
نجاسة (najāsah): Impurity that invalidates prayer and requires removal; pig and its parts are intrinsically impure; bones of maytah are not impure per se.
ظاهر المذهب (zāhir al-madhhab): The most authoritative and well-known position within the Hanafi school, derived from the six core books of Imam Muhammad.

Key Takeaway
Human teeth, hair, and bones are pure in the Hanafi school, but they cannot be sold or used out of respect for the human.
