Rulings on Reattaching and Carrying Human Body Parts in Prayer
Intra-Hanafi disputes on impurity of human ear, tooth, skin, and nail

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on carrying or reattaching a separated human tooth or ear during prayer, and explain the intra-Hanafi differences among Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad.

Core Idea
Non-flesh human parts like teeth and nails are pure; carrying them in prayer does not invalidate it.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq delves into the rulings on human body parts that become separated from a living person. The key question is whether such parts are impure and whether carrying them or reattaching them affects the validity of prayer. The Hanafi school distinguishes between parts that contain blood (flesh) and those that do not (teeth, nails, hair), leading to nuanced differences among the early authorities.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Its Ruling
Determine what action is being evaluated: carrying a separated ear or tooth in prayer, or reattaching it. The passage states the ruling is valid (sahih) according to some sources, with disagreement.
Focus on the specific act: carrying one's own separated part, not another's.
2
Locate the Basis for Purity
Understand the principle: parts without blood (like teeth and nails) do not become impure by death or separation; they are pure. This is the foundation for the ruling.
Refer to the maxim 'mā ubīna min al-ḥayy' — parts cut from a living being.
3
Map the Intra-Hanafi Ikhtilaf
Identify the disagreement: Abu Yusuf permits returning the ear to its place; the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) do not. Also, there is a dispute about carrying another's tooth.
Use the Sahibayn as 'the two' — Abu Yusuf and Muhammad.
4
Evaluate the Fatwa Hierarchy
Determine which position is relied upon. Fatawa Qadikhan and al-Khulasa support validity according to the apparent narration (zahir al-riwayah). This is the mufta bihi.
Later sources like al-Tanjis strengthen this view.
5
Apply to Skin and Nails
Extend the principle to human skin and nails: small skin flakes do not spoil water, large skin does; nails (as nerve) do not spoil water. Prayer remains valid if carrying such pure parts.
Skin is flesh, so large amounts affect water; nails are non-flesh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعلى هذا ما ذكر في التنجيس رجل قطعت أذنه أو قلعت سنه فأعاد أذنه إلى مكانها أو سنه الساقط إلى مكانها فصلي أو صلى وأذنه أو سنه في كمه يجزيه لأن ما ليس بلحم لا يحله الموت فلا يتنجس بالموت اه. من بين فرث ودم طاهراً فلا تكون مؤثرة بعد الموت. قوله : (وقالا) (نجس) يعني قالا أنفحة الميتة مطلقاً نجس ولبنها أيضاً نجس لأن تنجس المحل يوجب تنجس ما فيه . قوله: (وتطهر الجامدة بالغسل) قيد بالجامدة لأن المائعة لا تطهر بالغسل عندهما . كذا في شرح المصنف. (أقول) لا حاجة إلى إرداف قولهما لأنه في طرف النفي من قوله طاهر) ولو قال وقالا تظهر الجامدة بالغسل لكان كافياً لاح إلى اشتباه آخر وهو أن المائعة إن كانت مما تنعصر كان ينبغي أن تطهر، وإن كانت مما لا تنعصر فكذا عند أبي يوسف لما سبق من أن غير المنعصر عنده يطهر بالغسل والتجفيف ثلاثاً اهـ. قال لكن ما ذكره في السن ،مسلم، أما الأذن فقد قال في البدائع : ما أبين من الحي من الأجزاء إن كان المبان جزءاً فيه دم كاليد والأذن والأنف ونحوها فهو نجس بالإجماع، وإن لم يكن فيه دم كالشعر والصوف والظفر فهو ظاهر عندنا خلافاً للشافعي ا هـ . لكن في فتاوى قاضيخان والخلاصة : ولو قلع إنسان سنه أو قطع أذنه ثم أعادهما إلى مكانهما أو صلى وسنه أو أذنه في كمه تجوز صلاته في ظاهر الرواية ا هـ. فهذا يقوي ما في التنجيس. وفي السراج الوهاج : وإن قطعت أذنه قال أبو يوسف : لا بأس بأن يعيدها إلى مكانها، وعندهما لا يجوز ا هـ. وبما ذكرناه عن الفتاوى يندفع ما ذكر في بعض الحواشي أنه لو صلى وهو حامل سن غيره أو حامل سن نفسه ولم يضعها في مكانها تفسد صلاته اتفاقاً كما لا يخفى، وكذا ذكر في المعراج أنه لو صلى وهو حامل سن غيره لا يجوز بالاتفاق وفيه من النظر ما علمت. وفي الخلاصة وفتاوى قاضيخان والتنجيس والمحيط : جلد الإنسان إذا وقع في الماء أو قشره إن كان قليلاً مثل ما يتناثر من شقوق الرجل ونحوه لا يفسد الماء، وإن كان كثيراً يعني قدر الظفر يفسد والظفر لا يفسد الماء ا هـ . وعلل له في التنجيس بأن الجلد والقشر من جملة لحم الآدمي والظفر عصب وهذا كله مذهبنا
And upon this is based what was mentioned in al-Tanjis: A man whose ear was cut off or tooth was pulled out, and he returned his ear to its place or his fallen tooth to its place, and then prayed, or he prayed while his ear or tooth was in his sleeve, it suffices him, because what is not flesh is not affected by death, so it does not become impure by death. Between excrement and blood it is pure, so it has no effect after death. His statement: 'And they said: impure.' Meaning they said: The rennet of a dead animal is absolutely impure, and its milk also is impure, because the impurity of the container necessitates the impurity of what is in it. His statement: 'And the solid one is purified by washing.' He restricted to solid because the liquid is not purified by washing according to them. Thus in the commentary of the author. I say: There is no need to append his statement because it is on the side of negation from his statement 'pure'. If he had said 'And they said: the solid becomes pure by washing' it would have been sufficient, but it leads to another confusion, namely that if the liquid is of a type that can be squeezed out, it should become pure; if it is not of a type that can be squeezed out, then likewise according to Abu Yusuf, as previously stated that that which cannot be squeezed out is purified by washing and drying three times. He said: But what he mentioned about the tooth is conceded. As for the ear, al-Bada'i' said: What is cut off from a living being, if the cut part contains blood like the hand, ear, nose, and the like, it is impure by consensus; if it does not contain blood like hair, wool, and nail, it is apparent (purity) according to us, contrary to al-Shafi'i. But in the Fatawa of Qadikhan and al-Khulasa: If a person extracts his tooth or cuts his ear, then returns them to their places, or prays with his tooth or ear in his sleeve, his prayer is valid according to the apparent narration. This strengthens what is in al-Tanjis. In al-Siraj al-Wahhaj: If his ear is cut, Abu Yusuf said: There is no harm in returning it to its place, while according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad?) it is not permissible. By what we mentioned from the Fatawa, what was mentioned in some marginalia is refuted: that if he prays carrying another's tooth or his own tooth and does not place it in its place, his prayer is invalid by agreement, as is not hidden. Similarly, it is mentioned in al-Mi'raj that if he prays carrying another's tooth, it is invalid by agreement, but there is an element of consideration in that as you know. In al-Khulasa, Fatawa Qadikhan, al-Tanjis, and al-Muhit: Human skin, if it falls into water, or its peel, if it is small like what flakes off from cracks of the foot and similar, it does not spoil the water; if it is large, meaning the size of a nail, it spoils it, and the nail does not spoil the water. Al-Tanjis explains that skin and peel are part of human flesh, while the nail is sinew. All this is our school.
Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This passage continues the commentary on Kanz al-Daqaiq, focusing on human body parts separated from a living person. The author begins by affirming a ruling from al-Tanjis: if a person's ear is cut off or tooth pulled out, and he reattaches it or prays with it in his sleeve, his prayer is valid. The reason given is that these parts are not flesh (lahm) and therefore death (of the part) does not render them impure; they remain pure as they are between excrement and blood. The commentator then addresses a statement in the matn: 'And they said: impure' regarding the rennet (anfaha) of a dead animal. He explains that the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) consider the rennet and milk of a dead animal impure because the container (the stomach) is impure and conveys impurity. Then he discusses the matn's phrase 'and the solid one is purified by washing,' noting that only solid (jamidah) rennet is purified by washing, not liquid. He mentions that if the liquid is squeezable, it should become pure; if not, Abu Yusuf holds that it becomes pure by washing and drying three times. The author then returns to the ear/tooth issue, citing al-Bada'i' which distinguishes between parts with blood (like hands, ears, nose) which are impure by consensus, and parts without blood (like hair, wool, nails) which are pure according to the Hanafi school, contrary to Shafi'i. However, in Fatawa Qadikhan and al-Khulasa, the ruling for returning the ear or tooth to its place or carrying them in prayer is valid according to the apparent narration. Abu Yusuf permits returning the ear, but the two (Sahibayn?) do not. The author then refutes some marginalia that claim carrying another's tooth invalidates prayer by agreement, pointing out that the fatwa sources indicate otherwise. Finally, he discusses human skin and nails: a small amount of skin (like flakes) does not spoil water; a larger amount (like the size of a nail) does spoil water, but the nail itself does not spoil water because nail is sinew, not flesh. All this is the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
ما أبين من الحي (mā ubīna min al-ḥayy): A part cut off from a living being; its ruling depends on whether it contains blood or not.
الظفر (al-ẓufr): Fingernail or toenail; classified as nerve (asab) not flesh, so it does not spoil water even if separated.
الأنفحة (al-anfaha): Rennet; the contents of the stomach of an animal used for curdling milk. From a dead animal, it is impure according to the two (Sahibayn); Abu Hanifa considers it pure if solid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Fatawa of Qadikhan, what is the ruling for a person who prays with his own extracted tooth in his sleeve?
A) His prayer is invalid because the tooth is impure.
B) His prayer is valid according to the apparent narration.
C) His prayer is valid only if the tooth is reattached.
D) His prayer is invalid by consensus.

Key Takeaway
Non-flesh human body parts (teeth, nails, hair) are pure and do not invalidate prayer or spoil water, while flesh-containing parts are impure and have practical consequences for worship and purification.

Purity of Hair and Bones of Dead Animals: The Hanafi Proof
Refutation of Shafi'i's proof that all parts of dead animals are impure

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the purity of hair, bones, and skin of a dead animal and explain the evidence (dalil) that supports it.

Core Idea
Death only imparts impurity to what is permeated by life — hair and bones remain pure.

This lesson examines the Hanafi refutation of the Shafi'i position that all parts of a dead animal (maytah) are impure. Based on a fundamental legal principle and explicit hadith from the Prophet (peace be upon him), the Hanafi school holds that only the meat is impure; hair, bones, skin, and wool remain pure. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to distinguish between general and specific textual evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Shafi'i position and its proof
State that al-Shafi'i considers all parts of a dead animal impure, relying on the general term 'carrion' in Quran 5:3.
The Shafi'i argument treats the word 'al-maytah' as including hair, skin, and bones.
2
Identify the Hanafi position
The Hanafi school holds that only the meat is impure; hair, bones, skin, and wool are pure.
This ruling applies to non-consumption uses; meat remains forbidden to eat.
3
Analyze the Hanafi principle
The key principle: during life, all parts are pure; death only imparts impurity to what is permeated by life (flesh). Hair, bones, and wool are not permeated by life in the same way, so death does not render them impure.
This is a qiyas-based extension from the general rule of purity during life.
4
Examine the Hanafi hadith evidence
The hadith of the sheep of Maymuna: 'Only its consumption is forbidden' and 'Only its meat is forbidden, but I permit its skin.' Also, hadith of al-Daraqutni explicitly mentioning skin, hair, and wool.
These hadith specify the general Quranic prohibition, limiting it to consumption and meat.
5
Understand the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: Hanafis permit using hair, bones, and skin from dead animals for non-consumption purposes; Shafi'is consider them impure and forbid use except in dire necessity.
This affects rulings on leather, tools, and clothing made from dead animals.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال الشافعي: الكل نجس إلا شعر الآدمي لقوله تعالى حرمت عليكم الميتة ﴾ [المائدة : ٣] وهو عام للشعر وغيره. ولنا أن المعهود فيها حالة الحياة الطهارة وإنما يؤثر الموت النجاسة فيما يحله ولا تحلها الحياة فلا يحلها الموت، وإذا لم يحلها وجب الحكم ببقاء الوصف الشرعي المعهود لعدم المزيل وفي السنة أيضاً ما يدل عليه وهو قوله عليه السلام في شاة مولاة ميمونة حين مرّ بها ميتة «إنما حرم أكلها» في الصحيحين. وفي لفظ إنما حرم عليكم لحمها ورخص لكم في مسكها». وفي الباب حديث الدارقطني إنما حرم رسول الله له من الميتة لحمها فأما الجلد والشعر والصوف فلا بأس وهو وإن أعله بتضعيف عبد الجبار بن مسلم فقد ذكره ابن حبان في الثقاة فهو لا ينزل عن درجة الحسن، وأخرجه الدارقطني من طريق أخرى وضعفها، ومن طريق أخرى بمعناه ضعيفة. وأخرج البيهقي أنه عليه السلام كان يتمشط بمشط من عاج وضعفه، فهذه عدة أحاديث لو كانت ضعيفة حسن المتن فكيف ومنها ما لا ينزل عن الحسن وله الشاهد الأول؟ ما ابن أمير حاج بعد أن تكلم على المسألة : تنبيه وقد عرفت من هذا أن نفس الوعاء الذي سيصير كرشاً نجس ،بالاتفاق، وأن المراد بالإطلاق بكون المنفحة طاهرة عنده متنجسة عندهما إذا كانت مائعة هو اشتمل عليه الوعاء المذكور فقط . ثم هذا كله إذا كانت المنفحة من شاة ميتة كما فسره المصنف، أما إذا كانت من ذكية فهي طاهرة مطلقاً بالإجماع. اهـ. حلية. قوله : (أما الإذن فقد قال في البدائع الخ) يمكن التوفيق بينهما بأن يكون ما في البدائع بالنظر إلى غير المقطوع منه بدليل قول المؤلف في الأشباه كما نقله الشيخ علاء الدين الحصكفي المنفصل من الحي كميتته إلا في حق صاحبه فطاهر وإن كثر
Al-Shafi'i said: Everything is impure except human hair, because Allah said 'Forbidden to you is carrion' (5:3), which is general for hair and other parts. Our proof is that the established rule during life is purity, and death only affects impurity regarding what is permeated by life, and what life does not permeate, death does not permeate. If it does not permeate it, it is necessary to rule upon the continuation of the known legal description due to the absence of a remover. In the Sunnah also there is evidence for this: his statement (peace be upon him) regarding the sheep of the client of Maymuna when it passed by as a corpse: 'Its consumption is only forbidden' in the two Sahihs. In another wording: 'Only its meat is forbidden to you, and I have permitted its skin.' In that chapter is the hadith of al-Daraqutni: 'The Messenger of Allah only forbade from carrion its meat; as for the skin, hair, and wool, there is no harm.' Although he declared it weak due to the tadh'if of Abd al-Jabbar ibn Muslim, Ibn Hibban mentioned him among the trustworthy, so it does not fall below the level of hasan. Al-Daraqutni also narrated it from another chain and declared it weak, and from another chain with its meaning, also weak. Al-Bayhaqi narrated that the Prophet used to comb with a comb of ivory and declared it weak. These several hadith, even if weak, have a hasan text; how then, and among them are those that do not fall below hasan and have a witness? The author (Ibn Amir Hajj) said after discussing the issue: A note: You have known from this that the container itself that will become a stomach is impure by agreement, and that what is meant by the absolute statement that rennet is pure according to him (Abu Hanifa) and impure according to them (the two) when liquid is only what the container contains. Then all this is if the rennet is from a dead sheep as the author explained; if it is from a properly slaughtered animal, it is absolutely pure by consensus. End. al-Hilya. His statement: 'As for the ear, al-Bada'i' said...' It is possible to reconcile between them by saying that what is in al-Bada'i' is considering the part not from its owner, as evidenced by the author's statement in al-Ashbah as quoted by al-Haskafi: 'What separates from a living being is like its dead body except regarding its owner, so it is pure even if abundant.'
Source pages p. 192

Commentary
This passage presents the Hanafi response to the Shafi'i position that all parts of a dead animal (maytah) are impure. The Shafi'i argument relies on the general term 'carrion' in Quran 5:3, which they apply to all parts including hair and skin. The Hanafi proof is based on a principle: during life, these parts are pure; death only imparts impurity to what is permeated by life (i.e., what has a soul). Hair, bones, and wool are not permeated by life in the same way as flesh, so death does not render them impure. The Quranic prohibition of carrion refers to consumption of meat, not to all uses. This is supported by authentic hadith: in the story of the sheep of the client of Maymuna, the Prophet said 'Only its consumption is forbidden' and permitted its skin. Another hadith from al-Daraqutni explicitly states that only meat is forbidden, while skin, hair, and wool are allowed. Although the chain has been criticized, the text is supported by other chains and a witness (shahid). The author also mentions a hadith from al-Bayhaqi about a comb of ivory, though it is weak. The commentator then adds a note from Ibn Amir Hajj clarifying the rennet issue: the container (stomach) of a dead sheep is impure by agreement, but the rennet itself is pure according to Abu Hanifa if solid, and impure according to the two if liquid. If the rennet comes from a properly slaughtered animal, it is pure by consensus. Finally, the commentator reconciles an apparent contradiction regarding the ear: al-Bada'i' says a cut-off part with blood (like ear) is impure, while other sources say it is pure when reattached by its owner. The reconciliation is that al-Bada'i' refers to a part belonging to another person, while for the owner it remains pure, as stated in al-Ashbah: a part separated from a living being is like its dead body except in relation to its owner—it is pure for him.

Key Terms
الميتة (al-maytah): A dead animal not slaughtered in the prescribed manner; its meat is forbidden for consumption, but other parts may be pure.
المنفحة (al-minfaha): Rennet: the contents of the stomach of a ruminant, used to curdle milk. According to Abu Hanifa, it is pure if solid; according to the Sahibayn, it is impure if from a dead animal.
شاهد (shāhid): A corroborating hadith that supports the meaning of another hadith, strengthening its reliability.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is the primary Hanafi evidence that the skin and hair of a dead animal are pure?
A) The general meaning of Quran 5:3 includes skin and hair.
B) The principle that death only imparts impurity to what is permeated by life, and hair and skin are not.
C) The hadith that the Prophet used a comb of ivory.
D) The consensus of all schools.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school establishes that only the meat of a dead animal is impure; hair, bones, and skin remain pure based on explicit hadith and the legal principle of what life permeates.

Rulings on Detached Body Parts and Human Skin/Nails in Water
Defining impurity of detached human parts and effect of skin/nails on water

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on detached body parts based on presence or absence of blood, and evaluate the effect of human skin and nails on water purity.

Core Idea
Detached parts with blood are impure; without blood, pure. Skin in water spoils if abundant; nails never spoil.

This lesson from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the Hanafi position on the ritual purity of detached human body parts. It distinguishes between parts containing blood, which are najis by consensus, and parts without blood, which remain tahir. It also addresses the effect of human skin and nails on water, with a threshold ruling based on quantity. The lesson further clarifies the validity of prayer when carrying a detached part and notes an intra-Hanafi difference on reattaching a cut ear.

Guided Path
1
Categorize the detached part
Classify the part based on whether it contains blood (e.g., hand, ear) or not (e.g., hair, nail). The ruling (najis vs tahir) follows directly from the text of al-Bada'i'.
This is the primary distinction in the passage.
2
Apply the ruling on prayer with detached part
If a person prays while carrying a detached part (e.g., tooth in sleeve), the prayer is valid according to Zahir al-Riwaya. Understand the evidence from Fatawa Qadikhan and al-Khulasa.
This addresses a practical scenario and refutes opposing claims.
3
Assess skin/nail in water
Determine the amount (qalil vs kathir) of skin or scurf falling into water. Small amounts do not spoil the water; large amounts (size of a nail) do spoil. The nail itself never spoils water because it is nerve, not flesh.
This applies to the purity of water for wudu and ghusl.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لكن ما ذكره في السن ،مسلم، أما الأذن فقد قال في البدائع : ما أبين من الحي من الأجزاء إن كان المبان جزءاً فيه دم كاليد والأذن والأنف ونحوها فهو نجس بالإجماع، وإن لم يكن فيه دم كالشعر والصوف والظفر فهو ظاهر عندنا خلافاً للشافعي ا هـ . لكن في فتاوى قاضيخان والخلاصة : ولو قلع إنسان سنه أو قطع أذنه ثم أعادهما إلى مكانهما أو صلى وسنه أو أذنه في كمه تجوز صلاته في ظاهر الرواية ا هـ. فهذا يقوي ما في التنجيس. وفي السراج الوهاج : وإن قطعت أذنه قال أبو يوسف : لا بأس بأن يعيدها إلى مكانها، وعندهما لا يجوز ا هـ. وبما ذكرناه عن الفتاوى يندفع ما ذكر في بعض الحواشي أنه لو صلى وهو حامل سن غيره أو حامل سن نفسه ولم يضعها في مكانها تفسد صلاته اتفاقاً كما لا يخفى، وكذا ذكر في المعراج أنه لو صلى وهو حامل سن غيره لا يجوز بالاتفاق وفيه من النظر ما علمت. وفي الخلاصة وفتاوى قاضيخان والتنجيس والمحيط : جلد الإنسان إذا وقع في الماء أو قشره إن كان قليلاً مثل ما يتناثر من شقوق الرجل ونحوه لا يفسد الماء، وإن كان كثيراً يعني قدر الظفر يفسد والظفر لا يفسد الماء ا هـ . وعلل له في التنجيس بأن الجلد والقشر من جملة لحم الآدمي والظفر عصب وهذا كله مذهبنا
But what he mentioned about the tooth: it is accepted. As for the ear, it was said in al-Bada'i': Whatever is detached from a living being of parts — if the detached part contains blood like the hand, ear, nose, etc., it is najis by consensus. And if it does not contain blood, like hair, wool, and nail, then it is tahir in our madhhab, contrary to al-Shafi'i. However, in Fatawa Qadikhan and al-Khulasa: If a person knocks out his tooth or cuts his ear, then reattaches them in their places, or prays while his tooth or ear is in his sleeve, his prayer is valid according to the apparent narration (zahir al-riwaya). This supports what is in al-Tanjis. In al-Siraj al-Wahhaj: If his ear is cut off, Abu Yusuf said: There is no harm in reattaching it to its place. According to the two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad), it is not permissible. By what we mentioned from the fatawa, the objection mentioned in some glosses is refuted, that if he prays while carrying another's tooth or his own tooth without placing it in its place, his prayer is invalid by agreement, as is not hidden. Similarly, it was mentioned in al-Mi'raj that if he prays while carrying another's tooth, it is not permissible by agreement, and there is in this the consideration that you have known. In al-Khulasa, Fatawa Qadikhan, al-Tanjis, and al-Muhit: Human skin, if it falls into water, or its scurf — if it is a small amount like what flakes from cracks of feet and the like, it does not spoil the water. And if it is much, meaning the size of a nail, it spoils the water. And the nail itself does not spoil the water. It is explained in al-Tanjis that skin and scurf are part of human flesh, and the nail is nerve. And all this is our madhhab.
Source pages p. 192

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, a commentary on Kanz al-Daqaiq. It addresses the impurity (najasa) of detached human body parts and the effect of human skin and nails on water purity. The author begins by citing al-Bada'i' that parts containing blood (hand, ear, nose) are najis by consensus (ijma), while parts without blood (hair, wool, nail) are tahir in the Hanafi school, contrary to al-Shafi'i. He then presents conflicting rulings from later sources: Fatawa Qadikhan and al-Khulasa state that if a person prays with his detached tooth or ear in his sleeve (or reattached), the prayer is valid according to Zahir al-Riwaya (the six core books of Imam Muhammad). This supports the view in al-Tanjis (a Hanafi work on purity). However, al-Siraj al-Wahhaj reports an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Yusuf permits reattaching a cut ear, while Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (the two, i.e., al-Shaybani and Abu Hanifa) forbid it. The author notes that the fatawa (fatwa collections) resolve the apparent conflict. Finally, from al-Khulasa, Fatawa Qadikhan, al-Tanjis, and al-Muhit, a ruling on human skin and nails in water is given: a small amount (like flakes from cracked feet) does not spoil water; a large amount (size of a nail) does spoil; but the nail itself does not spoil water. The reason given in al-Tanjis is that skin and scurf are flesh, while the nail is nerve (tendon). This encapsulates the Hanafi position.

Key Terms
سن (sinn): Tooth; a detached body part without blood, thus tahir in Hanafi school.
ظفر (zifr): Fingernail; classified as nerve (asab) and does not spoil water when detached.
عصب (asab): Nerve/tendon; the substance of nail, considered separate from flesh.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the ruling on a detached human ear that contains blood?
A) It is tahir (pure).
B) It is najis (impure) by consensus.
C) It is makruh (disliked).
D) The ruling depends on the amount.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, detached body parts are najis if they contain blood and tahir if they do not; skin in water spoils it if abundant, but nails never spoil water.

Ruling on Rennet (Minfaha) from Dead and Slaughtered Animals
Parsing the ruling on rennet purity with an intra-Hanafi dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on rennet from dead and slaughtered animals and identify the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding liquid rennet from a dead animal.

Core Idea
Rennet from a dead animal is pure (Abu Hanifa) but impure (Sahibayn) if liquid — the stomach container is always impure.

Rennet, the enzyme used to curdle milk for cheese, is extracted from the stomach of ruminants. In Hanafi fiqh, its purity depends on whether the animal was slaughtered Islamically or found dead. This passage from al-Hilya clarifies the ruling, highlighting an important difference between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two disciples regarding liquid rennet from a dead animal.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two objects
The passage discusses two distinct items: the container (stomach/kirsh) and the rennet (minfaha) it holds. Their rulings differ.
Keep them separate: container vs content.
2
Ruling on the container
The stomach itself from a dead animal is najis by agreement (ittifaq) of all three imams.
This is a fixed ruling.
3
Distinguish the source animal
The ruling for the rennet depends on whether the sheep was slaughtered (zakiyya) or died naturally (mayta).
Slaughtered = halal; dead = not slaughtered.
4
Ruling for slaughtered animal
For a slaughtered sheep, the rennet is absolutely tahir (mutlaqan) by consensus (ijma).
No dispute here.
5
Ruling for dead animal and the ikhtilaf
If the sheep is dead, Abu Hanifa rules the rennet tahir, but the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) rule it mutanajjis if it is liquid. The disagreement is only about the liquid state.
Solid rennet is likely tahir by agreement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما ابن أمير حاج بعد أن تكلم على المسألة : تنبيه وقد عرفت من هذا أن نفس الوعاء الذي سيصير كرشاً نجس ،بالاتفاق، وأن المراد بالإطلاق بكون المنفحة طاهرة عنده متنجسة عندهما إذا كانت مائعة هو . اشتمل عليه الوعاء المذكور فقط . ثم هذا كله إذا كانت المنفحة من شاة ميتة كما فسره المصنف، أما إذا كانت من ذكية فهي طاهرة مطلقاً بالإجماع. اهـ. حلية.
Ibn Amir Hajj after discussing the issue: A note. You have already known from this that the container itself that will become the stomach (krish) is najis by agreement. And that what is meant by the absolute statement that the rennet is tahir according to him (Abu Hanifa) and mutanajjis (impure due to contamination) according to them (the two, i.e., Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) if it is liquid is only what the mentioned container contains. Then all this is if the rennet is from a dead sheep, as the author explained. However, if it is from a slaughtered (zakiyya) sheep, then it is absolutely tahir by consensus. End quote from al-Hilya.
Source pages p. 192

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement within the commentary, addressing the rennet (minfaha) taken from the stomach of a sheep. The note clarifies that the stomach container itself is najis (impure) by agreement, regardless. The rennet (the liquid enzyme) within: if it is liquid, according to Imam Abu Hanifa it is tahir (pure), while according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) it is mutanajjis (ritually impure due to contamination). This ikhtilaf applies only to the rennet from a dead sheep (i.e., not slaughtered Islamically). If the sheep is slaughtered (zakiyya), then by consensus (ijma) the rennet is absolutely tahir. The source is al-Hilya (a Hanafi commentary). This ruling demonstrates how the Hanafi school applies the principle of original purity (istishab) and the effect of istihsan versus qiyas in matters of impurity.

Key Terms
منفحة (minfaha): Rennet; the enzyme from the stomach used to curdle milk for cheese. The text discusses its purity when taken from a dead or slaughtered sheep.
كرش (kirsh): Stomach (abomasum) of a ruminant; the container that holds the rennet; ruled najis by agreement when from a dead animal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on rennet from a dead sheep?
A) It is tahir by consensus.
B) It is najis by consensus.
C) It is tahir according to Abu Hanifa and mutanajjis according to the two if liquid.
D) It is mutanajjis according to all.

Key Takeaway
The purity of rennet from a dead sheep is disputed within the Hanafi school: Abu Hanifa holds it pure, while his two disciples hold it impure if liquid; from a slaughtered sheep, it is pure by consensus.

Taharah — Purification

Inter-Madhhab Dispute on the Purity of Hair, Bone, and Nerve from Dead Animals
Presenting Shafi'i argument and Hanafi response with proofs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the purity of hair, bone, and nerve from dead animals, explain the Hanafi proofs from Quran, hadith, and rational arguments, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
Life does not inhabit hair, bone, or nerve, so death does not transfer impurity.

This lesson examines a classical dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools over whether parts of a dead animal that lack life (hair, bone, nerve) are impure. The Shafi'i school holds that everything from a dead animal is najis based on the general verse of Quran 5:3. The Hanafi school argues that impurity only applies to parts that contained life during the animal's life; these parts never had life, so they remain pure. Understanding this dispute reveals how each school approaches the interpretation of general texts and the role of rational analysis (talil) in fiqh.

Guided Path
1
State the Shafi'i position and its proof
Identify that the Shafi'i school says all parts of a dead animal are najis, citing Quran 5:3 'Forbidden to you are dead animals' as a general command covering everything.
Shafi'i makes exception only for human hair based on another hadith.
2
Identify the Hanafi position and its core rationale
The Hanafi position is that hair, bone, and nerve are tahir because they are not inhabited by life; death only transfers impurity to what had life.
The default state is purity; death can only change it if life was present.
3
Examine the Hanafi hadith evidence
The hadith of the sheep of Maymunah specifies 'only its consumption is forbidden', implying permissibility of other uses and thus purity.
The hadith is sound (muttafaq alayh) and provides specification (takhsis) of the general Quranic verse.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response to the Quran verse
The general verse is specified by the hadith and by the rational principle that impurity originates from blood and moistures, which are absent in these parts.
This response addresses the Shafi'i argument that the verse is general and unqualified.
5
Note the intra-Hanafi difference on methodology and the final relied-upon view
Two methods are presented: parts never had life vs impurity due to blood. The second method is preferred for nerve because nerve has sensation, and both methods lead to tahir.
The mufta bihi is that hair, bone, and nerve are tahir.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال الشافعي: الكل نجس إلا شعر الآدمي لقوله تعالى حرمت عليكم الميتة ﴾ [المائدة : ٣] وهو عام للشعر وغيره. ولنا أن المعهود فيها حالة الحياة الطهارة وإنما يؤثر الموت النجاسة فيما يحله ولا تحلها الحياة فلا يحلها الموت، وإذا لم يحلها وجب الحكم ببقاء الوصف الشرعي المعهود لعدم المزيل وفي السنة أيضاً ما يدل عليه وهو قوله عليه السلام في شاة مولاة ميمونة حين مرّ بها ميتة «إنما حرم أكلها» في الصحيحين. وفي لفظ إنما حرم عليكم لحمها ورخص لكم في مسكها». وفي الباب حديث الدارقطني إنما حرم رسول الله له من الميتة لحمها فأما الجلد والشعر والصوف فلا بأس وهو وإن أعله بتضعيف عبد الجبار بن مسلم فقد ذكره ابن حبان في الثقاة فهو لا ينزل عن درجة الحسن، وأخرجه الدارقطني من طريق أخرى وضعفها، ومن طريق أخرى بمعناه ضعيفة. وأخرج البيهقي أنه عليه السلام كان يتمشط بمشط من عاج وضعفه، فهذه عدة أحاديث لو كانت ضعيفة حسن المتن فكيف ومنها ما لا ينزل عن الحسن وله الشاهد الأول؟ ثم قوله : (أما الإذن فقد قال في البدائع الخ) يمكن التوفيق بينهما بأن يكون ما في البدائع بالنظر إلى غير المقطوع منه بدليل قول المؤلف في الأشباه كما نقله الشيخ علاء الدين الحصكفي المنفصل من الحي كميتته إلا في حق صاحبه فطاهر وإن كثر كذا في فتح القدير مختصراً. وفي البدائع : لأصحابنا طريقان أحدهما أن هذه الأشياء ليست بميتة لأن الميتة من الحيوانات في عرف الشرع اسم لما زالت حياته لا أحد بصنع من العباد أو بصنع غير مشروع ولا حياة في هذه الأشياء فلا تكون ميتة والثاني أن نجاسة الميتات ليست لأعيانها بل لما فيها من الدماء السائلة والرطوبات النجسة ولم توجد في هذه الأجزاء ا هـ . وقد اقتصر في الهداية على الطريقة الأولى، وفي غاية البيان على الثانية، ولا يخفى أن الطريقة المذكورة في الهداية لا تجري في العصب لأن فيه حياة لما فيه من الحركة، ألا ترى أنه يتألم الحي بقطعه بخلاف العظم فإن قطع قرن البقرة لا يؤلمها فدل أنه ليس في العظم حياة. كذا في النهاية. ولهذا كان فيه روايتان؛ فالأولى هي الطريقة الثانية وعليها لا يحتاج إلى الجواب عن قوله تعالى قال من يحيى العظام وهي رميم قل يحييها الذي أنشأها أول مرة ﴾ [يس: ٧٩] فإن هذه الأشياء من الميتات إلا أن نجاسة الميتات إنما هي لما فيها من الدماء والرطوبات والعصب صقيل لا يتصور فيه ذلك، وكذا في العظم والشعر. وأما الجواب عن الطريقة الأولى فمن ثلاثة أوجه الأول ما ذكره في الكشاف بقوله: ولقد استشهد بهذ الآية من يثبت الحياة في العظام ويقول إن عظام الموتى نجسة لأن الموت يؤثر فيها من قبل أن الحياة تحلها، وأما أصحاب أبي حنيفة رحمهم الله فهي عندهم طاهرة وكذلك الشعر والعصب ويزعمون أن الحياة لا تحلها فلا يؤثر فيها الموت ويقولون المراد بإحياء العظام في الآية ردها إلى ما كانت عليه غضة رطبة في بدن حي حساس اهـ. ولا يتوهم أن صاحب الكشاف لم يرتض ما ذكره عن الحنفية بدليل قوله يزعمون لأن زعم مطية الكذب كما قيل لأنا لا نسلم أن زعم خاص في الباطل بل يستعمل تارة فيه وتارة في الحق. فمن الأول قوله تعالى زعم الذين كفروا أن لن يبعثوا [التغابن : ۷] ومن الثاني قوله من حديث مسلم زعم رسولك أن الله افترض علينا خمس صلوات صرح به النووي في شرح مسلم وأطال الكلام فيه. والثاني أن المراد بالعظام النفوس كما في معراج الدراية وحينئذ يعود الضمير في قوله وهي رميم إلى العظام الحقيقية على طريقة الاستخدام لأن من أقسامه كما عرف في علم البديع أن يراد بلفظ له معنيان أحدهما ثم يؤتي بعده بضمير يعود في اللفظ عليه وفي المعنى على معناه الآخر كقول معاوية بن أبي ملك : إذا نزل السماء بأرض قوم رعيناه وإن كانوا غضابا فإنه أراد بالسماء المطر وأراد بالضمير في عيناه النبات والنبات أحد معنى السماء لأنه مجاز عنه باعتبار أن المطر سببه وسوغ له عود الضمير إلى النبات وإن لم يكن تقدم له ذكر فتأمل. وفي شرح العلامة المقدسي قلت والجواب عن الإشكال أن إعادة الأذن وثباتها إنما يكون غالباً بعود الحياة إليها فلا يصدق أنها مما أبين من الحي لأنها بعود الحياة إليها صارت كأنها لم تبن،
Al-Shafi'i said: Everything is najis except human hair, due to His statement: 'Forbidden to you are dead animals' (5:3), which is general and includes hair and other things. Our (Hanafi) proof is that the customary state during life is purity; death only affects impurity on what life can enter and inhabit; life does not enter these parts, so death does not enter them. Since it does not enter, we must rule that the original Shari'ah attribute (purity) remains due to the absence of what removes it. In the Sunnah also there is evidence for it: his statement (peace be upon him) concerning the sheep of the freed slave of Maymunah when it passed by dead: 'Only its consumption is forbidden' – in the two Sahihs. In one wording: 'Only its meat is forbidden to you, and its skin is permitted to you.' In this chapter is the hadith of al-Daraqutni: 'The Messenger of Allah only forbade from the dead animal its meat; as for the skin, hair, and wool, there is no harm.' Even though al-Daraqutni declared it weak due to 'Abd al-Jabbar ibn Muslim, but Ibn Hibban mentioned him among the trustworthy, so it does not fall below the level of hasan. Al-Daraqutni also transmitted it via another chain and declared it weak, and via another chain with its meaning which is weak. Al-Bayhaqi transmitted that he (peace be upon him) used to comb his hair with an ivory comb and declared it weak. These are several hadith; even if they were weak, they strengthen the matn. How could they, when some of them do not fall below hasan? And there is the first witness? Then his statement: 'As for the ear, it was said in al-Bada'i' etc.' It can be reconciled between them by saying that what is in al-Bada'i' is with regard to what is not cut off from him, as indicated by the author's statement in al-Ashbah as reported by Shaykh 'Ala' al-Din al-Haskafi: 'What is separated from the living is like its dead except for its owner; it is tahir even if numerous.' This is in Fath al-Qadir briefly. In al-Bada'i': Our scholars have two methods. First: These things are not dead animals because 'dead animal' in the custom of Shari'ah is a name for what has lost its life through no human action or through unlawful action; but there is no life in these things, so they are not dead animals. Second: The impurity of dead animals is not due to their essences but due to what is in them of flowing blood and impure moistures; these parts have no such thing. Al-Hidayah restricted itself to the first method, while Ghayat al-Bayan restricted to the second. It is not hidden that the method mentioned in al-Hidayah does not apply to nerve because there is life in it due to movement; don't you see that the living feels pain when it is cut, unlike bone? Cutting a cow's horn does not pain it, indicating that there is no life in bone. This is in al-Nihayah. For this reason, there are two reports about it. The first (i.e., the second method) is the one that does not need an answer to His statement: 'Who will revive the bones when they are decayed? Say: He will revive them who created them the first time' (36:79). For these things are from dead animals, but the impurity of dead animals is only due to blood and moistures; nerve is solid and such is not conceivable in it; similarly for bone and hair. The answer to the first method comes from three aspects. First: what al-Zamakhshari mentioned in al-Kashshaf: 'Some have cited this verse to affirm life in bones and say that the bones of the dead are najis because death affects them since life enters them. But the followers of Abu Hanifa hold that they are tahir, similarly hair and nerve; they claim that life does not enter them, so death does not affect them. They say the meaning of reviving bones in the verse is restoring them to what they were: fresh and moist in a sensitive, living body.' Do not imagine that the author of al-Kashshaf did not approve what he mentioned from the Hanafis because he said 'they claim (yaz'umuna)', for claim is a vehicle of lying as is said. We do not accept that 'claim' is specific to falsehood; rather it is used sometimes for falsehood and sometimes for truth. From the first: His statement: 'Those who disbelieve claim (zama'a) that they will never be resurrected' (64:7). From the second: what is in Muslim's hadith: 'Your Messenger claimed (zama'a) that Allah has obligated five prayers upon us' – al-Nawawi clarified this in his commentary on Muslim and spoke at length. Second: What is meant by bones is souls, as in Mi'raj al-Dirayah. In that case, the pronoun in 'they are decayed' (wa hiya ramim) refers to real bones via the method of usage (istikhdam), because from its divisions – as is known in the science of badi' – that a word with two meanings is intended, then a pronoun is brought that refers back to the word but in meaning refers to its other meaning. Like the statement of Mu'awiyah ibn Abi Malik: 'When the sky (mountain) alights upon a people's land, we pasture upon it even if they are angry.' He meant by 'sky' rain, and by the pronoun in 'we pasture upon it' he meant plants, and plants are one meaning of sky because it is metaphorical considering rain is its cause. This allows the pronoun to refer to plants even though it was not mentioned previously. So ponder. In the commentary of 'Allamah al-Maqdisi: I said: The answer to the problem is that reattaching the ear and its stability usually happen by life returning to it, so it is not considered as something detached from the living because by life returning to it, it becomes as if it was never detached.
Source pages p. 192

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools regarding the impurity (najasa) of hair, bone, and nerve from dead animals. The Shafi'i position is that everything from a dead animal is najis except human hair (due to a hadith): they cite the general verse of the Quran 5:3 forbidding dead animals. The Hanafi response presents their proof: the original state during life is purity; death only brings impurity to that which life can enter and inhabit (lahu hayat). Since hair, bone, and nerve do not have life flowing through them (they are not susceptible to life), death does not affect them, and the original ruling of purity remains. The Sunnah evidence is the hadith of the sheep of Maymunah in which the Prophet said only consumption is forbidden, and skin and hair are permitted. The author then discusses the authenticity of supporting hadith from al-Daraqutni and others, concluding they are at least hasan. Then the focus shifts to a dispute within the Hanafi school (intra-Hanafi) regarding the rationale: two methods are given in al-Bada'i'. The first method: these parts are not 'dead animals' because they never had life; the second: impurity is attached to blood and moistures, which are absent. Al-Hidayah chooses the first, Ghayat al-Bayan the second. The second method is preferred for nerve and bone because nerve has life (motion/pain), so the first method fails. A detailed answer is given to those who use Quran 36:79 to argue that bones have life and thus become najis upon death. The author provides three responses: (1) Al-Zamakhshari's explanation that Hanafis interpret 'revival of bones' as restoration to freshness, not literal life; (2) the word 'bones' in the verse may refer to souls (nufus), not literal bones; (3) regarding the ear reattachment, it is not considered detached if life returns to it. Overall, the passage firmly establishes the Hanafi ruling of tahara for hair, bone, and nerve from dead animals.

Key Terms
ميتة (maytah): Dead animal that has not been slaughtered Islamically; its meat is forbidden, but parts without life-breath may be tahir.
عظم (azm): Bone; considered to have no life, thus not affected by death; tahir in Hanafi school.
شعر (sha'r): Hair; does not have life-breath, so death does not make it najis.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary Hanafi proof that hair from a dead animal is tahir?
A) The command in the Quran to eat only permissible foods.
B) The principle that life does not enter hair, so death does not affect it.
C) The analogy (qiyas) with water.
D) The consensus of the companions.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes between parts of a dead animal that contained life and those that did not; the latter remain pure because impurity is transferred only through life.

Taharah — Purification
intermediate
8 min
The Hanafi Ruling on Purity of Bones, Hair, and Sinew from Dead Animals
Hanafi analysis of purity of bones/hair/sinew from dead animals

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that bones, hair, and sinew from dead animals are pure, explain the two Hanafi methodological approaches, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument using Quranic and hadith proofs.

Core Idea
Bones, hair, and sinew from dead animals are pure – impurity is due to blood and moisture, not the parts themselves.

This lesson examines a key Hanafi ruling on taharah: the purity of animal parts that never had flowing blood or real life. The text from al-Bahr al-Raiq presents two approaches and defends the position against the Shafi'i school. You will learn the proofs from Quran and hadith and the logical reasoning behind the Hanafi view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine which animal parts are being discussed (bones, sinew, hair, wool, fur) and state the Hanafi ruling: they are pure (tahir), not najis.
2
Locate the dalil
Find the Quranic evidence cited: Surah al-Nahl 16:80 (general benefit from wool, fur, hair) and the hadith of Mawla Maimunah (using hair of a dead goat).
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Explain how the general permission in 16:80 indicates purity unless there is a specific prohibition. Also, the original state of these parts is pure by consensus before death.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
The Shafi'i school argues that all parts of a dead animal are impure based on Surah al-Ma'idah 5:3 and because bones/hair have growth/life.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The Hanafi response: the prohibition in 5:3 is for eating, not benefit; parts without blood or life are unaffected by death; growth does not imply real life as in plants.

Source Text

كتاب الطهارة
كذا: الآية على في فتح القدير مختصراً. وفي البدائع : لأصحابنا طريقان أحدهما أن هذه الأشياء ليست بميتة لأن الميتة من الحيوانات في عرف الشرع اسم لما زالت حياته لا أحد بصنع من العباد أو بصنع غير مشروع ولا حياة في هذه الأشياء فلا تكون ميتة والثاني أن نجاسة الميتات ليست لأعيانها بل لما فيها من الدماء السائلة والرطوبات النجسة ولم توجد في هذه الأجزاء ا هـ . وقد اقتصر في الهداية على الطريقة الأولى، وفي غاية البيان على الثانية، ولا يخفى أن الطريقة المذكورة في الهداية لا تجري في العصب لأن فيه حياة لما فيه من الحركة، ألا ترى أنه يتألم الحي بقطعه بخلاف العظم فإن قطع قرن البقرة لا يؤلمها فدل أنه ليس في العظم حياة. كذا في النهاية. ولهذا كان فيه روايتان؛ فالأولى هي الطريقة الثانية وعليها لا يحتاج إلى الجواب عن قوله تعالى قال من يحيى العظام وهي رميم قل يحييها الذي أنشأها أول مرة ﴾ [يس: ٧٩] فإن هذه الأشياء من الميتات إلا أن نجاسة الميتات إنما هي لما فيها من الدماء والرطوبات والعصب صقيل لا يتصور فيه ذلك، وكذا في العظم والشعر. وأما الجواب عن الطريقة الأولى فمن ثلاثة أوجه الأول ما ذكره في الكشاف بقوله: ولقد استشهد بهذ الآية من يثبت الحياة في العظام ويقول إن عظام الموتى نجسة لأن الموت يؤثر فيها من قبل أن الحياة تحلها، وأما أصحاب أبي حنيفة رحمهم الله فهي عندهم طاهرة وكذلك الشعر والعصب ويزعمون أن الحياة لا تحلها فلا يؤثر فيها الموت ويقولون المراد بإحياء العظام في الآية ردها إلى ما كانت عليه غضة رطبة في بدن حي حساس اهـ. ولا يتوهم أن صاحب الكشاف لم يرتض ما ذكره عن الحنفية بدليل قوله يزعمون لأن زعم مطية الكذب كما قيل لأنا لا نسلم أن زعم خاص في الباطل بل يستعمل تارة فيه وتارة في الحق. فمن الأول قوله تعالى زعم الذين كفروا أن لن يبعثوا [التغابن : ۷] ومن الثاني قوله من حديث مسلم زعم رسولك أن الله افترض علينا خمس صلوات صرح به النووي في شرح مسلم وأطال الكلام فيه. والثاني أن المراد بالعظام النفوس كما في معراج الدراية وحينئذ يعود الضمير في قوله وهي رميم إلى العظام الحقيقية على طريقة الاستخدام لأن من أقسامه كما عرف في علم البديع أن يراد بلفظ له معنيان أحدهما ثم يؤتي بعده بضمير يعود في اللفظ عليه وفي المعنى على معناه الآخر كقول معاوية بن أبي ملك :
إذا نزل السماء بأرض قوم رعيناه وإن كانوا غضابا
فإنه أراد بالسماء المطر وأراد بالضمير في عيناه النبات والنبات أحد معنى السماء لأنه مجاز عنه باعتبار أن المطر سببه وسوغ له عود الضمير إلى النبات وإن لم يكن تقدم له ذكر فتأمل. وفي شرح العلامة المقدسي قلت والجواب عن الإشكال أن إعادة الأذن وثباتها إنما يكون غالباً بعود الحياة إليها فلا يصدق أنها مما أبين من الحي لأنها بعود الحياة إليها صارت كأنها لم تبن، البحر الرائق/ ج ١/ م ١٣
استعداد لتقدم ذكر سببه وهو السماء التي أريد بها المطر فكذلك ما نحن فيه، فإن العظام له معنيان : أحدهما مراد وهو النفوس مجازاً من إطلاق البعض وإرادة الكل، والمعنى الآخر وهو العظام الحقيقية غير مراد . ثم الضمير في قوله وهي رميم يعود إلى العظام بالمعنى الغير المراد لا بالمعنى المراد وهو النفوس فكان من باب الاستخدام هذا ما ظهر لي. الثالث ما ذكره في غاية البيان والعناية أن المراد أصحاب العظام على تقدير مضاف . فإن قلت : المفهوم من الآية احياؤها في الآخرة وأحوالها لا تناسب أحوال الدنيا قلنا : سوق الكلام صريح في الرد على من أنكر عادتها في الآخرة إلى ما كانت عليه في الدنيا بعد أن صارت بالية خالية عن العود إليها في زعمهم وقد استدل بعض مشايخنا لغير العظم ونحوه بقوله تعالى ومن أصوافها وأوبارها وأشعارها أثاثاً ومتاعاً إلى حين) [النحل : ٨٠] ووجه الدلالة الآية عموم فإن الله تعالى من علينا بأن جعل لنا الانتفاع ولم يخص شعر الميتة من المذكاة فهو عموم إلا أن يمنع منه دليل. وأيضاً فإن الأصل كونها طاهرة قبل الموت بإجماع، ومن زعم أنه انتقل إلى نجاسة فعليه البيان. فإن قيل : حرمت عليكم الميتة وذلك عبارة عن الجملة قلنا : نخصه بما ذكرنا فإنه منصوص عليه في ذكر الصوف وليس في آيتكم ذكر الصوف صريحاً فكان دليلنا أولى . كذا ذكر القرطبي في تفسيره وذكر أن الصوف للغنم والوبر للإبل والشعر للمعز. وقد أجاب الاتقاني في غاية البيان أيضاً عن استدلالهم بقوله تعالى حرمت عليكم الميتة ﴾ [المائدة : ٣] بأنا لا نسلم أن المراد منه حرمة الانتفاع فلم لا يجوز أن يكون المراد منه حرمة الأكل بدليل ما رويناه في حديث مولاة ميمونة؟ ولئن قال الشافعي في بعض هذه الأشياء رطوبة فنقول نحن أيضاً نقول بنجاسته إذا بقيت الرطوبة، وكلامنا فيما إذا لم تبق الرطوبة في العظم والحافر والظلف ونحوه وإذا غسل الشعر ونحوه وأزيل عنه الدم المتصل والرطوبة النجسة . ولئن قال الشعر ينمو بنماء الأصل فنقول : نعم ينمو لكن لا نسلم أن النماء يدل على الحياة الحقيقية كما في النبات والشجر. وقوله بنماء الأصل غير مسلم أيضاً لأنه الأصل كما إذا هزل الحيوان بسبب مرض فطال شعره ا هـ . وقد وقع في الهداية تعريف الموت بزوال الحياة فقال في كشف الأسرار شرح أصول فخر الإسلام من باب الأهلية : الموت عند أهل السنة أمر وجودي لأنه ضد الحياة لقوله تعالى خلق الموت والحياة ﴾ [الملك: ٢] وعند المعتزلة : هو زوال الحياة فهو أمر عدمي وتفسير صاحب الهداية بزوال الحياة تفسير بلازمه. كذا نقل عن العلامة شمس الأئمة الكردري ا هـ . وهكذا أوله في الكافي . وذكر في معراج الدراية أن الموت ضد الحياة والضدان صفتان وجوديتان يتعاقبان على موضوع ينمو مع نقصان ولو فرضنا شخصاً مات ثم أعيدت حياته معجزة أو كرامة لعاد طاهراً .اهـ. قوله : (فإن قلت المفهوم من الأية) أي فإن قلت في الجواب عن الآية جواباً رابعاً قوله : ( وإذا غسل الشعر) معطوف على
Book of Purification. Likewise: the verse upon [it] in Fath al-Qadir briefly. And in al-Bada'i': Our companions have two approaches. One: These things are not dead (maytah) because 'maytah' from animals in the legal custom is a name for what has lost its life without human action or by illegitimate action, and there is no life in these things, so they are not dead. Second: The impurity of dead animals is not due to their essences but due to the flowing blood and impure moistures in them, and these are not found in these parts. End. And al-Hidayah limited itself to the first approach, and Ghayat al-Bayan to the second. And it is not hidden that the approach mentioned in al-Hidayah does not apply to sinew because there is life in it due to its movement; do you not see that a living being feels pain when it is cut? Unlike bone, for cutting a cow's horn does not pain it, so it indicates that there is no life in bone. Likewise in al-Nihayah. Therefore there are two narrations; the first is the second approach, and upon it there is no need to answer the statement of Allah Most High: 'They say, Who will give life to bones while they are decayed? Say, He will give them life who produced them the first time.' [Ya-Sin 36:79] For these things are from dead animals, except that the impurity of dead animals is only due to the blood and moistures, and sinew is solid, such cannot be imagined in it, and likewise in bone and hair. As for the answer to the first approach, it is from three aspects: First, what al-Zamakhshari mentioned in al-Kashshaf, saying: By this verse, one who affirms life in bones has argued, saying that the bones of the dead are impure because death affects them due to life entering them. As for the companions of Abu Hanifa (may Allah have mercy on them), they are pure according to them, and likewise hair and sinew, and they claim that life does not enter them, so death does not affect them, and they say what is meant by giving life to bones in the verse is returning them to what they were: soft and moist in a living sensitive body. End. And do not think that the author of al-Kashshaf disapproved of what he mentioned from the Hanafis due to his saying 'they claim' because 'claim' is a mount of falsehood as has been said. For we do not accept that 'claim' is specific to falsehood; rather it is used sometimes for it and sometimes for truth. From the first is the saying of Allah Most High: 'Those who disbelieved claimed that they will never be resurrected' [al-Taghabun 64:7]. From the second is the hadith of Muslim: 'Your Messenger claimed that Allah has prescribed upon us five prayers.' Al-Nawawi explicitly stated this in his commentary on Muslim and spoke at length about it. Second, what is meant by 'bones' is the souls, as in Mi'raj al-Dirayah. Then the pronoun in 'and they are decayed' refers to the real bones by way of usage (al-istikhdam), because it is part of it, as known in the science of badi', that a word with two meanings is intended, one of which is used, then a pronoun is brought that refers back to the word in wording and to the other meaning in sense. Like the saying of Mu'awiya ibn Abi Mulayk: 'When the sky descends upon the land of a people, we graze on it even if they are angry.' For he meant by 'sky' rain, and the pronoun in 'we graze on it' refers to vegetation, and vegetation is one meaning of 'sky' because it is a metaphor for it considering that rain is its cause, and it allowed the pronoun to refer to vegetation even though it was not previously mentioned. So reflect. And in the commentary of al-Maqdisi: I said, the answer to the objection is that the restoration of the ear and its stability usually occurs by the return of life to it, so it is not considered that it is from what was cut off from a living being because by the return of life to it it becomes as if it was not cut off. Al-Bahr al-Raiq, vol. 1, part 13. Preparation: because its cause was previously mentioned, which is the sky intended for rain. So too our case. For 'bones' has two meanings: one is intended, which is the souls, metaphorically from applying a part and intending the whole, and the other meaning is the real bones, not intended. Then the pronoun in 'and they are decayed' refers to bones in the unintended sense, not the intended sense which is the souls, so it is of the type of usage (al-istikhdam). This is what appeared to me. Third, what was mentioned in Ghayat al-Bayan and al-'Inayah: that what is meant is 'the owners of bones' by way of an implied genitive. If you say: The understood from the verse is its revival in the afterlife, and its states do not suit the states of this world. We say: The flow of speech is explicit in refuting those who denied its return in the afterlife to what it was in this world after it became decayed, devoid of return in their claim. And some of our mashayikh argued for other than bone and similar with His statement: 'And from their wool, their fur, and their hair as furnishings and enjoyment for a time' [al-Nahl 16:80]. The indication of the verse is generality, for Allah blessed us by making us benefit from it, and He did not specify the hair of a dead animal from a slaughtered one, so it is a generality unless there is a preventing proof. Also, the original ruling is that it is pure before death by consensus, and whoever claims that it became impure must provide evidence. If it is said: 'Forbidden to you is the dead animal' [al-Ma'idah 5:3], and that is an expression of the whole, we say: We specify it with what we mentioned, for it is explicitly mentioned regarding wool, and in your verse there is no explicit mention of wool, so our evidence is stronger. Thus al-Qurtubi mentioned in his tafsir, and he stated that wool is for sheep, fur for camels, and hair for goats. And al-Itqani in Ghayat al-Bayan also replied to their argument with His statement 'Forbidden to you is the dead animal' by saying we do not accept that what is meant is prohibition of benefit; why may it not be that what is meant is prohibition of eating, as evidenced by what we narrated in the hadith of Mawla Maimunah? And if al-Shafi'i says that in some of these things there is moisture, we say: we also say it is impure if the moisture remains, and our discussion is when the moisture remains no more in bone, hoof, cloven hoof, and the like, and when the hair and similar have been washed and any attached blood and impure moisture removed. And if he says that hair grows by growth of the origin, we say: yes it grows, but we do not accept that growth indicates real life as in plants and trees. And his saying by growth of the origin is also not accepted because it is the origin, as when an animal becomes lean due to illness and its hair grows long. End. And it has occurred in al-Hidayah defining death as the removal of life, and he said in Kashf al-Asrar Sharh Usul Fakhr al-Islam from the section on legal capacity: Death according to Ahl al-Sunnah is an existential matter because it is the opposite of life, as per the verse 'He created death and life' [al-Mulk 67:2]. According to the Mu'tazila, it is the removal of life, so it is a non-existential matter. And the interpretation of the author of al-Hidayah as removal of life is an interpretation of its necessary concomitant. Thus quoted from al-Allamah Shams al-A'imma al-Kardari. End. And likewise he interpreted it in al-Kafi. And he mentioned in Mi'raj al-Dirayah that death is the opposite of life, and the two opposites are two existential attributes that alternate upon a subject that grows with deficiency, and if we suppose a person died and then his life is restored as a miracle or a miracle, he returns pure. End. His saying: (For you said: what is understood from the verse) means: for you said in answer to the verse as a fourth answer. His saying: (and when the hair is washed) is conjuncted to.

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq is an expansion probe into the Hanafi ruling on the purity of bones, sinew (عصب), hair, and similar parts from dead animals. The author begins by presenting two methodological approaches (طريقان) of the Hanafi school. First: these parts are not considered 'dead' (ميتة) in the legal sense because they never possessed life; the legal definition of 'dead animal' is what lost life without proper slaughter. Second: even if they are part of a dead animal, their impurity is not inherent but due to blood and moisture—since these parts lack blood, they remain pure. The commentator notes that al-Hidayah followed the first approach, while Ghayat al-Bayan followed the second. He then critiques the first approach as it does not apply to sinew, which has life and movement, unlike bone. The discussion then turns to the Quranic verse about reviving bones (Ya-Sin 36:79), which opponents use to claim bones have life and thus become impure upon death. The Hanafi response is threefold: (1) citing al-Kashshaf, the verse means returning bones to their original moist state, not affirming continuous life; (2) interpreting 'bones' metaphorically as 'souls' to avoid the problem, using the rhetorical device of استخدام (istikhdam) where a word with two meanings is used, and a pronoun refers to the unintended meaning; (3) reading 'owners of bones' as an implied genitive. The text then defends the purity of hair and wool using Surah al-Nahl 16:80, arguing that general permissibility of benefit from wool, fur, and hair applies to dead animals unless specified. Against the verse forbidding dead animals (al-Ma'idah 5:3), the Hanafis respond that the prohibition is on eating, not benefit, citing the hadith of Mawla Maimunah (using hair of dead animals). Further rebuttals address moisture and growth arguments. Finally, the passage defines death as an existential attribute (the opposite of life) according to Ahl al-Sunnah, supporting the idea that parts without life are unaffected. Throughout, the author maintains the Hanafi position that bones, sinew, hair, wool, etc., from dead animals are pure (طاهر) when free of moisture.

Key Terms
الميتة (al-maytah): A dead animal that died without proper Islamic slaughter; legally impure according to most, but its parts are subject to ruling.
العصب (al-'asab): Sinew or tendon; considered to have life and movement, but still pure because it lacks blood/moisture.
الاستخدام (al-istikhdam): A rhetorical device where a word with two meanings is used, and a pronoun returns to the other meaning.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what condition must be met for the hair of a dead animal to be considered pure?
A) The hair must be from a slaughtered animal.
B) The hair must be free from blood and impure moisture.
C) The hair must be removed before death.
D) The hair must be from an animal that died of natural causes.

Key Takeaway
Bones, hair, and sinew from dead animals are pure in Hanafi fiqh because impurity is due to blood and moisture, not the solid parts themselves.

Taharah — Purification
intermediate
6 min
Purity of Hair, Wool, and Bones from Dead Animals: Hanafi vs Shafi'i
Expanding on the Hanafi ruling with proofs and refutation of Shafi'i

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the purity of hair, wool, and bones from dead animals, identify the evidence, and explain the Hanafi response to the Shafi'i objections.

Core Idea
Hair and bones from dead animals are pure for use unless moist; the prohibition of carrion is for eating, not use.

This lesson examines a key dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools over the purity of certain parts of dead animals (maytah). While the Shafi'i school considers all parts of a dead animal impure, the Hanafi school distinguishes between the impure flesh and solid parts like hair, wool, and bones. The Hanafi position relies on Quranic verse 16:80, consensus on pre-death purity, and istihsan. We will explore the proofs and responses presented in the text.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi ruling
Identify the precise ruling: hair, wool, and bones of dead animals are pure (tahir) for use, provided they are dry or washed free of impurities.
2
Trace the Hanafi dalil
Locate the evidence: Quran 16:80 gives general permission to use wool, fur, and hair; the default state before death is purity; the hadith of Maimunah's freed slave woman; and istihsan based on the principle that the prohibition of carrion is for eating, not other uses.
The text also cites support from al-Qurtubi and al-Atqani.
3
Understand the Shafi'i objection
Identify the opposing evidence: the general prohibition of carrion (Quran 5:3); argument that hair grows by the life of the animal, indicating impurity; and the presence of moisture from the dead body.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
Examine how the Hanafi scholars rebut each Shafi'i argument: 5:3 refers to eating, not use; growth does not indicate real life (as in plants); moisture is addressed by requiring washing or dryness.
The text also includes a discussion on the definition of death to refute the growth argument.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Note the practical difference: Hanafis allow using wool from a dead animal for clothing or carpets after washing; Shafi'is consider it impure and prohibit handling it.

فإن قلت : المفهوم من الآية احياؤها في الآخرة وأحوالها لا تناسب أحوال الدنيا قلنا : سوق الكلام صريح في الرد على من أنكر عادتها في الآخرة إلى ما كانت عليه في الدنيا بعد أن صارت بالية خالية عن العود إليها في زعمهم وقد استدل بعض مشايخنا لغير العظم ونحوه بقوله تعالى ومن أصوافها وأوبارها وأشعارها أثاثاً ومتاعاً إلى حين) [النحل : ٨٠] ووجه الدلالة الآية عموم فإن الله تعالى من علينا بأن جعل لنا الانتفاع ولم يخص شعر الميتة من المذكاة فهو عموم إلا أن يمنع منه دليل. وأيضاً فإن الأصل كونها طاهرة قبل الموت بإجماع، ومن زعم أنه انتقل إلى نجاسة فعليه البيان. فإن قيل : حرمت عليكم الميتة وذلك عبارة عن الجملة قلنا : نخصه بما ذكرنا فإنه منصوص عليه في ذكر الصوف وليس في آيتكم ذكر الصوف صريحاً فكان دليلنا أولى . كذا ذكر القرطبي في تفسيره وذكر أن الصوف للغنم والوبر للإبل والشعر للمعز. وقد أجاب الاتقاني في غاية البيان أيضاً عن استدلالهم بقوله تعالى حرمت عليكم الميتة ﴾ [المائدة : ٣] بأنا لا نسلم أن المراد منه حرمة الانتفاع فلم لا يجوز أن يكون المراد منه حرمة الأكل بدليل ما رويناه في حديث مولاة ميمونة؟ ولئن قال الشافعي في بعض هذه الأشياء رطوبة فنقول نحن أيضاً نقول بنجاسته إذا بقيت الرطوبة، وكلامنا فيما إذا لم تبق الرطوبة في العظم والحافر والظلف ونحوه وإذا غسل الشعر ونحوه وأزيل عنه الدم المتصل والرطوبة النجسة . ولئن قال الشعر ينمو بنماء الأصل فنقول : نعم ينمو لكن لا نسلم أن النماء يدل على الحياة الحقيقية كما في النبات والشجر. وقوله بنماء الأصل غير مسلم أيضاً لأنه الأصل كما إذا هزل الحيوان بسبب مرض فطال شعره ا هـ . وقد وقع في الهداية تعريف الموت بزوال الحياة فقال في كشف الأسرار شرح أصول فخر الإسلام من باب الأهلية : الموت عند أهل السنة أمر وجودي لأنه ضد الحياة لقوله تعالى خلق الموت والحياة ﴾ [الملك: ٢] وعند المعتزلة : هو زوال الحياة فهو أمر عدمي وتفسير صاحب الهداية بزوال الحياة تفسير بلازمه. كذا نقل عن العلامة شمس الأئمة الكردري ا هـ . وهكذا أوله في الكافي .
If you say: The understanding from the verse is its revival in the Hereafter and its states are not appropriate for the states of this world. We say: The flow of speech is clear in refutation of those who denied its return in the Hereafter to what it was in this world after it had become decayed and devoid of return in their opinion. And some of our scholars argued for other than bone and its like by His statement: 'And from their wool and their fur and their hair, furnishings and enjoyment for a time' [al-Nahl 16:80]. The indication of the verse is generality, for Allah has favored us by making permissible for us the benefit, and He did not specify the hair of the dead from the slaughtered, so it is a generality unless evidence prevents it. Also, the original state of them is purity before death by consensus, and whoever claims that it has transferred to impurity must provide proof. If it is said: 'Forbidden to you is carrion' [al-Ma'idah 5:3] and that refers to the whole, we say: We specify it by what we have mentioned, for it is explicitly mentioned in the mention of wool, and in your verse there is no explicit mention of wool, so our proof is stronger. Similarly, al-Qurtubi mentioned in his Tafsir, and he stated that wool is for sheep, fur for camels, and hair for goats. And al-Atqani responded in Ghayat al-Bayan to their argument by His statement 'Forbidden to you is carrion' that we do not accept that the intended meaning is prohibition of benefit, so why is it not permissible that the intended meaning is the prohibition of eating, as evidenced by what we narrated in the hadith of the freed slave woman of Maimunah? And if al-Shafi'i says that some of these things have moisture, we say we also rule its impurity if moisture remains, and our discussion is when moisture does not remain in the bone, hoof, cloven hoof, and similar, and when the hair and the like are washed and the attached blood and impure moisture are removed. And if he says that hair grows by the growth of the origin, we say: Yes it grows, but we do not accept that growth indicates real life as in plants and trees. And his statement 'by the growth of the origin' is also not accepted because it is the origin as when an animal becomes emaciated due to illness and its hair grows long. End. And al-Hidayah mentioned the definition of death as the cessation of life, and in Kashf al-Asrar, Sharh Usul Fakhr al-Islam, from the chapter of legal capacity, it is stated: Death according to Ahl al-Sunnah is an existential matter because it is the opposite of life, as per His statement 'He who created death and life' [al-Mulk 67:2]. According to the Mu'tazilah, it is the cessation of life, so it is a non-existential matter. And the interpretation of the author of al-Hidayah as the cessation of life is an interpretation by its concomitant. Thus it is transmitted from the scholar Shams al-A'immah al-Kardari. End. And similarly he interpreted it in al-Kafi.
Source pages p. 194

Commentary
This passage is part of the commentary on the Book of Purification (Kitab al-Taharah) in al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, a Hanafi fiqh text. The author is addressing the ruling on using parts of dead animals (maytah), specifically hair, wool, and bones. The Shafi'i school holds that these are impure because they are part of the dead animal which is generally forbidden. The Hanafi school, however, distinguishes between the impure flesh and blood and the solid parts like hair, wool, and bones, which remain pure after death. The proof is based on the Quranic verse (16:80) which mentions using wool, fur, and hair from animals for furnishings, without specifying whether the animal is slaughtered or dead. The general principle is that these are pure before death by consensus, and the burden of proof is on those who claim impurity. The general prohibition of carrion in 5:3 is interpreted as referring to eating, not other uses, supported by the hadith of the freed slave woman of Maimunah (where the Prophet allowed using the skin of a dead animal after tanning). Additionally, the moistness of the parts is considered: if there is moisture from the dead animal, it is impure, but if the parts are dry or washed, they are pure. The argument that hair grows and thus indicates life is refuted: growth does not necessarily indicate real life, as hair can grow even on an emaciated animal. A digression into the definition of death is included to support the claim that hair growth does not imply life: according to Ahl al-Sunnah, death is an existential attribute (not merely absence of life), and the cessation of life is a concomitant definition, not the essence. This reinforces the idea that the dead animal's parts are not impure due to life being present. The position stated here is the relied-upon Hanafi position.

Key Terms
ميتة (maytah): Dead animal that has not been slaughtered according to Islamic law.
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference; a method of reasoning that departs from strict qiyas in favor of a more equitable ruling.
عام (amm): General term that encompasses all its individuals without restriction.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the primary condition for hair from a dead animal to be considered pure?
A) The hair must be from an animal that was slaughtered.
B) The hair must be dry or washed free of any impure moisture.
C) The hair must be tanned (dabgh).
D) The hair must be from a camel only.

Key Takeaway
Hair, wool, and bones from dead animals are pure in the Hanafi school when dry or washed, based on Quran 16:80, consensus, and istihsan, distinguishing use from eating.

Defining Death: An Existential Attribute or Mere Absence?
This passage defines death and critiques alternative views.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of death as an existential attribute, cite the Quranic evidence, and distinguish it from the Mu'tazili view.

Core Idea
Death is a created existential attribute, not mere absence of life.

In this passage from Bahr al-Ra'iq, the author delves into a theological dispute with direct fiqh implications: what is the nature of death? The Hanafi position, aligning with Ahl al-Sunnah, holds that death is an existential quality created by Allah, which leads to the ruling that a resurrected body returns pure.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key claim about death
Locate the statement from Mirqat al-Dirayah that death is the opposite of life and both are existential attributes.
2
Trace the objection from Ghayat al-Bayan
Understand the objection: cessation of life cannot be non-existential because it cannot coexist with life, so it must be an opposite.
The objector assumes opposition requires both to be existential.
3
Analyze the author's response distinguishing opposition types
The author distinguishes real opposition (between two existents) from the relation of privation and possession (like blindness and sight). Cessation of life is privation, not a true opposite.
4
Evaluate the Quranic proof used by al-Tibi
Al-Tibi cites 'He created death and life' (67:2) – the created must be existential; non-existence cannot be created.
This is the primary Hanafi dalil.
5
Conclude the relied-upon view and its fiqh implication
The Sunni/Hanafi view that death is existential is established; this paves the way for the purity ruling on resurrection.
