وذكر في معراج الدراية أن الموت ضد الحياة والضدان صفتان وجوديتان يتعاقبان على موضوع ينمو مع نقصان ولو فرضنا شخصاً مات ثم أعيدت حياته معجزة أو كرامة لعاد طاهراً .اهـ. واحد ويستحيل اجتماعهما ويجوز ،ارتفاعهما وزوال الحياة ليس بضد الحياة كما أن زوال السكون ليس بضد السكون فكان هذا تعريفاً بلازمه ا هـ . وتعقبه في غاية البيان بأنا لا نسلم أن زوال الحياة ليس بضد لها، وكيف يقال هذا وزوال الحياة مع الحياة لا يجتمعان؟ وليس معنى التضاد إلا هذا ولا نسلم أن زوال الحياة ليس بوجودي فهل لزوال الحياة وجود أم لا؟ فإن قلت نعم فيكون زوال الحياة وجودياً، وإن قلت لا فيكون حينئذ زوال الحياة حياة وهو محال لأن عدم زوال الحياة عبارة عن الحياة ا هـ . ولا يخفى ضعفه لأن الموت نفس زوال الحياة لا عدم زوالها ولا يلزم من كون نقيض الشيء عدمياً أن يكون عدم عدمه حتى يكون نفي النفي فيكون اثباتاً. وأما جعله زوال الحياة ضداً لها فغير مسلم لأن التضاد الحقيقي . أن يكون بين الموجودين اللذين يمكن تعقل أحدهما الذهول عن الآخر تعاقب على الموضوع ويكون بينهما غاية الخلاف وهي ما يكون مقتضى كل منهما مغاير المقتضى الآخر كالسواد والبياض فإن مقتضى أحدهما قبض البصر ومقتضى الثاني تفريقه، ولا شك أن زوال الحياة عدمي فلا يكون ضداً لها وإنما يكون بينهما تقابل العدم والملكة، وقد ذكر بعض الأصوليين في شرح المغني أن هذا الفرق إنما هو على اصطلاح أهل العقول، أما على اصطلاح الأصوليين فالضد ما يقابل الشيء ويكون بينهما . غاية الخلاف، سواء كانا وجوديين أو أحدهما وجودي والآخر عدمي. وقد اختار صاحب الكشاف أن الموت عدمي فقال : والحياة ما يصح بوجوده الإحساس، وقيل ما يوجب كون الشيء حياً وهو الذي يصح منه أن يعلم ويقدر والموت عدم ذلك فيه، ومعنى خلق الموت والحياة إيجاد ذلك المصحح وإعدامه. قال الطيبي رحمه الله في حاشيته: قوله «والموت عدم ذلك فيه الانتصاف لمذهب القدرية أن الموت عدم واعتقاد السنية أنه أمر وجودي ضاد الحياة، وكيف يكون عدمياً وقد وصف بكونه مخلوقاً وعدم الحوادث أزلي، ولو كان المعدوم مخلوقاً لزم وقوع الحوادث أزلاً وهو ظاهر البطلان وقال صاحب الفوائد: لو كان الموت عدم الحياة استحال أن يكون مخلوقاً وقد قال بعد ذلك : معنى خلق الموت والحياة إيجاد ذلك المصحح وإعدامه، وهذا أيضاً منظور فيه. وقال الإمام: هي الصفة التي يكون الموصوف بها بحيث يصح أن يعلم ويقدر واختلفوا في الموت قبل إنه عبارة عن عدم هذه الصفة، وقيل صفة وجودية مضادة للحياة لقوله تعالى الذي خلق الموت والحياة ﴾ [الملك: ٢] والعدم لا يكون مخلوقاً. هذا هو التحقيق إلى هنا كلام الطيبي رحمه الله تعالى. وقال الإمام القرطبي في تفسيره قال العلماء الله رضي عنهم : : الموت ليس بعدم محض ولا فناء صرف وإنما هو تعلق قوله «إذا لم تبق الرطوبة». قوله : (وقد قال بعد ذلك) فاعل «قال ضمير يعود» إلى صاحب
And it is mentioned in Mirqat al-Dirayah that death is the opposite of life, and opposites are two existential attributes that alternate upon one subject. It grows with deficiency. And if we suppose a person died and then his life is restored by a miracle or a saintly act, he would return pure. End. And it is impossible for them to both gather, and it is permissible for both to be removed. And the cessation of life is not an opposite of life, just as the cessation of stillness is not an opposite of stillness, so this was a definition by its concomitant. End. And he objected to it in Ghayat al-Bayan: We do not accept that the cessation of life is not an opposite to it. How can this be said while cessation of life and life cannot come together? And the meaning of opposition is nothing but this. And we do not accept that the cessation of life is not existential. Does cessation of life have existence or not? If you say yes, then cessation of life is existential, and if you say no, then cessation of life is life, which is impossible because the absence of cessation of life is life. End. And its weakness is not hidden because death is the very cessation of life, not the absence of its cessation. And from the fact that the contrary of something is non-existential, it does not follow that its non-existence would be its existence, such that the negation of negation would be affirmation. As for making the cessation of life an opposite to it, it is not accepted because real opposition is between two existents, each of which can be conceived while ignoring the other, alternating upon one subject, and between them there is the utmost difference, which is that the implication of each is different from the implication of the other, like blackness and whiteness, for the implication of one is contraction of sight and the other is dispersion. And there is no doubt that the cessation of life is non-existential, so it is not an opposite to it. Rather, between them is the relationship of the privation and the possession. And some usuli scholars mentioned in Sharh al-Mughni that this distinction is according to the terminology of theologians; as for the terminology of usulis, the opposite is what opposes the thing and between them is the utmost difference, whether both are existential or one existential and the other non-existential. And the author of al-Kashshaf chose that death is non-existential, saying: Life is that by whose existence sensation becomes sound, and it is said: that which makes a thing alive, and that is that from which it is sound that it knows and has power, and death is the absence of that in it. And the meaning of 'He created death and life' is the bringing into existence of that sound basis and its removal. Al-Tibi (may Allah have mercy on him) said in his gloss: His statement 'and death is the absence of that in it' does justice to the Qadari sect that death is non-existential, while the Sunni belief is that it is an existential matter opposite to life. How could it be non-existential while it is described as being created, and the non-existence of events is eternal. And if the non-existent were created, it would necessitate the occurrence of events eternally, which is clearly false. And the author of al-Fawa'id said: If death were the absence of life, it would be impossible for it to be created, and he said later: The meaning of 'He created death and life' is the bringing into existence of that sound basis and its removal, and this is also subject to consideration. And the Imam said: It is the attribute by which the described becomes such that it can know and have power. And they differed concerning death before: Is it an expression of the absence of this attribute, or is it an existential attribute opposite to life, as per His statement 'He who created death and life'? And non-existence cannot be created. This is the verification. This is the speech of al-Tibi. And Imam al-Qurtubi said in his Tafsir: The scholars (may Allah be pleased with them) said: Death is not pure non-existence nor pure annihilation, but rather it is a connection. His statement 'when the moisture does not remain'. His statement: 'He said later' - the subject of 'said' is the author.
Source pages p. 194

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion on the nature of death (maut) and its implications for purity in the Hanafi school. The author begins by citing from Mirqat al-Diraya that death is the opposite of life, and both are existential attributes that alternate on a subject. The key ruling presented is that if a person dies and then is miraculously brought back to life (by a miracle or saintly act), he will return to life in a state of purity (tahir). This ruling is significant because it implies that death does not make the body inherently impure; rather, the body can be purified upon resurrection. The author then critiques the definition of death as mere cessation (zawal) of life, arguing that cessation is not an opposite (didd) of life, as opposites must both be existential. He discusses the objection from Ghayat al-Bayan, which argues that cessation of life and life cannot coexist, so cessation must be an opposite. The author refutes this by distinguishing between real opposition (between two existents) and the relation of privation and possession (like sight and blindness). He then quotes al-Zamakhshari (author of al-Kashshaf) who holds death is non-existential. Al-Tibi's gloss defends the Sunni position that death is existential and created, citing the Quranic verse 'He created death and life' (67:2). The non-existential cannot be created. Al-Qurtubi's statement is quoted, suggesting death is not pure non-existence but a 'connection' (ta'alluq). The passage ends abruptly with a reference to the moisture issue from the previous discussion. The overall argument reinforces the Hanafi view that death is an existential attribute, and this supports the purity of the body after death and the possibility of resurrection to a pure state.

Key Terms
موت (mawt): Death; an existential attribute that is the opposite of life, created by Allah.
ضد (didd): Opposite; two existential attributes that cannot coexist and alternate upon one subject.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) 'Every soul shall taste death' (3:185)
B) 'He created death and life' (67:2)
C) 'And We have made from water every living thing' (21:30)
D) 'Then We cause to die and then give life' (23:15)

Key Takeaway
Death is a created existential attribute, not a mere absence, and this concept directly supports the Hanafi ruling that a resurrected person is pure.

Purity upon Resurrection: The Fiqh Implication of the Definition of Death
This passage contains the ruling on purity after resurrection and its basis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a resurrected person is pure (tahir) and explain how it follows from the existential nature of death.

Core Idea
A resurrected person returns pure because death is an removable existential attribute.

Building on the definition of death as an existential attribute, this lesson focuses on the direct fiqh ruling: if a person dies and is resurrected by miracle or saintly act, he becomes pure (tahir). This ruling demonstrates the practical importance of the correct theological understanding of death.

Guided Path
1
Recall the ruling from Mirqat al-Dirayah
Find the statement: 'لو فرضنا شخصاً مات ثم أعيدت حياته معجزة أو كرامة لعاد طاهراً' – this is the core ruling.
2
Understand the underlying principle
The ruling depends on death being an existential attribute that can be removed by Allah. When life returns, the impurity caused by death is removed.
Refer to the previous lesson on the nature of death.
3
Identify the condition for the ruling
The scenario requires a genuine death followed by a miraculous resurrection (mu'jizah or karamah).
This is not a medical revival.
4
Distinguish from the normal state of a dead body
Normally, a dead body is ritually impure (najis) and requires ghusl. Resurrection uniquely restores purity.
5
Reflect on the ta'leel (reason) behind the ruling
The effective cause is the removal of the existential attribute of death, which brings the body back to its prior pure state.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

والبياض فإن مقتضى أحدهما قبض البصر ومقتضى الثاني تفريقه، ولا شك أن زوال الحياة عدمي فلا يكون ضداً لها وإنما يكون بينهما تقابل العدم والملكة، وقد ذكر بعض الأصوليين في شرح المغني أن هذا الفرق إنما هو على اصطلاح أهل العقول، أما على اصطلاح الأصوليين فالضد ما يقابل الشيء ويكون بينهما . غاية الخلاف، سواء كانا وجوديين أو أحدهما وجودي والآخر عدمي. وقد اختار صاحب الكشاف أن الموت عدمي فقال : والحياة ما يصح بوجوده الإحساس، وقيل ما يوجب كون الشيء حياً وهو الذي يصح منه أن يعلم ويقدر والموت عدم ذلك فيه، ومعنى خلق الموت والحياة إيجاد ذلك المصحح وإعدامه. قال الطيبي رحمه الله في حاشيته: قوله «والموت عدم ذلك فيه الانتصاف لمذهب القدرية أن الموت عدم واعتقاد السنية أنه أمر وجودي ضاد الحياة، وكيف يكون عدمياً وقد وصف بكونه مخلوقاً وعدم الحوادث أزلي، ولو كان المعدوم مخلوقاً لزم وقوع الحوادث أزلاً وهو ظاهر البطلان وقال صاحب الفوائد: لو كان الموت عدم الحياة استحال أن يكون مخلوقاً وقد قال بعد ذلك : معنى خلق الموت والحياة إيجاد ذلك المصحح وإعدامه، وهذا أيضاً منظور فيه. وقال الإمام: هي الصفة التي يكون الموصوف بها بحيث يصح أن يعلم ويقدر واختلفوا في الموت قبل إنه عبارة عن عدم هذه الصفة، وقيل صفة وجودية مضادة للحياة لقوله تعالى الذي خلق الموت والحياة ﴾ [الملك: ٢] والعدم لا يكون مخلوقاً. هذا هو التحقيق إلى هنا كلام الطيبي رحمه الله تعالى. وقال الإمام القرطبي في تفسيره قال العلماء الله رضي عنهم : : الموت ليس بعدم محض ولا فناء صرف وإنما هو تعلق قوله «إذا لم تبق الرطوبة». قوله : (وقد قال بعد ذلك) فاعل «قال ضمير يعود» إلى صاحب
And it is mentioned in Mirqat al-Dirayah that death is the opposite of life, and opposites are two existential attributes that alternate upon one subject. It grows with deficiency. And if we suppose a person died and then his life is restored by a miracle or a saintly act, he would return pure. End. And it is impossible for them to both gather, and it is permissible for both to be removed. And the cessation of life is not an opposite of life, just as the cessation of stillness is not an opposite of stillness, so this was a definition by its concomitant. End. And he objected to it in Ghayat al-Bayan: We do not accept that the cessation of life is not an opposite to it. How can this be said while cessation of life and life cannot come together? And the meaning of opposition is nothing but this. And we do not accept that the cessation of life is not existential. Does cessation of life have existence or not? If you say yes, then cessation of life is existential, and if you say no, then cessation of life is life, which is impossible because the absence of cessation of life is life. End. And its weakness is not hidden because death is the very cessation of life, not the absence of its cessation. And from the fact that the contrary of something is non-existential, it does not follow that its non-existence would be its existence, such that the negation of negation would be affirmation. As for making the cessation of life an opposite to it, it is not accepted because real opposition is between two existents, each of which can be conceived while ignoring the other, alternating upon one subject, and between them there is the utmost difference, which is that the implication of each is different from the implication of the other, like blackness and whiteness, for the implication of one is contraction of sight and the other is dispersion. And there is no doubt that the cessation of life is non-existential, so it is not an opposite to it. Rather, between them is the relationship of the privation and the possession. And some usuli scholars mentioned in Sharh al-Mughni that this distinction is according to the terminology of theologians; as for the terminology of usulis, the opposite is what opposes the thing and between them is the utmost difference, whether both are existential or one existential and the other non-existential. And the author of al-Kashshaf chose that death is non-existential, saying: Life is that by whose existence sensation becomes sound, and it is said: that which makes a thing alive, and that is that from which it is sound that it knows and has power, and death is the absence of that in it. And the meaning of 'He created death and life' is the bringing into existence of that sound basis and its removal. Al-Tibi (may Allah have mercy on him) said in his gloss: His statement 'and death is the absence of that in it' does justice to the Qadari sect that death is non-existential, while the Sunni belief is that it is an existential matter opposite to life. How could it be non-existential while it is described as being created, and the non-existence of events is eternal. And if the non-existent were created, it would necessitate the occurrence of events eternally, which is clearly false. And the author of al-Fawa'id said: If death were the absence of life, it would be impossible for it to be created, and he said later: The meaning of 'He created death and life' is the bringing into existence of that sound basis and its removal, and this is also subject to consideration. And the Imam said: It is the attribute by which the described becomes such that it can know and have power. And they differed concerning death before: Is it an expression of the absence of this attribute, or is it an existential attribute opposite to life, as per His statement 'He who created death and life'? And non-existence cannot be created. This is the verification. This is the speech of al-Tibi. And Imam al-Qurtubi said in his Tafsir: The scholars (may Allah be pleased with them) said: Death is not pure non-existence nor pure annihilation, but rather it is a connection. His statement 'when the moisture does not remain'. His statement: 'He said later' - the subject of 'said' is the author.
Source pages p. 194

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion on the nature of death (maut) and its implications for purity in the Hanafi school. The author begins by citing from Mirqat al-Diraya that death is the opposite of life, and both are existential attributes that alternate on a subject. The key ruling presented is that if a person dies and then is miraculously brought back to life (by a miracle or saintly act), he will return to life in a state of purity (tahir). This ruling is significant because it implies that death does not make the body inherently impure; rather, the body can be purified upon resurrection. The author then critiques the definition of death as mere cessation (zawal) of life, arguing that cessation is not an opposite (didd) of life, as opposites must both be existential. He discusses the objection from Ghayat al-Bayan, which argues that cessation of life and life cannot coexist, so cessation must be an opposite. The author refutes this by distinguishing between real opposition (between two existents) and the relation of privation and possession (like sight and blindness). He then quotes al-Zamakhshari (author of al-Kashshaf) who holds death is non-existential. Al-Tibi's gloss defends the Sunni position that death is existential and created, citing the Quranic verse 'He created death and life' (67:2). The non-existential cannot be created. Al-Qurtubi's statement is quoted, suggesting death is not pure non-existence but a 'connection' (ta'alluq). The passage ends abruptly with a reference to the moisture issue from the previous discussion. The overall argument reinforces the Hanafi view that death is an existential attribute, and this supports the purity of the body after death and the possibility of resurrection to a pure state.

Key Terms
موت (mawt): Death; an existential attribute that is the opposite of life, created by Allah.
طهارة (taharah): Ritual purity; the state of being free from ritual impurity (hadath or najasah).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on a person who dies and is resurrected by a miracle or saintly act?
A) He remains impure until he performs ghusl.
B) He becomes pure immediately.
C) He becomes pure only if he was a Muslim.
D) His purity depends on the type of miracle.

Key Takeaway
A resurrected person returns pure because death is a removable existential attribute, not a permanent non-existence.

Draining a Well Due to Impurity: Conditions and Exceptions
Stating the ruling that a well must be drained when a non-animal impurity falls in, with conditions and exceptions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on draining a well when impurity falls in, identify the size threshold (10x10 cubits), and explain the exceptions for animal droppings and the intra-Hanafi dispute.

Core Idea
A well smaller than 10x10 cubits becomes impure and must be fully drained; larger wells require draining only if water changes.

In this passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, the author explains the ruling on well water purification when an impurity falls in. The matn states that the well is to be drained, but the commentary clarifies that this applies to wells smaller than ten cubits by ten cubits. Larger wells are treated like a large body of water and only become impure if the water's color, taste, or smell changes. Additionally, certain animal droppings are excepted because they are considered pure. The passage also records a minority intra-Hanafi view that a deep well may be like running water, but the commentator rejects this as contradicting the established school rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is 'nazh al-bi'r' (draining the well) when impurity falls in. The ruling is that it is wajib (obligatory) to drain the well completely. Note: this applies only to non-animal impurities (e.g., blood, urine, wine) except for certain animal droppings (camel, sheep, pigeon, sparrow) which are pure.
2
Locate the dalil (proof) for the ruling
The proof is an analogy (qiyas) to a small basin (hawd saghir). Just as a small basin of water becomes entirely impure when any impurity falls into it, so does a well, because both are stagnant water. The well is treated 'like a small basin' (bi-manzilat al-hawd al-saghir).
3
Identify the exception for large wells (10x10 cubits)
If the well is ten cubits by ten cubits or larger, it does not become impure simply by the fall of impurity; it only becomes impure if the water changes in color, taste, or smell. This large well is likened to a cistern or large body of water that resists impurity.
The size threshold is not depth but surface area (10x10).
4
Note the exceptions for animal droppings
The matn says the well is not drained by the droppings of camels and sheep, or the excrement of pigeons and sparrows. These are considered pure substances (tahir), so they do not render the water impure.
'Ba'r' (دروppings of camels/sheep) and 'khur' (excrement of birds whose meat is eaten) are pure in the Hanafi school.
5
Consider the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf and tarjih
There is a reported view from Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf that if a well is deep (depth ten cubits or more) and not wide, it does not become impure because it resembles running water. However, the commentator rejects this as contradicting the majority of the school (jumhur al-ashab) and the established rulings (masa'il) in the books. The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is that the well is like a small basin, so it must be drained when impurity falls in (unless it reaches the 10x10 size threshold).
The tarjih mechanism: consistency with the body of fiqh rules outweighs a rare narration.

طاهران وتنزح البئر بوقوع نجس لا ببعرتي إبل وغنم وخرء حمام وعصفور وبول ما قوله : ( وتنزح البئر بوقوع (نجس) لما ذكر حكم الماء القليل بأنه يتنجس كله عند وقوع النجاسة فيه حتى يراق كله ورد عليه ماء البئر نقضاً في أنه لا ينزح كله في بعض الصور فذكر أحكامه . قال الشارحون ومنهم المصنف في المستصفى : إن المراد بنزح البئر نزح مائها إطلاقاً لاسم المحل على الحال كقولهم جرى الميزاب وسال الوادي» «وأكل القدر» والمراد ما حل فيها للمبالغة في إخراج جميع الماء والمراد بالبئر هنا التي لم تكن عشراً في عشر، أما إذا كانت عشراً في عشر لا تنجس بوقوع نجس إلا بالتغير كما يفيده ما سنذكره. والمراد بالنجس هنا هو الذي ليس حيواناً كالدم والبول والخمر، وأما أحكام الحيوان الواقع فيها فسنذكرها مفصلة. وبهذا يظهر ضعف ما في التبيين من أن المصنف أطلق ولم يقدر بشيء لم يعين ما وقع فيها من النجاسة فأي نجس وقع فيها يوجب نزحها وإنما ينجس ماء البئر كله بقليل النجاسة لأن البئر عندنا بمنزلة الحوض الصغير تفسد بما يفسد به الحوض الصغير إلا أن يكون عشراً في عشر. كذا في فتاوى قاضيخان وفي التفاريق عن أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف : البئر لا تنجس كالماء الجاري البئر إذا لم تكن عريضة وكان عمق مائها عشرة أذرع فصاعداً فوقعت النجاسة فيها لا يحكم بنجاستها في أصح الأقاويل ا هـ. وعزاه في القنية إلى شرح صدر القضاة وذكر ابن وهبان أنه مخالف لما أطلقه جمهور الأصحاب . كذا في شرح منية المصلي. ولا يخفى أن هذا التصحيح لو ثبت لانهدمت مسائل أصحابنا المذكورة في كتبهم وقد عللوا بأن البئر لما وجب إخراج النجاسة منها ولا يمكن إخراجها منها إلا بنزح كل مائها
Two pure [substances?] and the well is drained by the occurrence of impurity, not by droppings of camels and sheep, and the excrement of pigeons and sparrows, and urine of what? His statement: 'and the well is drained by the occurrence of impurity' – when he mentioned the ruling of small water, that it all becomes impure when impurity falls into it until it is all poured out, the water of the well was objected to as a counterexample that it is not all drained in some cases, so he mentioned its rulings. The commentators, including the author in al-Mustasfa, said: The intended meaning of 'draining the well' is draining its water, by using the name of the vessel for its contents, as they say 'the waterspout flowed' and 'the valley flowed' and 'the pot ate'. What is intended is the water in it, for hyperbole in removing all the water. The intended well here is that which is not ten cubits by ten cubits. As for if it is ten by ten, it does not become impure by the occurrence of impurity except by change, as will be mentioned. The intended impurity here is that which is not an animal, like blood, urine, and wine. As for the rulings of an animal falling in, we will mention them in detail. By this, the weakness of what is in al-Tabyin is shown: that the author made it absolute and did not specify anything; he did not specify what impurity fell in; any impurity that falls in necessitates draining it. Rather, all the well water becomes impure by a small amount of impurity because the well with us is like a small basin; it is spoiled by what spoils a small basin, unless it is ten by ten. Thus in Fatawa Qadikhan, and in al-Tafariq from Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf: The well does not become impure like running water; if the well is not wide and its water depth is ten cubits or more, and impurity falls into it, it is not judged to be impure according to the soundest pronouncements. He attributed it in al-Qunyah to Sharh Sadr al-Quda, and Ibn Wihban mentioned that it contradicts what the majority of our companions have stated. Thus in Sharh Munyat al-Musalli. It is not hidden that if this correction were established, it would demolish the issues of our companions mentioned in their books. And they have given the reason that since the removal of impurity from the well is obligatory, and it cannot be removed except by draining all its water.

Commentary
This passage is a ruling_with_proof regarding the purification of a well when impurity falls into it. The matn states: 'The well is drained (i.e., its water is completely removed) by the occurrence of impurity, but not by droppings of camels and sheep, and the excrement of pigeons and sparrows, and urine of...' (the text breaks off). The commentary clarifies that the ruling applies to wells smaller than 10x10 cubits; larger wells (10x10 or more) do not become impure unless the water changes in color, taste, or smell. The impurity in question is non-animal (e.g., blood, urine, wine). The passage also mentions a differing opinion attributed to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf that a deep well (depth 10 cubits or more) may be like running water and not become impure, but the commentator (from the Hanafi school) dismisses this as contradicting the majority view and the established rulings of the school. The reasoning (ta'lil) given is that the well is like a small basin: any small amount of impurity renders all the water impure, necessitating complete drainage. The exceptions for animal droppings are based on their being less problematic.

Key Terms
نزح (nazh): Draining or bailing out all water from a well for purification.
عشراً في عشر (ashran fi ashran): Ten cubits by ten cubits (approx. 4.5m x 4.5m), a size threshold for a well to be considered large and not easily contaminated.
بعر (ba'r): Droppings of camels and sheep (بعر literally means dung of cloven-hoofed animals).

Amounts of Water to Remove from Wells for Various Dead Animals
Rulings on well purification based on transmitted traditions and consensus

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the number of buckets to remove from a well when a mouse, chicken, cat, or human dies in it, and cite the primary evidence (hadith and consensus).

Core Idea
The amount of water to remove depends on the size of the dead animal, based on prophetic commands and companion consensus.

When an animal dies in a well, the water becomes impure to some degree. The Hanafi school, following traditions (athar), has specified exact amounts of water to be removed for different animals. In this lesson, we will study the specific rulings and the direct evidence from the Prophet (ﷺ) and the Companions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the animal and its relative size
The amount to be removed is based on the size of the dead animal. Small animals like mice require fewer buckets; larger animals like chickens require more; humans require all water.
Size here refers to the body volume that contacts water.
2
Recall the specific number of buckets from hadith
For a mouse: 20 or 30 buckets; for a chicken: 40 buckets. These numbers come from prophetic reports.
The variance in mouse narration (20 or 30) shows flexibility.
3
Note the consensus on complete removal for large animals
When a human or similarly sized animal dies, all water must be removed, as was done by Ibn 'Abbas and Ibn al-Zubayr for Zamzam.
This was done in the presence of Companions without objection, establishing ijma.
4
Understand the scope of the ruling
If the animal disintegrates or swells, all water must be removed because moisture from the corpse contacts all parts.
This is based on the principle that direct contact determines impurity spread.

ليس للرأي فيها مدخل وما ذكره خلافه . كذا تعقبه شارح المنية. والذي ظهر لي أن ما ذكره في البدائع لا يخالف ما صرحوا به لأنه ذكر أن هذا معنى خفي فقهي لا قياس جلي ولا يكون من قبيل الرأي إلا القياس الجلي. وأما القياس الخفي فهو المسمى بالاستحسان إن قال في التوضيح: القياس جلي وخفي. فالخفي يسمى بالاستحسان لكنه أعم من القياس الخفي فإن كل قياس خفي استحسان وليس كل استحسان قياساً خفياً لأن الاستحسان قد يطلق على غير القياس الخفي أيضاً لكن الغالب في كتب أصحابنا أنه إذا ذكر الاستحسان أريد به القياس الخفي، وهو دليل يقابل القياس الجلي الذي يسبق إليه الإفهام وهو حجة عندنا لأن ثبوته بالدلائل التي هي حجة إجماعاً لأنه إما بالأثر كالسلم والإجارة وبقاء الصوم في النسيان، وإما بالإجماع كالاستصناع وإما بالضرورة كطهارة الحياض والآبار. وأما بالقياس الخفي إلى آخر ما ذكر في أصول الفقه وكذا في كثير من كتب الأصول، فظهر بهذا أن طهارة الآبار بالنزح إنما ثبتت بالقياس الخفي الذي ثبت بالضرورة.
Know that rulings of wells are based on following traditions (athar) and not analogy (qiyas), for analogy would either require that they never become pure, as Bishr said due to impossibility because impurity mixes with mud and walls and water seeps gradually, or that they never become impure by dropping the ruling of impurity due to difficulty of avoidance or purification, as transmitted from Muhammad who said: My opinion and Abu Yusuf's opinion agreed that well water is like running water because its bottom and water is taken from its top, so it does not become impure like a bath basin. We said: and what is upon us except that we remove buckets from it, taking from the traditions (athar), and the way is that a person is in the hand of the Prophet (peace be upon him) and his companions (may Allah be pleased with them) like a blind man in the hand of a guide. Thus in Fath al-Qadir and other commentaries. And in al-Bada'i, after mentioning the two analogies, he said: But we abandoned the two apparent analogies based on the report (khabar) and tradition (athar) and a kind of subtle jurisprudence (fiqh khafi). As for the report: Abu Ja'far al-Astarushni narrated with his chain from the Prophet (peace be upon him) that he said regarding a mouse that dies in a well: twenty buckets are removed, and in another narration thirty. And from Abu Sa'id al-Khudri that he said regarding a chicken that died in a well: forty buckets are removed. And from Ibn 'Abbas and Ibn al-Zubayr that they ordered the complete removal of Zamzam water when a black man died in it, in the presence of the Companions, and no one objected, so consensus (ijma') was established. As for the subtle jurisprudence: it is that these things have spilled blood which permeates their parts at death, making them impure, and the water has come into contact with these things; water becomes impure or spoiled by adjacency to impurity, because the principle is that what is adjacent to impurity is impure by Shari'ah. The Prophet (peace be upon him) said regarding a mouse that dies in solid ghee: 'Cut around it and throw it away, and eat the rest.' So the Prophet ruled the impurity of what is adjacent to impurity. For a mouse and similar, the amount of water adjacent to it is what our scholars estimated: twenty or thirty buckets due to its small body. So he ruled the impurity of this amount of water, because what is beyond this amount does not touch the mouse, but rather what touches the mouse touches it, and the Shari'ah came to rule impurity on what is adjacent to impurity, not on what is adjacent to what is adjacent to impurity. Do you not see that the Prophet ruled the purity of what is adjacent to the ghee that touched the mouse, and ruled the impurity of what touched the mouse? This is because if what is adjacent to the adjacent of impurity were ruled impure, then what is adjacent to that would also be ruled impure, and so on ad infinitum, leading to the conclusion that a drop of urine or a mouse falling into a vast ocean would make all its water impure due to the connection of its parts, and this is invalid. For a chicken, cat, and similar, the adjacency is greater due to larger body, so the impurity is estimated accordingly. For a human and anything the size of a human body like a sheep and similar, the water is all adjacent in the usual case due to the size, so it necessitates making all the water impure. Similarly, if any of these fallers disintegrated or swelled, because then moisture comes out of them due to softness, contacting all parts of the water; before that, it only contacts the amount we mentioned due to hardness. For this reason, Muhammad said: If a mouse's tail falls into a well, all the water is removed because the cut area is not free of moisture, so it contacts the parts and spoils them. This is a good explanation if it were not contrary to the general works of our companions, which clearly state that rulings of wells have no room for opinion (ra'y), and what he mentioned is contrary to that. Thus the commentator of al-Munyah criticized him. And what appears to me is that what he mentioned in al-Bada'i does not contradict what they stated, because he said this is a subtle juristic meaning (fiqh khafi), not explicit analogy (qiyas jali), and opinion (ra'y) only includes explicit analogy. As for hidden analogy (qiyas khafi), it is called istihsan. He said in al-Tawdih: Analogy is explicit and hidden. The hidden is called istihsan, but it is more general than hidden analogy, for every hidden analogy is istihsan, but not every istihsan is hidden analogy, because istihsan is also applied to other than hidden analogy. But the prevalent usage in the books of our companions is that when istihsan is mentioned, it means hidden analogy. It is a proof that opposes the explicit analogy that first comes to mind, and it is a proof with us because its establishment is by proofs that are proof by consensus, for it is either by tradition (athar) like salam (forward sale) and ijara (hiring) and the continuation of fasting in forgetfulness, or by consensus (ijma') like istisna' (commission to manufacture), or by necessity (darurah) like the purification of tanks and wells. And so on until the end mentioned in usul al-fiqh and similarly in many books of usul. Thus it appears from this that the purification of wells by removal is established only by hidden analogy which is established by necessity.
Source pages p. 197

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof that discusses the amount of water to be removed from a well when various dead animals fall into it, and establishes that these rulings are based on transmitted reports (athar) and a subtle juristic reasoning (fiqh khafi) rather than explicit analogy. The author begins by stating that rulings of wells are founded on following traditions, not analogy, because analogy would lead to either complete purification impossibility or complete immunity from impurity. He then transmits from Muhammad and Abu Yusuf that well water is like running water, but the Hanafi school chooses to follow the traditions. He then cites specific traditions: from the Prophet regarding a mouse (20 or 30 buckets), from Abu Sa'id al-Khudri regarding a chicken (40 buckets), and from Ibn 'Abbas and Ibn al-Zubayr regarding a dead body in Zamzam (complete removal). The 'fiqh khafi' explanation then provides a rational basis: impurity spreads by direct contact (mujawarah), but not by indirect contact (adjacent of adjacent). The amount to be removed corresponds to the volume of water that directly touches the dead animal's body, which is estimated based on size. For a human or similarly sized animal, all water is adjacent and thus must be removed entirely. The passage also addresses objections from other Hanafi works that deny any role for reason in these rulings, but the author reconciles by explaining that this reasoning is hidden analogy (qiyas khafi) known as istihsan, which is a valid proof in the Hanafi school when based on tradition, consensus, or necessity. The purification of wells via removal is therefore established by this hidden analogy necessitated by practical need.

Key Terms
الفقه الخفي (al-fiqh al-khafi): A subtle juristic reasoning that explains rulings without recourse to explicit analogy, here used to justify the amounts of water removal from wells.
القياس الخفي (qiyas khafi): Hidden analogy or istihsan, a reasoning that opposes the apparent analogy and is based on a subtler 'illa.
الآبار (al-abar): Wells; the subject of specific rulings concerning purification when impurity falls into them.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, how many buckets of water must be removed if a mouse dies in a well?
A) 10 buckets
B) 20 or 30 buckets
C) 40 buckets
D) All water

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school prescribes specific amounts of water removal for different dead animals in wells, derived from prophetic hadith and companion consensus, not from pure analogy.

Taharah — Purification
intermediate
10 min
The Subtle Jurisprudence (Fiqh Khafi) and Hidden Analogy (Qiyas Khafi)
Explains the methodological basis for well rulings: fiqh khafi as istihsan

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain what fiqh khafi is, how it functions as a hidden analogy (qiyas khafi) or istihsan, and why the Hanafi school considers it a valid proof in justifying well purification rulings.

Core Idea
Fiqh khafi is a hidden analogy (qiyas khafi) that refines the apparent analogy; it is a valid proof in Hanafi usul when based on necessity or tradition.

The rulings for well purification, though based on traditions, also have a subtle rational basis called 'fiqh khafi'. This concept, developed by the Hanafi school, explains why specific amounts of water are required by appealing to the principle of direct adjacency. In this lesson, we will dissect this reasoning and see how it relates to the broader Hanafi principle of istihsan.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two apparent analogies that were abandoned
The passage mentions that analogy would either require the well never to become pure (Bishr) or never to become impure (Sahibayn). These are abandoned in favor of transmitted reports and a subtle reasoning.
This sets the stage: the ruling is not derived from explicit qiyas jali.
2
Analyze the hadith of the mouse in ghee
The Prophet (ﷺ) ruled that only the part of ghee directly touching the mouse is impure; the rest is pure. This establishes the principle that impurity spreads to what is adjacent (al-mujawar) but not to what is adjacent to that (jar al-jar).
This principle is the core of the hidden analogy.
3
Apply the principle to well water
For a small animal like a mouse, only the water directly in contact (about 20-30 buckets) is impure. For a larger animal, more water is directly adjacent, up to the entire well.
The amount correlates with the surface area of the corpse.
4
Recognize that this reasoning is qiyas khafi
The argument uses a subtle 'illa (adjacency) that is not immediately obvious, thus it is hidden analogy (qiyas khafi), also called istihsan.
This distinguishes it from the apparent analogies that were abandoned.
5
Place fiqh khafi in the broader Hanafi usul framework
The passage explains that istihsan is a valid proof in the Hanafi school, based on tradition, consensus, or necessity. The well rulings fall under the category of necessity (darurah).
This clarifies that the ruling is not arbitrary but rooted in established usul.

Source Text

واعلم أن مسائل الآبار مبنية على اتباع الآثار دون القياس فإن القياس فيها إما أن لا تطهر أصلاً كما قال بشر لعدم الإمكان لاختلاط النجاسة بالأوحال والجدران والماء ينبع شيئاً فشيئاً، وإما أن لا تتنجس اسقاطاً لحكم النجاسة حيث تعذر الاحتراز أو التطهير كما نقل عن محمد أنه قال : اجتمع رأيي ورأي أبي يوسف أن ماء البئر في حكم الجاري لأنه أسفله ويؤخذ من أعلاه فلا يتنجس كحوض الحمام قلنا وما علينا أن ننزح منها دلاء أخذاً بالآثار ومن الطريق أن يكون الإنسان في يد النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم وأصحابه رضي الله كالأعمى في يد القائد. كذا في فتح القدير وغيره من الشروح، وفي البدائع بعد ما ذكر القياسين قال : إلا أنا تركنا القياسين الظاهرين بالخبر والأثر وضرب من الفقه الخفي. أما الخبر فما روى أبو جعفر الأستروشني بإسناده عن النبي الله أنه قال في الفأرة تموت في البئر ينزح منها عشرون، وفي رواية ثلاثون. وعن أبي سعيد الخدري أنه قال في دجاجة ماتت في البئر ينزح منها أربعون دلواً. وعن ابن عباس وابن الزبير أنهما أمرا بنزح جميع ماء زمزم حين مات فيها زنجي وكان بمحضر من الصحابة ولم ينكر عليهما أحد فانعقد الإجماع عليه. وأما الفقه الخفي فهو أن في هذه الأشياء دماً مسفوحاً وقد تشرب في أجزائها عند الموت فنجسها وقد جاور هذه الأشياء الماء والماء يتنجس أو يفسد بمجاورة النجس لأن الأصل أن ما جاور النجس نجس بالشرع . قال صلى الله عليه وسلم في الفأرة تموت في السمن الجامد يقوّر ما حولها ويلقى وتؤكل البقية . فقد حكم النبي بنجاسة جار النجس، وفي الفأرة ونحوها ما يجاورها من الماء مقدار ما قدره أصحابنا وهو عشرون دلواً أو ثلاثون لصغر جثتها . فحكم بنجاسة هذا القدر من الماء لأن ما وراء هذا القدر لم يجاور الفأرة بل جاورها ما جاور الفأرة، والشرع ورد بتنجيس جار الخبث لا بتنجيس جار جار النجس، ألا ترى أن النبي محكم بطهارة جار السمن الذي جاور الفأرة، وحكم بنجاسة ما جاور الفأرة، وهذا لأن جار جار النجس لو حكم بنجاسته لحكم أيضاً بنجاسة ما جاور جار النجس ثم هكذا إلى ما لا نهاية له، فيؤدي إلى أن قطرة من بول أو فأرة لو وقعت في بحر عظيم أن يتنجس جميع مائه لاتصال بين أجزائه وذلك فاسد وفي الدجاجة والسنور وأشباه ذلك المجاورة أكثر لزيادة ضخامة في جثتها فقدر بنحاسة ذلك القدر والآدمي وما كان جثته مثل جثته كالشاة ونحوها مجاور الماء جميع في العادة لعظم جثته فيوجب تنجيس جميع الماء، وكذا إذا تفسخ شيء من هذه الواقعات أو انتفخ لأن عند ذلك تخرج البلة منها لرخاوة فيها فتجاور جميع أجزاء الماء وقبل ذلك لا يجاور إلا قدر ما ذكرنا لصلابة فيها ولهذا قال محمد: إذا وقع في البئر ذنب فأرة ينزح جميع الماء لأن موضع القطع لا ينفك عن بلة فيجاور أجزاء فيفسدها ا هـ. وهذا تقرير حسن لو لم يكن مخالفاً لعامة كتب أصحابنا فإنها مصرحة بأن مسائل الآبار ليس للرأي فيها مدخل وما ذكره خلافه . كذا تعقبه شارح المنية. والذي ظهر لي أن ما ذكره في البدائع لا يخالف ما صرحوا به لأنه ذكر أن هذا معنى خفي فقهي لا قياس جلي ولا يكون من قبيل الرأي إلا القياس الجلي. وأما القياس الخفي فهو المسمى بالاستحسان إن قال في التوضيح: القياس جلي وخفي. فالخفي يسمى بالاستحسان لكنه أعم من القياس الخفي فإن كل قياس خفي استحسان وليس كل استحسان قياساً خفياً لأن الاستحسان قد يطلق على غير القياس الخفي أيضاً لكن الغالب في كتب أصحابنا أنه إذا ذكر الاستحسان أريد به القياس الخفي، وهو دليل يقابل القياس الجلي الذي يسبق إليه الإفهام وهو حجة عندنا لأن ثبوته بالدلائل التي هي حجة إجماعاً لأنه إما بالأثر كالسلم والإجارة وبقاء الصوم في النسيان، وإما بالإجماع كالاستصناع وإما بالضرورة كطهارة الحياض والآبار. وأما بالقياس الخفي إلى آخر ما ذكر في أصول الفقه وكذا في كثير من كتب الأصول، فظهر Know that rulings of wells are based on following traditions (athar) and not analogy (qiyas), for analogy would either require that they never become pure, as Bishr said due to impossibility because impurity mixes with mud and walls and water seeps gradually, or that they never become impure by dropping the ruling of impurity due to difficulty of avoidance or purification, as transmitted from Muhammad who said: My opinion and Abu Yusuf's opinion agreed that well water is like running water because its bottom and water is taken from its top, so it does not become impure like a bath basin. As for the report: Abu Ja'far al-Astarushni narrated with his chain from the Prophet (peace be upon him) that he said regarding a mouse that dies in a well: twenty buckets are removed, and in another narration thirty. Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof that discusses the amount of water to be removed from a well when various dead animals fall into it, and establishes that these rulings are based on transmitted reports (athar) and a subtle juristic reasoning (fiqh khafi) rather than explicit analogy. The author begins by stating that rulings of wells are founded on following traditions, not analogy, because analogy would lead to either complete purification impossibility or complete immunity from impurity. He then transmits from Muhammad and Abu Yusuf that well water is like running water, but the Hanafi school chooses to follow the traditions. He then cites specific traditions: from the Prophet regarding a mouse (20 or 30 buckets), from Abu Sa'id al-Khudri regarding a chicken (40 buckets), and from Ibn 'Abbas and Ibn al-Zubayr regarding a dead body in Zamzam (complete removal). The 'fiqh khafi' explanation then provides a rational basis: impurity spreads by direct contact (mujawarah), but not by indirect contact (adjacent of adjacent). The amount to be removed corresponds to the volume of water that directly touches the dead animal's body, which is estimated based on size. For a human or similarly sized animal, all water is adjacent and thus must be removed entirely. The passage also addresses objections from other Hanafi works that deny any role for reason in these rulings, but the author reconciles by explaining that this reasoning is hidden analogy (qiyas khafi) known as istihsan, which is a valid proof in the Hanafi school when based on tradition, consensus, or necessity. The purification of wells via removal is therefore established by this hidden analogy necessitated by practical need.

Key Terms
الفقه الخفي (al-fiqh al-khafi): A subtle juristic reasoning that explains rulings without recourse to explicit analogy, here used to justify the amounts of water removal from wells.
القياس الخفي (qiyas khafi): Hidden analogy or istihsan, a reasoning that opposes the apparent analogy and is based on a subtler 'illa.

Taharah — Purification
intermediate
8 min
Droppings of Camels and Sheep in Wells: Ruling and Istihsan Methods
States that droppings of camels and sheep do not require water removal, with two methods of istihsan

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on camel and sheep droppings in a well, explain why istihsan overrides qiyas, and identify the two methods of istihsan referenced in al-Hidayah.

Core Idea
Dry droppings of camels and sheep in a well do not necessitate water removal due to istihsan, overriding the analogy to ordinary impurities.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents a specific ruling on taharah: when a dry dropping of a camel or sheep falls into a well, the Hanafi school holds that no water removal is required. The author explains that this ruling is based on istihsan (juristic preference), not on qiyas (analogy). We will examine the reasoning behind istihsan, the two methods mentioned, and the marginal critique that clarifies the proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling and Its Basis
The passage opens by stating that water of a well is not removed due to a single dropping of a camel or sheep. It immediately notes that this is istihsan, not qiyas.
The key phrase: 'لا ينزح ماء البئر' (water of the well is not removed).
2
Recognize the Analogical Argument (Qiyas)
The text explicitly states that qiyas would require the water to become impure because impurity fell into a small amount of water, analogous to a vessel. This is the default principle.
Qiyas here is jali (evident) because the illa is the presence of impurity in a small quantity of water.
3
Understand Why Istihsan Overrides
Istihsan is a proof that departs from qiyas due to a stronger evidence – here, desert wells have a custom and necessity that differentiate them from vessels. The first method (from al-Hidayah) focuses on the nature of desert wells.
The text references 'آبار الفلوات' (desert wells). Unfortunately the full description is cut off.
4
Analyze the Marginal Critique
The marginal note questions a previous argument about ghee (samn). It objects that the hadith about ghee applies to solid ghee, not liquid; water is liquid, so the analogy is invalid. The commentator responds that the pronoun in 'it is a proof with us' refers to istihsan, confirming that istihsan is the relied-upon source.
This critique shows the careful distinction between solid and liquid states in istihsan reasoning.
5
Draw the Practical Consequence
The mufta bihi (operative ruling) is that no water removal is required. This is the position relied upon for fatwa, based on the first method of istihsan preferred by the author of al-Hidayah.
The second method is not fully stated in this excerpt.

His statement: (Not due to droppings of camels and sheep) meaning water of the well is not removed due to the fall of a single dropping of camels and sheep into it, and this is istihsan. The analogy would be that the water becomes absolutely impure because impurity falls into a small amount of water, like a vessel. Two methods of istihsan are mentioned: the first, preferred by the author of al-Hidayah, confining himself to it, that the wells of the deserts are not... (marginal note: Do you not see that the Prophet ruled the purity of what is adjacent to ghee? I say: it is objected to him that if the ghee were liquid, he (peace be upon him) said: if it is liquid, do not approach it, and water is of this kind, not of the solid kind; ponder.) His statement: (And it is a proof with us) said in al-Tawdih: the pronoun refers to istihsan. End. And upon this, the established...

Commentary
This passage is a ruling about droppings of camels and sheep (ba'rat ibil wa ghanam) falling into a well: no water removal is required. The author first states that this ruling is based on istihsan (juristic preference), while the apparent analogy would require all water to be contaminated because impurity falls into a small quantity of water. Then two methods of istihsan are promised, but only the first is partially mentioned, from al-Hidayah, focusing on desert wells (a subtle point that desert wells are not typically used for ritual purity? The text is cut off). The marginal notes provide a critique: a learner objects that the analogy to solid ghee (in the earlier hadith) does not apply because water is liquid, and the Prophet said not to approach liquid ghee. The commentator responds that the pronoun in 'it is a proof with us' refers to istihsan. Due to the cut-off passage, the complete reasoning is incomplete, but the ruling itself is clear: droppings do not necessitate removal, by istihsan.

Key Terms
بعرة (ba'rah): A single dropping (pellet) of a camel or sheep, considered a dry impurity.
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference; a proof that overrides analogy due to a stronger evidence, such as necessity, consensus, or custom.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, camel and sheep droppings in a well do not trigger water removal because istihsan overrides the default analogy, highlighting the school's use of juristic preference to ease rulings.

Taharah — Purification

The Principle of Adjacent Impurity (Jar al-Najis) and Its Proof
Explaining the principle and its proof from hadith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi principle of adjacent impurity, cite the hadith proof, and apply it to determine which part of water becomes impure when a mouse falls in.

Core Idea
Only the immediate neighbour of impurity becomes impure; the neighbour's neighbour remains pure.

This lesson introduces a key principle in Hanafi taharah: jar al-najis. The Prophet's ruling on clarified butter with a mouse teaches us that impurity does not spread infinitely. We will see how this principle applies to water and other liquids, and why a drop of urine does not contaminate an entire ocean.

Guided Path
1
State the principle
State the rule that only the immediate neighbour of impurity (jar al-khubth) becomes impure, and the neighbour's neighbour (jar jar al-najis) remains pure.
This is the core legal rule.
2
Identify the dalil
Recall the hadith of the Prophet ﷺ regarding clarified butter and a mouse, where the part directly touching the mouse is impure but the adjacent part is pure.
The hadith is an evidence for the limit of impurity spread.
3
Understand the reasoning
Explain that if impurity spread to the neighbour's neighbour, it would lead to infinite regression, which is invalid (fasid).
Reductio ad absurdum argument.
4
Apply to a large body of water
Apply the principle: a drop of urine falling into a great ocean does not contaminate all water because only the immediate part is affected.
Contrary to what some might assume.
5
Apply to different creatures
Distinguish between large creatures (e.g., human, sheep) that entirely adjoin water, causing all water to become impure, and smaller ones (e.g., chicken, cat) where only a portion is impure. Also account for decomposition or swelling.
The size and state affect the extent of adjacency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
جثتها . فحكم بنجاسة هذا القدر من الماء لأن ما وراء هذا القدر لم يجاور الفأرة بل جاورها ما جاور الفأرة، والشرع ورد بتنجيس جار الخبث لا بتنجيس جار جار النجس، ألا ترى أن النبي حكم بطهارة جار السمن الذي جاور الفأرة، وحكم بنجاسة ما جاور الفأرة، وهذا لأن جار جار النجس لو حكم بنجاسته لحكم أيضاً بنجاسة ما جاور جار النجس ثم هكذا إلى ما لا نهاية له، فيؤدي إلى أن قطرة من بول أو فأرة لو وقعت في بحر عظيم أن يتنجس جميع مائه لاتصال بين أجزائه وذلك فاسد وفي الدجاجة والسنور وأشباه ذلك المجاورة أكثر لزيادة ضخامة في جثتها فقدر بنحاسة ذلك القدر والآدمي وما كان جثته مثل جثته كالشاة ونحوها مجاور الماء جميع في العادة لعظم جثته فيوجب تنجيس جميع الماء، وكذا إذا تفسخ شيء من هذه الواقعات أو انتفخ لأن عند ذلك تخرج البلة منها لرخاوة فيها فتجاور جميع أجزاء الماء وقبل ذلك لا يجاور إلا قدر ما ذكرنا لصلابة فيها ولهذا قال محمد: إذا وقع في البئر ذنب فأرة ينزح جميع الماء لأن موضع القطع لا ينفك عن بلة فيجاور أجزاء فيفسدها ا هـ. وهذا تقرير حسن لو لم يكن مخالفاً لعامة كتب أصحابنا فإنها مصرحة بأن مسائل الآبار ليس للرأي فيها مدخل وما ذكره خلافه . كذا تعقبه شارح المنية. والذي ظهر لي أن ما ذكره في البدائع لا يخالف ما صرحوا به لأنه ذكر أن هذا معنى خفي فقهي لا قياس جلي ولا يكون من قبيل الرأي إلا القياس الجلي. وأما القياس الخفي فهو المسمى بالاستحسان إن قال في التوضيح: القياس جلي وخفي. فالخفي يسمى بالاستحسان لكنه أعم من القياس الخفي فإن كل قياس خفي استحسان وليس كل استحسان قياساً خفياً لأن الاستحسان قد يطلق على غير القياس الخفي أيضاً لكن الغالب في كتب أصحابنا أنه إذا ذكر الاستحسان أريد به القياس الخفي، وهو دليل يقابل القياس الجلي الذي يسبق إليه الإفهام وهو حجة عندنا لأن ثبوته بالدلائل التي هي حجة إجماعاً لأنه إما بالأثر كالسلم والإجارة وبقاء الصوم في النسيان، وإما بالإجماع كالاستصناع وإما بالضرورة كطهارة الحياض والآبار. وأما بالقياس الخفي إلى آخر ما ذكر في أصول الفقه وكذا في كثير من كتب الأصول، فظهر بهذا أن طهارة الآبار بالنزح إنما ثبتت بالقياس الخفي الذي ثبت بالضرورة.
Its body. So he ruled the impurity of this amount of water because what is beyond this amount does not adjoin the mouse, but rather what adjoins the mouse adjoins it. The Law has come with making the neighbour of impurity impure, not making the neighbour of the neighbour of impurity impure. Do you not see that the Prophet ruled the purity of the neighbour of the clarified butter that adjoined the mouse, and ruled the impurity of what adjoined the mouse. This is because if the neighbour of the neighbour of impurity were ruled impure, then what adjoins the neighbour of impurity would also be ruled impure, and so on ad infinitum, leading to a drop of urine or a mouse falling into a great ocean causing all its water to become impure due to the connection between its parts, and that is invalid. As for a chicken, cat, and the like, the adjacency is greater due to the increase in the size of their bodies, so impurity is estimated for that amount. As for a human and what has a body like his, such as a sheep and the like, all of it adjoins the water usually due to the size of its body, so it necessitates the impurity of all the water. Likewise, if any of these fallen objects disintegrates or swells, because then moisture comes out of them due to softness, so it adjoins all parts of the water. Before that, it only adjoins the amount we mentioned due to solidity. For this reason, Muhammad said: If a mouse's tail falls into a well, all the water is to be drawn because the place of cut is not free from moisture, so it adjoins parts and spoils them, end quote. This is a good explanation if it were not contradictory to the general books of our companions, for they explicitly state that matters of wells have no room for opinion, and what he mentioned contradicts that. Such is the critique by the commentator of Al-Munyah. What appears to me is that what he mentioned in Al-Bada'i does not contradict what they stated, because he mentioned that this is a subtle juristic meaning, not an obvious analogy, and nothing from the category of opinion except the obvious analogy. As for the hidden analogy, it is called istihsan. It is said in Al-Tawdih: Analogy is of two types: obvious and hidden. The hidden is called istihsan, but it is more general than the hidden analogy, for every hidden analogy is istihsan, but not every istihsan is a hidden analogy because istihsan may also be applied to other than hidden analogy. However, the common practice in the books of our companions is that when they mention istihsan, they intend the hidden analogy. It is a proof that contrasts with the obvious analogy that comes quickly to the mind, and it is a proof according to us because its establishment is by proofs that are decisive by consensus. It is either by tradition (athar) like salam and ijarah and the continuity of fasting during forgetfulness, or by consensus like istisna', or by necessity like the purity of basins and wells. And by the hidden analogy, etc., as mentioned in Usul al-Fiqh and likewise in many books of Usul. So it became clear that the purity of wells by drawing water is established by the hidden analogy that is established by necessity.
Source pages p. 198

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the fiqh principle of adjacent impurity (jar al-najis) and its application to water when a mouse falls in. The author begins by stating the ruling that only the water directly surrounding a fallen mouse becomes impure, not the entire body of water. He derives this from the principle that Islamic law makes impure the neighbour of impurity, but not the neighbour of the neighbour of impurity. To support this, he cites the hadith where the Prophet ﷺ ruled that the clarified butter adjacent to a dead mouse is pure (because it is solid and can be removed), but the part that directly touches the mouse is impure. The author then applies this principle to various scenarios: if a drop of urine falls into a large sea, the whole sea does not become impure because the impurity spreads only to the immediate vicinity. For larger creatures like chickens, cats, or humans, their entire bodies typically touch the water, so the whole water becomes impure. If the creature is decomposed or swollen, moisture spreads throughout, thus contaminating all water. The author also reports Muhammad al-Shaybani's view that if a mouse's tail falls into a well, all water must be drawn because the cut end is moist and contacts water. This is criticized by the commentator of Al-Munyah as contrary to the principle that well issues are not subject to opinion. The author defends it as a subtle fiqh meaning falling under hidden analogy (qiyas khafi), which is acceptable. He then distinguishes between obvious analogy (qiyas jali), which is opinion, and hidden analogy, which is istihsan. He explains that istihsan is a valid proof based on tradition, consensus, or necessity. Thus, the purity of wells by drawing water is established by hidden analogy based on necessity.

Key Terms
جار الخبث (jar al-khubth): The matter that directly touches a source of impurity (the neighbour of impurity).
جار جار النجس (jar jar al-najis): The matter that touches the matter that touches the impurity (the neighbour of the neighbour of impurity).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a mouse falls into a bucket of water, how much of the water becomes impure?
A) All of the water becomes impure.
B) Only the water directly surrounding the mouse becomes impure.
C) None of the water becomes impure because the mouse is dry.
D) Only the water that the mouse touches plus a span around it.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school limits impurity to the immediate contact area, preventing impractical infinite contamination.

The Intra-Hanafi Dispute and Hidden Analogy (Qiyas Khafi)
Explaining the intra-Hanafi dispute and the concept of hidden analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding a mouse's tail in a well and describe how hidden analogy (qiyas khafi) serves as a valid proof in Hanafi usul.

Core Idea
Muhammad al-Shaybani's view on the mouse's tail is based on hidden analogy (qiyas khafi), a form of istihsan, but the majority Hanafi position relies on the principle of jar al-najis.

This lesson explores a specific disagreement within the Hanafi school: what happens if a mouse's tail falls into a well? While the general principle says only the immediate neighbour is impure, Muhammad al-Shaybani held that all the water becomes impure. We will examine his reasoning and the response, which leads into a discussion of hidden analogy (qiyas khafi) and its place in Hanafi legal theory.

Guided Path
1
State the disagreement
State Muhammad al-Shaybani's view that if a mouse's tail falls into a well, all water must be drawn because the cut end is moist and adjoins all parts.
This contradicts the general principle of jar al-najis.
2
Understand his proof
Explain that the tail's cut end is moist, so it contacts all water particles, thus necessity dictates that all water is contaminated.
This is a hidden analogy (qiyas khafi).
3
Understand the critique
The majority of Hanafi scholars criticize this as opinion (ra'y) because well matters are not subject to opinion. The commentator of Al-Munyah explicitly rejects it.
The author of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq defends it as hidden analogy, not opinion.
4
Identify the methodological issue
Distinguish between obvious analogy (qiyas jali) which is opinion, and hidden analogy (qiyas khafi) which is called istihsan and is a valid proof.
Hidden analogy is a proof based on tradition, consensus, or necessity.
5
Place hidden analogy in Hanafi usul
Explain that hidden analogy is a valid proof (hujja) in the Hanafi school, established by proofs that are decisive by consensus, and it is the basis for ruling on well purification.
The purity of wells by drawing water is established by hidden analogy based on necessity.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the fiqh principle of adjacent impurity (jar al-najis) and its application to water when a mouse falls in. Key Terms
قیاس خفی (qiyas khafi): A hidden analogy that is considered a form of istihsan; it is a proof based on subtle rational inference.
استحسان (istihsan): A method of preferring a different ruling over the obvious analogy due to a stronger evidence (such as necessity, custom, or public good).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the author of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq defend Muhammad al-Shaybani's view on the mouse's tail even though it contradicts the majority?
A) Because Muhammad's view is based on a direct hadith.
B) Because it is based on hidden analogy (qiyas khafi), which is a valid proof called istihsan.
C) Because the majority view is actually weaker.
D) Because the commentator of Al-Munyah changed his mind.

Key Takeaway
Hidden analogy (qiyas khafi) is a valid Hanafi proof, but in the case of the mouse's tail, the majority principle of jar al-najis prevails.
